Jump to:

These notes assume no prior knowledge and are built backwards from what the written exam actually asks. Each chapter follows the same rhythm: first the intuition (why the idea exists), then the definitions (every symbol explained before use), then the central results, then the derivations that examiners ask you to reproduce, then a box listing the real exam question patterns, a fully worked past-exam example, and finally the common mistakes that cost marks.

The single most important fact about this exam: it rewards reproducible derivations, not memorised trivia. Roughly 80\

1Foundations: Derivatives, Payoffs and No-Arbitrage (background for every exam)

The Core Idea

A derivative is a contract whose payoff is derived from the price of something else — a stock, an exchange rate, an interest rate, a barrel of oil. Think of it as a side bet on an asset you may never own: an option on a stock is like a refundable deposit on a house — you pay a small amount now for the right to buy later at a locked-in price, and if the market moves against you, you simply walk away and lose only the deposit.

Why can we price such bets at all? Because of one deceptively simple principle: no arbitrage — there is no free lunch. If two portfolios are guaranteed to pay the same amount in the future, they must cost the same today; otherwise you could buy the cheap one, sell the expensive one, pocket the difference, and carry zero risk. Every single formula in this course — forward prices, binomial trees, Black–Scholes, bond-pricing PDEs, CDS spreads — is nothing more than this principle dressed in progressively fancier clothes. If you ever get lost in the algebra, come back to this: find a portfolio that replicates the payoff; the price of the derivative is the cost of the replica.

The second recurring idea is discounting: money tomorrow is worth less than money today, because money today can be invested at the risk-free rate \(r\). A certain payment of 1 at time \(T\) is worth \(e^{-rT}\) today. A bond price is a discounted promise; a derivative price is a discounted expected payoff (under the right probabilities — that subtlety is the heart of the course).

Key Definitions and Notation

SymbolMeaning
\(S_t\) (or \(S\))price of the underlying asset at time \(t\)
\(K\)strike (exercise) price, or delivery price of a forward
\(T\)maturity/expiration date; \( = T-t\) is time to maturity
\(r\)continuously compounded risk-free interest rate
\(c_t,\ p_t\)prices of a European call and put at time \(t\)
\(C_t,\ P_t\)prices of American call and put (when the distinction matters)
\(F_0,\ F(t,T)\)forward price agreed at time \(0\) (resp.\ \(t\)) for delivery at \(T\)
\(f_t\)value of an already existing forward contract at time \(t\)
\(P(t,T)\)price at \(t\) of a zero-coupon bond paying 1 at \(T\)
\((x)^+\)\((x,0)\)
  • Long = you own the asset/claim (you gain if the price rises). Short = you owe it (you gain if the price falls).
  • A forward contract is an agreement, made today, to buy (long) or sell (short) the underlying at a fixed future date \(T\) for a fixed delivery price \(K\). It costs nothing to enter: \(K\) is chosen as the forward price \(F_0\) so that the initial value is zero. Payoff to the long at \(T\): \(S_T - K\).
  • A futures contract is an exchange-traded forward: standardised, marked to market daily (gains/losses settled every day through margin accounts), with a clearing house eliminating counterparty risk.
  • A European call (put) gives the right, not the obligation, to buy (sell) the underlying at strike \(K\) at time \(T\). Payoffs: \(c_T = (S_T-K)^+\), \(p_T = (K-S_T)^+\). An American option can be exercised at any time up to \(T\).
  • A swap exchanges two streams of cash flows, e.g.\ fixed-rate interest against floating-rate interest (interest rate swap) or premium payments against default protection (credit default swap).
  • Moneyness: a call is in the money if \(S>K\), at the money if \(S\approx K\), out of the money if \(SArbitrage: a strategy with zero initial cost, no possibility of loss, and a positive probability of gain. The no-arbitrage principle says such strategies cannot persist in equilibrium.
  • Law of one price: two portfolios with identical payoffs in every future state must have identical prices today.

Central Results

Present value / discounting

With a constant continuously compounded rate \(r\), the value today of receiving 1 for sure at time \(T\) is \[ P(0,T) = e^{-rT}. \] Why this shape: \(e^{rT}\) is what 1 unit grows to under continuous compounding (\(\lim_{m\to\infty}(1+r/m)^{mT}\)); discounting is just growth run backwards.

Put--call parity (Europeans, no dividends)

\[ c + Ke^{-rT} = p + S_0 . \] Reading: "call plus cash" \(=\) "put plus stock". Both sides are worth \(\max(S_T,K)\) at maturity, so they must cost the same today. With a continuous dividend yield \(q\), replace \(S_0\) by \(S_0e^{-qT}\).

Option price bounds and early exercise
  • \(c \le S_0\) and \(p \le Ke^{-rT}\); \(c \ge (S_0 - Ke^{-rT})^+\) and \(p \ge (Ke^{-rT}-S_0)^+\).
  • An American call on a non-dividend stock is never exercised early: \(C = c\). Intuition: exercising early pays \(S-K\); the live option is worth at least \(S - Ke^{-r\tau} > S-K\), i.e.\ selling beats exercising — exercising throws away the time value of the strike and the insurance against the stock falling.
  • An American put may be exercised early (deep in the money, getting \(K\) now beats \(K\) later): \(P \ge p\).

Key Derivations

Proof of put–call parity (a two-portfolio no-arbitrage argument — the template for every proof in this course):
  1. Portfolio A: one European call plus cash \(Ke^{-rT}\) invested at rate \(r\).
  2. Portfolio B: one European put plus one share.
  3. At \(T\): if \(S_T > K\), A pays \((S_T - K) + K = S_T\) and B pays \(0 + S_T = S_T\). If \(S_T \le K\), A pays \(0 + K = K\) and B pays \((K-S_T) + S_T = K\). So both pay \(\max(S_T,K)\) in every state.
  4. By the law of one price, their costs today are equal: \(c + Ke^{-rT} = p + S_0\).
If parity failed, e.g.\ \(c + Ke^{-rT} > p + S_0\), you would sell A, buy B, invest the difference, and enjoy a riskless profit — the arbitrage that enforces the formula.

Exam Questions — What They Actually Ask

Exam Pattern
  • "Define a forward contract / a European call option / a swap." (definitions worth easy marks — give the payoff formula too)
  • "Draw the payoff diagram of a long call / short put / long forward."
  • "State and prove put–call parity." (the two-portfolio table above is the answer)
  • "Explain why an American call on a non-dividend-paying stock should not be exercised early."
  • "What is an arbitrage opportunity?" — always give the three-part definition (zero cost, no downside, possible upside).

Worked Example

June 2024, Question 1 --- State prices, put-call parity, and the forward

Question. An economy has two dates (\(t=0\) and \(t=T\)) and two states. A non-dividend-paying stock costs \(S_0\) and pays \(uS_0\) (up) or \(dS_0\) (down), \(u>e^{rT}>d\ge0\). A zero-coupon bond costs \(e^{-rT}\) and pays 1 in both states.

(a) Explain and compute the Arrow–Debreu state prices \((\psi_u,\psi_d)\) and risk-neutral probabilities \((q_u,q_d)\).

(b) Find the arbitrage-free prices \(c_0\) and \(p_0\) of a European call and put with strike \(K\) and expiry \(T\).

(c) Compare a long-call/short-put portfolio with a forward contract on the stock; show that the difference in time-0 value equals \(S_0 - Ke^{-rT}\).

Solution.

(a) State prices \(\psi\) satisfy \(e^{-rT} = a\psi_u + a\psi_d\) and \(S_0 = uS_0\psi_u + dS_0\psi_d\) (with \(a=e^{rT}\)), giving \[ \psi_u = \frac{a-d}{a(u-d)}, \qquad \psi_d = \frac{u-a}{a(u-d)}. \] Risk-neutral probabilities: \(q_u = a\psi_u = \tfrac{a-d}{u-d}\), \(q_d = 1-q_u\). Both are positive by assumption.

(b) Call payoffs: \(c_T = \max(uS_0-K,0)\) or \(\max(dS_0-K,0)\). Price: \(c_0 = \psi_u\max(uS_0-K,0)+\psi_d\max(dS_0-K,0)\). By put-call parity the put is \(p_0 = c_0 - S_0 + Ke^{-rT}\) (equivalently \(p_0 = K/a - S_0 + c_0\)).

(c) Long call, short put at \(t=0\) costs \(c_0-p_0 = S_0 - K/a = S_0 - Ke^{-rT}\). A forward with delivery price \(F_0=aS_0\) has value zero at time 0; the option portfolio equals the present value of \(F_0-K\).

Key marks: (a) set up the two linear equations for \(\psi\) and solve; (b) state prices times payoffs; (c) the portfolio replicates a forward — same payoff at \(T\) up to the constant \(F_0-K\).

Common Mistakes

Common Mistakes
  • Forgetting to discount the strike: parity uses \(Ke^{-rT}\), never plain \(K\).
  • Confusing the payoff of an option (\((S_T-K)^+\), at maturity) with its profit (payoff minus the future value of the premium) — exam payoff diagrams usually ask for the payoff unless "profit" is explicit.
  • Claiming American puts are never exercised early — that result holds only for calls on non-dividend stocks.
2Forward and Futures Pricing (15/21 exams)

The Core Idea

Suppose gold costs \(S_0\) today and you promise to deliver one ounce in a year. What should you charge? You do not need to forecast the gold price. You can buy the ounce today for \(S_0\), financed by borrowing at rate \(r\), sit on it for a year, and deliver it. Your all-in cost at delivery is \(S_0e^{rT}\) — the spot price plus the "cost of carry" (interest on the money tied up). Competition among people who can all run this strategy forces the forward price to exactly \[ F_0 = S_0e^{rT}. \] This is the purest example of pricing by replication: the forward price is not a forecast, it is a storage-and-financing cost. Anything that makes holding the asset cheaper (dividends, foreign interest earned on a currency, a "convenience yield" from having physical oil in your tank) lowers the forward price; anything that makes holding costlier (storage fees) raises it.

The second question exams love: once a forward contract exists, what is it worth? At inception its value is zero by construction. Later, the market forward price \(F_t\) has drifted away from your locked-in delivery price \(K\), and your contract is worth the discounted difference \(f_t = (F_t - K)e^{-r(T-t)}\): you are guaranteed to be "ahead" by \(F_t - K\) at delivery, and a sure amount at \(T\) is worth its discounted value now.

Key Definitions and Notation

  • \(F_0 = F(0,T)\): the delivery price that makes a new forward contract worth zero today ("the forward price").
  • \(K\): the delivery price written into an existing contract (fixed once and for all at inception).
  • \(f_t\): the market value at time \(t\) of an existing (long) forward with delivery price \(K\).
  • \(I\): present value of known discrete income (e.g.\ dividends in cash) paid by the asset during the contract's life.
  • \(q\): continuous dividend/income yield of the asset; for a currency, \(q = r_f\), the foreign risk-free rate.
  • \(u\): storage cost expressed as a continuous yield; \(y\): convenience yield (benefit of physical possession).
  • \(r_d, r_f\): domestic and foreign risk-free rates; \(S_0\) for FX is the spot exchange rate in units of domestic currency per unit of foreign currency.

Central Results

Forward prices --- the cost-of-carry family

\[ \begin{array}{ll} \text{No income:} & F_0 = S_0e^{rT} \\[2pt] \text{Known cash income (PV \(=I\)):} & F_0 = (S_0 - I)\,e^{rT} \\[2pt] \text{Continuous yield } q: & F_0 = S_0\,e^{(r-q)T} \\[2pt] \text{Currency (interest-rate parity):} & F_0 = S_0\,e^{(r_d-r_f)T} \\[2pt] \text{Commodity (storage \(u\), convenience \(y\)):} & F_0 = S_0\,e^{(r+u-y)T} \end{array} \] All are one formula: forward \(=\) spot grown at the net cost of carry. Income received while holding the asset (\(q\), \(r_f\), \(y\)) enters with a minus sign; costs of holding (\(r\), \(u\)) with a plus sign.

Value of an existing forward

For a long forward with delivery price \(K\) and maturity \(T\), at any time \(t\le T\): \[ f_t = (F_t - K)\,e^{-r(T-t)}, \] where \(F_t = F(t,T)\) is the current forward price. Equivalently \(f_t = S_t e^{-q(T-t)} - Ke^{-r(T-t)}\) for an asset with yield \(q\). A short forward is worth \(-f_t\). At inception \(K=F_0\) so \(f_0 = 0\).

Forwards vs.\ futures

With deterministic interest rates, forward and futures prices coincide. With stochastic rates they differ slightly: daily marking-to-market means a long futures receives cash exactly when rates are high (if \(S\) and \(r\) are positively correlated), which is valuable, so then futures price \(>\) forward price. In this course you may normally treat them as equal unless the question asks for this discussion.

Key Derivations

Derivation of \(F_0 = S_0e^{rT}\) (cash-and-carry). Marks come from exhibiting both arbitrages:
  1. Suppose \(F_0 > S_0e^{rT}\). Today: borrow \(S_0\), buy the asset, short the forward (cost: zero). At \(T\): deliver the asset, receive \(F_0\), repay the loan \(S_0e^{rT}\). Riskless profit \(F_0 - S_0e^{rT} > 0\): arbitrage.
  2. Suppose \(F_0 < S_0e^{rT}\). Today: short-sell the asset for \(S_0\), invest at \(r\), go long the forward. At \(T\): pay \(F_0\), take delivery, return the borrowed asset; the deposit pays \(S_0e^{rT}\). Riskless profit \(S_0e^{rT}-F_0 > 0\).
  3. Neither can persist, so \(F_0 = S_0e^{rT}\). With income yield \(q\), the carry strategy holds only \(e^{-qT}\) units initially (reinvesting income into the asset), giving \(F_0 = S_0e^{(r-q)T}\).

Derivation of \(f_t = (F_t-K)e^{-r(T-t)}\). Compare two long forwards at time \(t\): one with delivery price \(K\) (value \(f_t\), unknown) and a freshly written one with delivery price \(F_t\) (value \(0\) by definition). Long the first, short the second: at \(T\) this portfolio pays \((S_T-K) - (S_T-F_t) = F_t - K\) — a certain amount. Its value today must be the discounted certain payoff: \(f_t - 0 = (F_t-K)e^{-r(T-t)}\).

Exam Questions — What They Actually Ask

Exam Pattern
  • "Define a forward contract and explain how the forward price is determined." (definition + cash-and-carry both directions)
  • "Find the no-arbitrage forward price of a stock paying dividends / a currency / a commodity with storage costs."
  • "Six months into the contract the spot has moved to …\ Find the value of the forward position." (\(f_t\) formula)
  • "Explain the difference between forward and futures contracts." (OTC vs.\ exchange, daily settlement, margins, counterparty risk)
  • "What is the convenience yield and why can the futures curve be in backwardation?" (\(y>r+u\) makes \(F_0

Worked Example

June 2021, Question 1 --- Forward price and Itô dynamics

Question. A stock costs \(S_t\) at time \(t\) and the zero-coupon bond price is \(P(t,T)\).

(a) No dividends: find the forward price \(F(t,T)\) by an arbitrage argument.

(b) Continuous dividend rate \(\delta\): what is \(F(t,T)\)?

(c) Denote call and put prices \(c(t,K,T)\) and \(p(t,K,T)\) with the dividend-paying stock. Derive put-call parity.

(d) Show how to back out the implied dividend rate from observable option and stock prices.

Solution.

(a) Two strategies to own one stock at \(T\): (A) forward contract, pay \(F(t,T)\) at \(T\); (B) buy the stock now for \(S_t\). No-arbitrage: \(S_t = P(t,T)\,F(t,T)\), so \(F(t,T) = S_t/P(t,T)\).

(b) With dividend \(\delta\), buy only \(e^{-\delta(T-t)}\) shares at \(t\), reinvest dividends to get 1 share at \(T\). Cost: \(e^{-\delta(T-t)}S_t\). Hence \(F(t,T) = e^{-\delta(T-t)}S_t/P(t,T)\).

(c) Long call, short put replicates a forward with delivery \(K\); its value is \(P(t,T)(F(t,T)-K)\). Therefore: \[ c - p = e^{-\delta(T-t)}S_t - P(t,T)K. \]

(d) Rearranging: \(\delta = -\dfrac{1}{T-t}\ln\!\dfrac{c-p+P(t,T)K}{S_t}\).

Key marks: (a) the two-strategy arbitrage argument; (b) the \(e^{-\delta(T-t)}\) discount on shares; (c) setting long call minus short put equal to a forward; (d) solving for \(\delta\).

Common Mistakes

Common Mistakes
  • Using the original forward price where the current one belongs: the value formula is \(f_t = (F_t - K)e^{-r(T-t)}\) with \(F_t\) recomputed from today's spot.
  • Sign errors on carry: dividends, foreign interest and convenience yield reduce \(F_0\); storage costs increase it.
  • Writing \(f_t = F_t - K\) without discounting — the difference is only realised at \(T\), so it must be discounted back.
3The Binomial Model and State Prices (8/21 exams)

The Core Idea

The binomial model is the whole theory of derivative pricing in miniature. Pretend that over the next period the stock can do only two things: go up by factor \(u\) or down by factor \(d\). That sounds absurdly crude, but with two possible outcomes and two traded instruments (the stock and a risk-free bond), you can build a portfolio that matches any payoff exactly — two equations, two unknowns. The option price is then forced by no-arbitrage to equal the cost of that replicating portfolio. Chain many short periods together and the crude model converges to Black–Scholes.

The astonishing punchline — and the idea the whole course revolves around — is that the real-world probability of an up-move never appears in the price. Two people who violently disagree about whether the stock will rise must still agree on the option price, because replication works regardless. Instead, the algebra spits out artificial "risk-neutral" probabilities \(q_u, q_d\), under which the stock earns exactly the risk-free rate, and every derivative price is the discounted expected payoff under those probabilities. This is your first meeting with the risk-neutral measure \(Q\) that dominates the rest of the course.

A useful reformulation: let \(\phi_u\) be the price today of a security paying 1 if the up-state occurs and 0 otherwise (an Arrow–Debreu security), and \(\phi_d\) likewise for the down-state. These state prices are like a menu of prices for pure "state lottery tickets"; any payoff \((V_u, V_d)\) then costs \(V_u\phi_u + V_d\phi_d\) — pricing becomes shopping.

Key Definitions and Notation

  • \(S_0\): stock price now; after one step of length \(\Delta t\) it is \(S_0u\) (up) or \(S_0d\) (down), with \(d < a < u\) where \(a = e^{r\Delta t}\).
  • \(u, d\): up and down multipliers; \(r\): risk-free rate, continuous compounding; \(a = e^{r\Delta t}\): the growth factor of cash over one step (use \(a = e^{(r-q)\Delta t}\) for a stock with dividend yield \(q\), and \(a=1\) for a futures price).
  • \(V_u, V_d\) (or \(c_u, c_d\)): derivative payoffs in the up/down state.
  • \(\Delta\): number of shares in the replicating portfolio; \(B\): cash position.
  • \(q_u\) (often just \(q\)): risk-neutral probability of the up-move; \(q_d = 1-q_u\).
  • \(\phi_u, \phi_d\): state (Arrow–Debreu) prices of the up- and down-state.
  • CRR calibration: to match a lognormal volatility \(\sigma\), take \(u = e^{\sigma\sqrt{\Delta t}}\), \(d = 1/u = e^{-\sigma\sqrt{\Delta t}}\).

Central Results

One-period binomial pricing

The replicating portfolio \(\Delta\) shares \(+\) cash \(B\) satisfies \(\Delta S_0u + Be^{r\Delta t} = V_u\) and \(\Delta S_0d + Be^{r\Delta t} = V_d\), giving \[ \Delta = \frac{V_u - V_d}{S_0(u-d)}, \qquad q = \frac{a - d}{u - d},\quad a = e^{r\Delta t}, \] \[ V_0 = \Delta S_0 + B = e^{-r\Delta t}\bigl[q\,V_u + (1-q)\,V_d\bigr]. \] Reading: price \(=\) discounted expectation under \(q\). The shape of \(q\): it is exactly the weight that makes the stock's expected growth equal the risk-free growth, \(qS_0u + (1-q)S_0d = S_0a\). Absence of arbitrage \(\iff d < a < u \iff 0 < q < 1\).

State prices and the fundamental theorem

\[ \phi_u = e^{-r\Delta t}\,q, \qquad \phi_d = e^{-r\Delta t}(1-q), \qquad V_0 = V_u\phi_u + V_d\phi_d . \] Consistency checks worth quoting in an exam: \(\phi_u + \phi_d = e^{-r\Delta t}\) (a sure 1 is a zero-coupon bond) and \(\phi_u S_0u + \phi_d S_0d = S_0\) (the stock prices itself). Fundamental theorem of asset pricing: no arbitrage \(\iff\) strictly positive state prices exist \(\iff\) an equivalent risk-neutral measure \(Q\) exists; the market is complete \(\iff\) they are unique.

Multi-period trees and American options

Work backwards from maturity. European: at each node \(V = e^{-r\Delta t}[qV_u + (1-q)V_d]\). American: at each node \[ V = \max\Bigl(\underbrace{\text{exercise value}}_{(K-S)^+ \text{ for a put}},\; \underbrace{e^{-r\Delta t}[qV_u+(1-q)V_d]}_{\text{continuation value}}\Bigr). \] As the number of steps \(n\to\infty\) with CRR calibration, the European call price converges to the Black–Scholes formula.

Key Derivations

Deriving \(q=(a-d)/(u-d)\) from replication:
  1. Solve the two replication equations: subtracting gives \(\Delta = (V_u-V_d)/(S_0(u-d))\); substituting back, \(B = e^{-r\Delta t}\,(uV_d - dV_u)/(u-d)\).
  2. The option price is the portfolio cost: \(V_0 = \Delta S_0 + B\). Collect the coefficients of \(V_u\) and \(V_d\): \[ V_0 = e^{-r\Delta t}\left[\frac{a-d}{u-d}\,V_u + \frac{u-a}{u-d}\,V_d\right]. \]
  3. Define \(q = (a-d)/(u-d)\); note \( (u-a)/(u-d) = 1-q\) and both lie in \((0,1)\) exactly when \(d

    Exam Questions — What They Actually Ask

    Exam Pattern
    • "In a one/two-step tree with given \(S_0,u,d,r\), price a European call/put." (risk-neutral probabilities + backward induction)
    • "Find the replicating portfolio \((\Delta, B)\) at time 0."
    • "Find the state prices \(\phi_u, \phi_d\) and use them to price the claim."
    • "Price the American put in the same tree; at which nodes is early exercise optimal?"
    • "Show that no arbitrage requires \(d < e^{r\Delta t} < u\)."

    Worked Example

    June 2026, Question 1 --- Binomial tree, risk-neutral valuation, early exercise

    Question. A non-dividend-paying stock with current price \(S_0\) follows a recombining binomial tree with up-factor \(u\), down-factor \(d\), and constant continuously-compounded rate \(r\) over each step \(\Delta t\).

    (a) Derive the risk-neutral probability \(q\) of an up-move and explain risk-neutral valuation.

    (b) Derive \(c_0\) for a European call with strike \(K\) and expiry \(2\Delta t\).

    (c) Assuming \(r\ge0\), give a no-arbitrage argument that early exercise of an American call is never optimal on a non-dividend-paying stock; how does a large dividend change this?

    Solution.

    (a) Construct \(\Pi = \Delta\) shares \(-\) 1 option, choose \(\Delta = (f_u - f_d)/(S_0 u - S_0 d)\) to make the portfolio riskless. No-arbitrage requires \(\Pi\) to grow at rate \(r\); solving for \(f\) gives \(f = e^{-r\Delta t}[q\,f_u + (1-q)f_d]\) with \[ q = \frac{e^{r\Delta t}-d}{u-d}. \] Risk-neutral valuation: in the risk-neutral world every asset earns \(r\); derivative prices are discounted risk-neutral expected payoffs.

    (b) Terminal payoffs: \(f_{uu}=\max(u^2S_0-K,0)\), \(f_{ud}=\max(udS_0-K,0)\), \(f_{dd}=\max(d^2S_0-K,0)\). Backward induction: \[ c_0 = e^{-2r\Delta t}\bigl[q^2 f_{uu} + 2q(1-q)f_{ud} + (1-q)^2 f_{dd}\bigr]. \]

    (c) Two reasons early exercise is sub-optimal: (i) insurance value — exercising kills the right to walk away if the stock falls; (ii) time value of money — waiting means paying \(K\) later, not now. Formally, \(c \ge S_0 - Ke^{-rT} > S_0 - K\) when \(r>0\), so the option is always worth more than its intrinsic value. A large dividend before expiry can flip this because the stock price drops ex-dividend, making the call less valuable if held.

    Key marks: (a) delta-hedge argument, no-arbitrage, explicit formula for \(q\); (b) terminal payoffs and backward induction; (c) two-part argument (insurance + time value of \(K\)) and mention of dividend exception.

    Common Mistakes

    Common Mistakes
    • Using \(\Delta t = T\) instead of \(T/n\) in \(a = e^{r\Delta t}\) — the single most common numerical slip in two-step questions.
    • Treating \(q\) as a real-world probability, or "estimating" it from the stock's expected return \(\mu\). \(q\) comes only from \(a,u,d\).
    • For American options, forgetting to compare exercise vs.\ continuation at intermediate nodes (not just at maturity).
4GBM, Stochastic Calculus and Itô's Lemma (19/21 exams)

The Core Idea

To price options in continuous time we need a mathematical model of how prices wiggle. The building block is Brownian motion \(z_t\): the continuous-time limit of a coin-flip random walk. Think of a drunkard taking infinitely many infinitesimally small steps — over any interval of length \(\Delta t\) his displacement is normal with mean 0 and variance \(\Delta t\). The crucial, strange property: the wiggles are so violent that \((\mathrm{d} z)^2 = \mathrm{d} t\) — the square of the noise is not negligible, it behaves like time itself. That single fact is why ordinary calculus fails and why Itô's lemma has an extra term.

Stock prices cannot go negative, and what matters to investors is percentage returns, so we model the return, not the price: \(\mathrm{d} S/S = \mu\,\mathrm{d} t + \sigma\,\mathrm{d} z\). This is geometric Brownian motion (GBM): in each instant the stock earns a deterministic drift \(\mu\,\mathrm{d} t\) plus a normal shock with standard deviation \(\sigma\sqrt{\mathrm{d} t}\). Its solution makes \(\ln S_T\) normally distributed — prices are lognormal, returns are normal.

Itô's lemma is the chain rule of this world. If \(S\) follows a stochastic process and \(G(S,t)\) is any smooth function — an option price, \(\ln S\), a bond yield — then \(G\) also follows a stochastic process, and Itô tells you its drift and volatility. The extra ingredient beyond the ordinary chain rule is the second-derivative term \(\tfrac12 G_{SS}(\mathrm{d} S)^2 = \tfrac12 G_{SS}\sigma^2S^2\mathrm{d} t\): because the path jiggles, curvature of \(G\) converts noise into drift. A convex function of a noisy variable drifts upward more than the naive chain rule suggests (think of averaging over the jiggle: convexity means ups help more than downs hurt). This lemma is the single most-used tool on the exam — it appears in some form in nearly every paper.

Key Definitions and Notation

  • \(z_t\) (also written \(W_t\)): a Wiener process / Brownian motion: \(z_0=0\); increments \(z_{t+\Delta t}-z_t \sim N(0,\Delta t)\), independent across non-overlapping intervals; continuous paths. Informally \(\mathrm{d} z = \epsilon\sqrt{\mathrm{d} t}\) with \(\epsilon\sim N(0,1)\).
  • Multiplication table (memorise): \((\mathrm{d} z)^2 = \mathrm{d} t\), \(\mathrm{d} z\,\mathrm{d} t = 0\), \((\mathrm{d} t)^2 = 0\).
  • Generalised Wiener process: \(\mathrm{d} x = a\,\mathrm{d} t + b\,\mathrm{d} z\) with constants \(a\) (drift) and \(b\) (volatility coefficient).
  • Itô process: \(\mathrm{d} x = a(x,t)\,\mathrm{d} t + b(x,t)\,\mathrm{d} z\) — drift and volatility may depend on the state and time.
  • GBM: \(\mathrm{d} S = \mu S\,\mathrm{d} t + \sigma S\,\mathrm{d} z\); \(\mu\) = expected return (drift, per year), \(\sigma\) = volatility (per \(\sqrt{\text{year}}\)).
  • \(G(S,t)\) (or \(G(x,t)\)): any twice-differentiable function of the process and time; subscripts denote partials: \(G_S = \partial G/\partial S\), etc.

Central Results

Itô's lemma --- THE exam tool

If \(\mathrm{d} x = a\,\mathrm{d} t + b\,\mathrm{d} z\) and \(G = G(x,t)\), then \[ \mathrm{d} G \;=\; \Bigl(\frac{\partial G}{\partial t} + a\,\frac{\partial G}{\partial x} + \tfrac12 b^2\,\frac{\partial^2 G}{\partial x^2}\Bigr)\mathrm{d} t \;+\; b\,\frac{\partial G}{\partial x}\,\mathrm{d} z . \] For GBM (\(a = \mu S\), \(b = \sigma S\)): \[ \mathrm{d} G = \Bigl(G_t + \mu S\,G_S + \tfrac12\sigma^2S^2\,G_{SS}\Bigr)\mathrm{d} t + \sigma S\,G_S\,\mathrm{d} z . \] Why the shape: Taylor-expand \(G\) to second order, \(\mathrm{d} G = G_t\mathrm{d} t + G_x\mathrm{d} x + \tfrac12 G_{xx}(\mathrm{d} x)^2 + \dots\), and use \((\mathrm{d} x)^2 = b^2(\mathrm{d} z)^2 = b^2\mathrm{d} t\) (everything of higher order dies). The Itô correction \(\tfrac12 b^2G_{xx}\,\mathrm{d} t\) is the fingerprint of randomness.

Solution of GBM and lognormality

Applying Itô to \(G=\ln S\) (worked below) gives \(\mathrm{d}\ln S = (\mu - \tfrac12\sigma^2)\mathrm{d} t + \sigma\,\mathrm{d} z\), hence \[ S_T = S_0\exp\Bigl[(\mu - \tfrac12\sigma^2)T + \sigma z_T\Bigr], \qquad \ln S_T \sim N\Bigl(\ln S_0 + (\mu-\tfrac12\sigma^2)T,\ \sigma^2T\Bigr), \] and \(\mathrm{E}[S_T] = S_0e^{\mu T}\). Note the famous gap: the median growth rate is \(\mu-\tfrac12\sigma^2\), less than the mean rate \(\mu\), because a few huge lognormal outcomes drag the mean up. The \(-\tfrac12\sigma^2\) is pure Itô correction (with \(G=\ln S\) concave, noise subtracts drift).

Standard Itô applications --- learn these transformations

Given \(\mathrm{d} S = \mu S\,\mathrm{d} t + \sigma S\,\mathrm{d} z\):

\[ \begin{array}{lll} G = \ln S: & \mathrm{d} G = (\mu - \tfrac12\sigma^2)\,\mathrm{d} t + \sigma\,\mathrm{d} z & \text{(constant coefficients!)}\\[3pt] G = S^n: & \mathrm{d} G = \bigl(n\mu + \tfrac12 n(n-1)\sigma^2\bigr)G\,\mathrm{d} t + n\sigma\,G\,\mathrm{d} z & \text{(again GBM, new coefficients)}\\[3pt] G = 1/S: & \mathrm{d} G = (-\mu + \sigma^2)\,G\,\mathrm{d} t - \sigma\,G\,\mathrm{d} z & \text{(the } n=-1 \text{ case)}\\[3pt] G = e^{aS}: & \mathrm{d} G = \bigl(a\mu S + \tfrac12 a^2\sigma^2S^2\bigr)G\,\mathrm{d} t + a\sigma S\,G\,\mathrm{d} z & \\[3pt] G = Se^{r(T-t)}: & \mathrm{d} G = (\mu - r)G\,\mathrm{d} t + \sigma G\,\mathrm{d} z & \text{(forward price of }S) \end{array} \] Bond-flavoured favourites: if \(\mathrm{d} P = \mu_P P\,\mathrm{d} t + \sigma_P P\,\mathrm{d} z\) for a bond price \(P(t,T)\), then the yield \(R = -\ln P/(T-t)\) follows (Itô applied to \(G(P,t) = -\ln P/(T-t)\)): \[ \mathrm{d} R = \frac{1}{T-t}\Bigl(R - \mu_P + \tfrac12\sigma_P^2\Bigr)\mathrm{d} t \;-\; \frac{\sigma_P}{T-t}\,\mathrm{d} z . \]

Shortcomings of GBM (a recurring essay part)
  • Constant volatility contradicted by data: volatility clusters in time and varies with strike/maturity (the implied-volatility smile/skew).
  • Normal log-returns underestimate extremes: empirical returns have fat tails and skewness; crashes (jumps) occur far more often than GBM allows. Remedies: jump-diffusions, stochastic volatility (e.g.\ Heston), local volatility.
  • Continuous paths: no jumps, so sudden news moves are impossible in the model.
  • Constant \(r\) and \(\mu\); no dividends unless added; independence of increments rules out momentum/mean reversion; and for interest rates, GBM is inappropriate because rates mean-revert and do not grow exponentially.

Key Derivations

Itô for \(G=\ln S\) — write this out until it takes 60 seconds (full version in the worked example below). The routine that earns the marks, always in this order:
  1. Identify the process: \(a = \mu S\), \(b = \sigma S\).
  2. Compute the three partials of \(G\): \(G_S\), \(G_{SS}\), \(G_t\).
  3. Substitute into Itô's lemma and simplify.
  4. (If asked) integrate: constant-coefficient processes integrate directly to give the distribution.

Exam Questions — What They Actually Ask

Exam Pattern
  • "Assume \(\mathrm{d} S = \mu S\,\mathrm{d} t + \sigma S\,\mathrm{d} z\). Use Itô's lemma to find the process followed by \(G\)" with \(G = \ln S,\ S^n,\ 1/S,\ e^{aS},\ S^2\), a forward price \(Se^{r(T-t)}\), a bond price \(P(r,t)\), \(\ln P\), or the yield \(R(t,T)\).
  • "Show that \(S_T\) is lognormally distributed and give \(\mathrm{E}[S_T]\)."
  • "Define a Wiener process / an Itô process."
  • "What are the shortcomings of GBM as a model of stock prices?"
  • "Is \(G\) itself a GBM? What are its drift and volatility?" (for \(G = S^n\): yes, with drift \(n\mu + \tfrac12n(n-1)\sigma^2\) and volatility \(n\sigma\))

Worked Example

August 2024, Question 1 --- Itô's lemma on the exponential

Question. The log-price \(Z_t\) follows \(\mathrm{d} Z = \mu\,\mathrm{d} t + \sigma\,\mathrm{d} z\) under \(P\).

(a) Let \(G(x)=e^x\). Use Itô's lemma to find the process followed by \(S_t := G(Z_t)\).

(b) For given \(Z_0\), state the distribution of \(Z_T\).

(c) What are the main empirical shortcomings of this model as a description of stock returns?

Solution.

(a) Since \(G(x) = G'(x) = G''(x) = e^x\) and \(\partial G/\partial t = 0\), Itô's lemma gives: \[ \mathrm{d} S = \Bigl(\mu G'(Z) + \tfrac{1}{2}\sigma^2 G''(Z)\Bigr)\mathrm{d} t + \sigma G'(Z)\,\mathrm{d} z = \Bigl(\mu + \tfrac{1}{2}\sigma^2\Bigr)S\,\mathrm{d} t + \sigma S\,\mathrm{d} z. \] So \(S\) follows a geometric Brownian motion with drift \(\mu+\tfrac12\sigma^2\) and volatility \(\sigma\).

(b) \(Z_T = Z_0 + \mu T + \sigma(z_T - z_0)\), so \(Z_T \sim N(Z_0 + \mu T,\,\sigma^2 T)\) — normally distributed. Equivalently, \(S_T = e^{Z_T}\) is log-normal.

(c) Key shortcomings: (i) continuous sample paths (real prices can jump); (ii) constant volatility (empirically, volatility clusters and the implied vol surface is skewed); (iii) normal log-returns imply thin tails (markets exhibit fat tails and negative skew); (iv) constant drift \(\mu\) is unrealistic over long horizons.

Key marks: (a) apply all three partials of \(G\), collect \(S\,\mathrm{d} t\) and \(S\,\mathrm{d} z\) terms; (b) state \(N(\mu T, \sigma^2 T)\) explicitly; (c) at least two shortcomings with brief explanation.

Common Mistakes

Common Mistakes
  • Dropping the Itô correction \(\tfrac12b^2G_{SS}\,\mathrm{d} t\) (i.e.\ using the ordinary chain rule). This is the error the question is designed to catch.
  • Writing the correction as \(\tfrac12\sigma^2G_{SS}\) instead of \(\tfrac12\sigma^2S^2G_{SS}\): the diffusion coefficient of GBM is \(b=\sigma S\), so it enters squared as \(\sigma^2S^2\).
  • Sign slip in \(G=\ln S\): \(G_{SS} = -1/S^2\), which produces the famous minus \(\tfrac12\sigma^2\).
  • Confusing \(\mathrm{E}[S_T] = S_0e^{\mu T}\) with \(S_0e^{(\mu-\sigma^2/2)T}\) (the latter is the median, i.e.\ \(e^{\mathrm{E}[\ln S_T]}\)).
5The Market Price of Risk (14/21 exams)

The Core Idea

Imagine two shops selling the same brand of coffee in different package sizes. Whatever the price per package, the price per kilogram must be the same, or customers arbitrage between shops. The market price of risk says exactly this about risky assets: if two securities are exposed to the same source of randomness (the same Brownian motion \(z\)), then whatever their individual expected returns and volatilities, their excess return per unit of volatility must be identical: \[ \lambda = \frac{\mu_f - r}{\sigma_f} = \frac{\mu_g - r}{\sigma_g}. \] \(\lambda\) is the "price per kilogram of risk": the extra return the market pays for bearing one unit of \(z\)-risk. If security \(f\) paid more per unit of risk than \(g\), you could combine a long position in \(f\) with a short position in \(g\), sized so the \(\mathrm{d} z\) terms cancel exactly, creating a riskless portfolio that outperforms the bank account — arbitrage.

Why does this matter so much? Because it is the bridge between the real world and the risk-neutral world. Substituting \(\mu = r + \lambda\sigma\) into any asset's dynamics and shifting the Brownian motion by \(\lambda\) (Girsanov's theorem, Section 8) turns every asset's drift into \(r\). The whole machinery of risk-neutral pricing — "discount the expected payoff at the risk-free rate as if everyone were risk-neutral" — works precisely because \(\lambda\) is common across assets and can be absorbed into the probability measure. And in interest-rate and commodity models, where the driving state variable (the short rate \(r\), a temperature, a convenience yield) is not itself a traded asset, \(\lambda\) survives in the pricing equations as a free parameter that must be estimated or calibrated — a point examiners love to probe.

Key Definitions and Notation

  • \(f, g\): prices of two traded securities (derivatives) driven by the same single Brownian motion \(z\): \[ \mathrm{d} f = \mu_f f\,\mathrm{d} t + \sigma_f f\,\mathrm{d} z, \qquad \mathrm{d} g = \mu_g g\,\mathrm{d} t + \sigma_g g\,\mathrm{d} z . \] \(\mu_f,\mu_g\): expected returns; \(\sigma_f,\sigma_g\): volatilities (possibly functions of state and time).
  • \(\lambda\): the market price of risk of the factor \(z\) — excess expected return per unit volatility, common to all assets driven by \(z\).
  • \(\theta\): a state variable (e.g.\ the short rate, a commodity convenience yield) with dynamics \(\mathrm{d}\theta = m\,\mathrm{d} t + s\,\mathrm{d} z\); it may or may not be a traded asset.
  • Real-world (physical) measure \(P\); risk-neutral measure \(Q\); under \(Q\) the Brownian motion is \(z^Q\) with \(\mathrm{d} z^Q = \mathrm{d} z + \lambda\,\mathrm{d} t\).

Central Results

Market price of risk

All traded securities exposed only to the Brownian motion \(z\) satisfy \[ \frac{\mu_f - r}{\sigma_f} = \lambda \quad\text{for a common }\lambda(t), \qquad\text{equivalently}\qquad \mu_f = r + \lambda\,\sigma_f . \] Shape of the formula: it is a security-market-line in continuous time — expected return \(=\) risk-free rate \(+\) (price of risk)\(\times\)(quantity of risk).

Risk-neutral dynamics

Setting \(\mathrm{d} z^Q = \mathrm{d} z + \lambda\,\mathrm{d} t\) (Girsanov) converts real-world dynamics into risk-neutral dynamics: \[ \mathrm{d} f = \mu_f f\,\mathrm{d} t + \sigma_f f\,\mathrm{d} z = (\mu_f - \lambda\sigma_f)f\,\mathrm{d} t + \sigma_f f\,\mathrm{d} z^Q = r f\,\mathrm{d} t + \sigma_f f\,\mathrm{d} z^Q . \] Under \(Q\) every traded asset drifts at \(r\); volatility is unchanged. For a non-traded state variable \(\theta\) with \(\mathrm{d}\theta = m\,\mathrm{d} t + s\,\mathrm{d} z\), the risk-neutral drift is \(m - \lambda s\): \[ \mathrm{d}\theta = (m - \lambda s)\,\mathrm{d} t + s\,\mathrm{d} z^Q . \] Because \(\theta\) itself is not an asset one can buy, no-arbitrage does not pin down \(m-\lambda s\) separately — \(\lambda\) must be specified exogenously or backed out from prices of traded derivatives. (This is exactly the situation in short-rate models, Section 10.)

Key Derivations

The two-security riskless-portfolio argument (the exam classic). Given \(\mathrm{d} f = \mu_f f\,\mathrm{d} t + \sigma_f f\,\mathrm{d} z\) and \(\mathrm{d} g = \mu_g g\,\mathrm{d} t + \sigma_g g\,\mathrm{d} z\):
  1. Form the portfolio \(\Pi\) holding \(\sigma_g\,g\) units of \(f\) and \(-\sigma_f f\) units of \(g\) (positions fixed over the next instant): \[ \Pi = (\sigma_g g)\,f - (\sigma_f f)\,g . \] The weights are chosen precisely so the random terms cancel.
  2. Its instantaneous change: \[ \mathrm{d}\Pi = \sigma_g g\,\mathrm{d} f - \sigma_f f\,\mathrm{d} g = (\mu_f\sigma_g - \mu_g\sigma_f)\,fg\,\mathrm{d} t . \] The \(\mathrm{d} z\) terms cancel: \(\Pi\) is (instantaneously) riskless.
  3. A riskless portfolio must earn the risk-free rate: \(\mathrm{d}\Pi = r\,\Pi\,\mathrm{d} t = r(\sigma_g - \sigma_f)fg\,\mathrm{d} t\). Equate coefficients: \[ \mu_f\sigma_g - \mu_g\sigma_f = r\sigma_g - r\sigma_f . \]
  4. Rearrange: \(\sigma_g(\mu_f - r) = \sigma_f(\mu_g - r)\), i.e. \[ \boxed{\ \frac{\mu_f - r}{\sigma_f} = \frac{\mu_g - r}{\sigma_g} \equiv \lambda\ } \] The ratio is the same for every pair of securities driven by \(z\), so it depends only on the risk factor, not the security: it deserves its own name, the market price of risk.

Exam Questions — What They Actually Ask

Exam Pattern
  • "Two derivatives \(f\) and \(g\) depend on the same Brownian motion. Show that \((\mu_f-r)/\sigma_f = (\mu_g-r)/\sigma_g\)." (the derivation above, verbatim)
  • "Define the market price of risk and interpret it."
  • "Derive the risk-neutral process for the underlying / for \(f\)." (substitute \(\mu = r+\lambda\sigma\) and shift the BM)
  • "The state variable \(\theta\) is not traded. Explain the role of the market price of risk in pricing derivatives on \(\theta\)." (risk-neutral drift \(m-\lambda s\); \(\lambda\) not determined by no-arbitrage alone)
  • "How does this relate to the Black–Scholes PDE / to the drift term \(r - \lambda\sigma\) in short-rate models?"

Worked Example

June 2025, Question 2 --- Deriving the market price of risk

Question. Two derivatives \(f_1\) and \(f_2\) depend on a single state variable \(\theta\) with \(\mathrm{d}\theta/\theta = m\,\mathrm{d} t + s\,\mathrm{d} z\). The prices follow GBMs with drifts \(\mu_1,\mu_2\) and volatilities \(\sigma_1,\sigma_2\); the risk-free rate is \(r\).

(a) Use an arbitrage argument to derive a relationship between \((\mu_1,\sigma_1)\) and \((\mu_2,\sigma_2)\).

(b) Define the market price of risk \(\lambda\) and express the general result.

(c) If \(\theta\) is itself a traded asset paying a continuous dividend rate \(\delta\), what can you say about \(\lambda\)?

Solution.

(a) Form the portfolio \(\Pi = \sigma_2 f_2 \cdot f_1 - \sigma_1 f_1 \cdot f_2\). Its \(\mathrm{d} z\) terms cancel: \(\Pi\) is locally risk-free. No-arbitrage requires \(\mathrm{d}\Pi = r\Pi\,\mathrm{d} t\), which after dividing by \(f_1f_2\) gives: \[ \frac{\mu_1 - r}{\sigma_1} = \frac{\mu_2 - r}{\sigma_2}. \]

(b) Since this ratio is the same for all derivatives on \(\theta\), define the market price of risk: \[ \lambda = \frac{\mu_i - r}{\sigma_i} \quad \text{(excess return per unit of volatility, equal for all such derivatives).} \] Every derivative satisfies \(\mu_i = r + \lambda\sigma_i\). Under \(Q\), Girsanov replaces \(\mathrm{d} z\) with \(\mathrm{d}\tilde{z} = \mathrm{d} z + \lambda\,\mathrm{d} t\), removing the excess return.

(c) If \(\theta\) is a traded asset with dividend yield \(\delta\), total return must equal \(r\): \(m + \delta = r + \lambda s\), giving \(\lambda = (m + \delta - r)/s\).

Key marks: (a) form the specific portfolio so \(\mathrm{d} z\) cancels; invoke no-arbitrage for \(r\Pi\,\mathrm{d} t\); rearrange to equal ratios; (b) name and define \(\lambda\); (c) include dividend in total return.

Common Mistakes

Common Mistakes
  • Choosing portfolio weights that do not cancel the noise (e.g.\ \(1\) of \(f\) minus \(1\) of \(g\)): the weights must be \(\sigma_g g\) and \(\sigma_f f\) (value-weighted by the other asset's volatility).
  • Forgetting that "riskless" portfolios must earn exactly \(r\) — stating the cancellation but never setting \(\mathrm{d}\Pi = r\Pi\,\mathrm{d} t\) leaves the derivation unfinished.
  • Claiming no-arbitrage determines \(\lambda\) for non-traded state variables. It does not; it only forces \(\lambda\) to be common across derivatives.
  • Sign conventions: some books use \(\mathrm{d} z^Q = \mathrm{d} z + \lambda\mathrm{d} t\) (drift decreases by \(\lambda\sigma\)). Pick one convention and stay consistent through the answer.
6The Black–Scholes–Merton Model (16/21 exams)

The Core Idea

Black, Scholes and Merton asked: if the stock follows GBM and you can trade continuously, what is a European option worth? Their answer rests on a beautiful trick — delta hedging. An option and its underlying stock are driven by the same random shock \(\mathrm{d} z\); so a portfolio that is long the option and short exactly \(\Delta = \partial V/\partial S\) shares has the shocks cancel out, instant by instant. What remains is a riskless portfolio, and riskless things must earn the risk-free rate \(r\). Writing down that statement as an equation produces a partial differential equation that every derivative on the stock must satisfy — the BSM PDE. The option price is then found by solving the PDE with the option's payoff as terminal condition.

Notice what vanished: the stock's expected return \(\mu\). Just as in the binomial model, disagreement about where the stock is headed is irrelevant, because hedging removes direction. Only the volatility \(\sigma\) — the size of the wiggles — matters. Equivalently (Feynman–Kac), the solution of the PDE is a discounted expectation under the risk-neutral measure: \(c = e^{-rT}\mathrm{E}^Q[(S_T-K)^+]\), where under \(Q\) the stock drifts at \(r\). Evaluating that lognormal integral gives the famous closed formula.

Read the call formula \(c = S_0\Phi(d_1) - Ke^{-rT}\Phi(d_2)\) like a sentence: "what I expect to receive minus what I expect to pay, both risk-neutrally discounted". \(\Phi(d_2)\) is the risk-neutral probability the option finishes in the money, so \(Ke^{-rT}\Phi(d_2)\) is the PV of the strike you will pay; \(S_0\Phi(d_1)\) is the PV of the stock you will receive, with \(d_1 > d_2\) because given exercise, the stock tends to be high (a conditional expectation, not just a probability, hides in \(\Phi(d_1)\)).

Key Definitions and Notation

  • \(V(S,t)\): price of a generic derivative as a function of stock price and time; \(c\), \(p\): European call and put prices.
  • \(\Phi(\cdot)\): standard normal cdf; \(\varphi(\cdot) = \Phi'\): standard normal density.
  • \(d_1 = \dfrac{\ln(S_0/K) + (r + \sigma^2/2)T}{\sigma\sqrt T}\), \(d_2 = d_1 - \sigma\sqrt T = \dfrac{\ln(S_0/K) + (r - \sigma^2/2)T}{\sigma\sqrt T}\).
  • \(q\): continuous dividend yield; \(r_d, r_f\): domestic/foreign rates (FX options); \(F_0\): forward/futures price (Black's model).
  • Assumptions of BSM: GBM with constant \(\mu,\sigma\); constant \(r\); no dividends (relaxed below); frictionless continuous trading, short-selling allowed, no arbitrage.

Central Results

The BSM partial differential equation

Any derivative \(V(S,t)\) on a non-dividend stock satisfies \[ \frac{\partial V}{\partial t} + rS\frac{\partial V}{\partial S} + \tfrac12\sigma^2S^2\frac{\partial^2V}{\partial S^2} = rV . \] Shape: it says "time decay \(+\) risk-neutral drift pickup \(+\) convexity pickup \(=\) risk-free growth of the value". Note \(\mu\) is absent and the drift coefficient is \(rS\) — the PDE lives in the risk-neutral world. With dividend yield \(q\), replace \(rS\,\partial V/\partial S\) by \((r-q)S\,\partial V/\partial S\).

BSM formulas

European call and put on a non-dividend stock: \[ c = S_0\Phi(d_1) - Ke^{-rT}\Phi(d_2), \qquad p = Ke^{-rT}\Phi(-d_2) - S_0\Phi(-d_1), \] with \(d_{1,2}\) as above. Consistency: \(c - p = S_0 - Ke^{-rT}\) (put–call parity), using \(\Phi(x)+\Phi(-x)=1\).

Dividend yield \(q\) (Merton): replace \(S_0 \to S_0e^{-qT}\) everywhere (also inside \(d_1\)): \(c = S_0e^{-qT}\Phi(d_1) - Ke^{-rT}\Phi(d_2)\), \(d_1 = \frac{\ln(S_0/K)+(r-q+\sigma^2/2)T}{\sigma\sqrt T}\).

FX options (Garman–Kohlhagen): an exchange rate is an asset paying the foreign rate: set \(q = r_f\), \(r = r_d\): \(c = S_0e^{-r_fT}\Phi(d_1) - Ke^{-r_dT}\Phi(d_2)\).

Options on futures (Black's formula): a futures price has zero drift under \(Q\) (it costs nothing to enter), i.e.\ \(q=r\): \[ c = e^{-rT}\bigl[F_0\Phi(d_1) - K\Phi(d_2)\bigr], \qquad d_1 = \frac{\ln(F_0/K)+\sigma^2T/2}{\sigma\sqrt T},\ d_2 = d_1 - \sigma\sqrt T . \] Black's formula is the workhorse for caps and swaptions in Sections 9 and 12.

Boundary sanity checks (quick marks)

As \(S_0\to\infty\): \(c \to S_0 - Ke^{-rT}\) (sure exercise = forward). As \(S_0\to0\): \(c\to0\). As \(\sigma\to0\): \(c \to (S_0 - Ke^{-rT})^+\). As \(T\to0\): \(c\to(S_0-K)^+\). Implied volatility is the \(\sigma\) that makes the BSM price match the market price; its variation across strikes (the smile) is evidence against constant-\(\sigma\) GBM.

Key Derivations

Deriving the BSM PDE (the single most-asked derivation in the course):
  1. Dynamics. \(\mathrm{d} S = \mu S\,\mathrm{d} t + \sigma S\,\mathrm{d} z\). By Itô, the derivative \(V(S,t)\) follows \[ \mathrm{d} V = \Bigl(V_t + \mu S V_S + \tfrac12\sigma^2S^2V_{SS}\Bigr)\mathrm{d} t + \sigma S V_S\,\mathrm{d} z . \]
  2. Hedge portfolio. Form \(\Pi = V - \Delta S\) (long the derivative, short \(\Delta\) shares), with \(\Delta\) held fixed over \([t, t+\mathrm{d} t]\): \[ \mathrm{d}\Pi = \mathrm{d} V - \Delta\,\mathrm{d} S = \Bigl(V_t + \mu S V_S + \tfrac12\sigma^2S^2V_{SS} - \Delta\mu S\Bigr)\mathrm{d} t + \sigma S\,(V_S - \Delta)\,\mathrm{d} z . \]
  3. Kill the risk. Choose \(\Delta = V_S = \partial V/\partial S\). The \(\mathrm{d} z\) term vanishes — and so does every appearance of \(\mu\): \[ \mathrm{d}\Pi = \Bigl(V_t + \tfrac12\sigma^2S^2V_{SS}\Bigr)\mathrm{d} t . \]
  4. Riskless portfolios earn \(r\). No-arbitrage: \(\mathrm{d}\Pi = r\Pi\,\mathrm{d} t = r(V - SV_S)\,\mathrm{d} t\). Equate: \[ V_t + \tfrac12\sigma^2S^2V_{SS} = rV - rSV_S \quad\Longrightarrow\quad V_t + rSV_S + \tfrac12\sigma^2S^2V_{SS} = rV . \qquad\blacksquare \]
  5. Boundary condition selects the instrument: \(V(S,T) = (S-K)^+\) for a call, \((K-S)^+\) for a put, \(S-K\) for a forward. Solving with the call payoff (via risk-neutral expectation of the lognormal) yields the BSM formula.
Remark for extra credit: the hedge must be rebalanced continuously (\(\Delta\) changes as \(S\) and \(t\) move); the portfolio is only instantaneously riskless.

Verifying the formula solves the PDE / the Greeks identity — see the Greeks chapter (Section 7), where \(\Theta + rS\Delta + \tfrac12\sigma^2S^2\Gamma = rc\) is shown to be nothing but the PDE evaluated at the call.

Exam Questions — What They Actually Ask

Exam Pattern
  • "Derive the Black–Scholes–Merton PDE" — steps 1–4 above; always state where \(\mu\) drops out and why the portfolio is only instantaneously riskless.
  • "State the BSM call formula and interpret \(\Phi(d_1)\) and \(\Phi(d_2)\)." (\(\Phi(d_2) = Q(S_T>K)\); \(\Phi(d_1)\) = delta / expected stock conditional on exercise)
  • "Compute a call/put price for given numbers."
  • "Adapt the formula to a dividend-paying stock / FX (Garman–Kohlhagen) / futures (Black)."
  • "List the assumptions of the model / discuss which are violated in practice." (link to GBM shortcomings, smile)
  • "Show the price satisfies the PDE / find \(\Delta,\Gamma,\Theta\) and verify \(\Theta + rS\Delta + \frac12\sigma^2S^2\Gamma = rc\)."

Worked Example

June 2020, Question 3 --- The BSM PDE and the forward contract

Question. A stock pays a continuous dividend rate \(\delta\).

(a) Find the forward price \(F_t\) for delivery at \(T\).

(b) Show that the value of a forward contract with delivery price \(K\) is \(V(S_t,t;K)=S_te^{-\delta(T-t)}-Ke^{-r(T-t)}\).

(c) The BSM PDE for a derivative on this stock is \(\partial_t V + \tfrac12\sigma^2S^2\partial_{SS}V + (r-\delta)S\partial_S V - rV = 0\). Verify that the forward value from (b) satisfies this PDE.

Solution.

(a) Buy \(e^{-\delta(T-t)}\) shares at \(t\), reinvest dividends: get 1 share at \(T\) at cost \(S_te^{-\delta(T-t)}\). Alternatively enter the forward. No-arbitrage: \(F_t = S_te^{(r-\delta)(T-t)}\).

(b) Under \(Q\), \(V = e^{-r(T-t)}\mathrm{E}^Q_t[S_T - K]\). Since \(\mathrm{E}^Q_t[S_T] = S_te^{(r-\delta)(T-t)}\): \[ V = S_te^{-\delta(T-t)} - Ke^{-r(T-t)}. \]

(c) Compute the three partial derivatives of \(V = Se^{-\delta\tau} - Ke^{-r\tau}\) (with \(\tau = T-t\)): \[ \partial_t V = \delta S e^{-\delta\tau} - rKe^{-r\tau}, \qquad \partial_S V = e^{-\delta\tau}, \qquad \partial_{SS}V = 0. \] Substitute into the PDE: \[ (\delta S e^{-\delta\tau} - rKe^{-r\tau}) + 0 + (r-\delta)S e^{-\delta\tau} - r(Se^{-\delta\tau} - Ke^{-r\tau}) = 0.\quad\checkmark \] The \(S\)-terms: \(\delta + (r-\delta) - r = 0\). The \(K\)-terms: \(-r + r = 0\). Both cancel exactly.

Key marks: (a) the dividend-adjusted arbitrage argument; (b) using \(\mathrm{E}^Q[S_T]=Se^{(r-\delta)\tau}\); (c) computing three partials correctly and showing the \(S\)-terms and \(K\)-terms each cancel separately.

Common Mistakes

Common Mistakes
  • Writing the PDE with \(\mu\) in it. The drift term is \(rS\,V_S\); if your final PDE contains \(\mu\), the hedge step went wrong.
  • Forgetting \(\sigma^2/2\) (not \(\sigma\) or \(\sigma^2\)) inside \(d_1\), or using \(\sigma T\) instead of \(\sigma\sqrt T\) in the denominator.
  • In dividend/FX variants, adjusting the drift inside \(d_1\) but forgetting to also discount the spot: it is \(S_0e^{-qT}\Phi(d_1)\).
  • Claiming the hedged portfolio is riskless forever: it is riskless only over \(\mathrm{d} t\) and must be rebalanced continuously.
7The Greeks and Hedging (15/21 exams)

The Core Idea

An option's price depends on the stock price, the passage of time, volatility and interest rates. The Greeks are simply the partial derivatives of the price with respect to each input — the option's "dashboard": Delta tells you how fast the value responds to the stock, Gamma how fast Delta itself changes (the curvature), Theta how the value bleeds as the clock ticks, Vega how it responds to volatility, Rho to interest rates. A trader running an option book does not want to gamble on the stock's direction, so she delta-hedges: holds \(-\Delta\) shares against each option so that, to first order, small moves in \(S\) leave the book's value unchanged — exactly the trick that produced the BSM PDE.

But a delta hedge is like balancing a ball on your palm: it only works for the current position, and as the stock moves the required \(\Delta\) changes — that is Gamma. High Gamma means the hedge decays quickly and must be rebalanced often. And option holders pay for that convexity through Theta: a hedged long-option position gains whenever the stock moves (Gamma is your friend) but loses value every day nothing happens (Theta is the rent you pay for Gamma). The BSM PDE makes this trade-off exact: \(\Theta + \tfrac12\sigma^2S^2\Gamma \approx r\times(\text{hedged value})\) — time decay and convexity gains offset each other at the risk-free rate. Examiners test precisely this identity.

Key Definitions and Notation

For a derivative value \(V(S,t)\) (call \(c\), put \(p\)), with \(\varphi\) the standard normal pdf and \(\tau = T-t\):
GreekDefinitionMeasuresBSM call value (no dividends)
Delta\( = V S\)exposure to \(S\)\((d_1) (0,1)\)
Gamma\( = ^2 V S^2\)curvature / hedge decay\((d_1)S_0 > 0\)
Theta\( = V t\)time decay\(-S_0(d_1)2 - rKe^-r(d_2)\)
Vega\( V = V\)volatility exposure\(S_0\,(d_1) > 0\)
Rho\( = V r\)rate exposure\(K e^-r(d_2) > 0\)
Put Greeks via put–call parity \(p = c - S_0 + Ke^{-r\tau}\) (differentiate!): \[ \Delta_p = \Phi(d_1) - 1 \in (-1,0), \qquad \Gamma_p = \Gamma_c, \qquad \mathcal V_p = \mathcal V_c, \] \[ \Theta_p = \Theta_c + rKe^{-r\tau}, \qquad \rho_p = \rho_c - K\tau e^{-r\tau}. \] A portfolio is delta-neutral (resp.\ gamma-, vega-neutral) if the corresponding portfolio Greek is 0. Greeks add across positions.

Central Results

The Greeks identity (from the BSM PDE)

Since every derivative satisfies \(V_t + rSV_S + \tfrac12\sigma^2S^2V_{SS} = rV\), and \(\Theta = V_t\), \(\Delta = V_S\), \(\Gamma = V_{SS}\): \[ \Theta + rS\Delta + \tfrac12\sigma^2S^2\Gamma = rV . \] Consequences examiners like: (i) for a delta-neutral portfolio, \(\Theta + \tfrac12\sigma^2S^2\Gamma = r\Pi\) — large positive Gamma must be paid for with large negative Theta, and vice versa; (ii) Theta is not a hedgeable risk (time is not random), it is the cost of the Gamma position.

Delta hedging mechanics

To delta-hedge a position with portfolio delta \(\Delta_\Pi\), trade \(-\Delta_\Pi\) shares (the stock has delta 1; a futures has delta \(e^{r\tau}\) per contract... in this course usually treated as 1 with continuous settlement caveats). The hedge must be rebalanced as \(S\) and \(t\) change; the rebalancing frequency needed grows with \(|\Gamma|\). To neutralise Gamma or Vega you need another option (stock has \(\Gamma = \mathcal V = 0\)): first set the Gamma/Vega with traded options, then re-adjust delta with the stock (stock trades do not disturb Gamma or Vega).

Shapes worth sketching

\(\Delta_c\) rises from 0 (deep OTM) through \(\approx0.5\) (near ATM, more precisely \(\Phi(d_1)\)) to 1 (deep ITM) — an S-curve. \(\Gamma\) is a bell peaking near ATM and exploding as \(\tau\to0\) at \(S\approx K\): near-expiry ATM options are hedging nightmares. Theta for a call is most negative ATM near expiry. Vega peaks ATM and grows with \(\sqrt\tau\).

Key Derivations

Deriving call Delta (why \(\Delta = \Phi(d_1)\), not \(\Phi(d_1)\) "plus extra terms"): differentiate \(c = S\Phi(d_1) - Ke^{-r\tau}\Phi(d_2)\) w.r.t.\ \(S\): \[ \Delta = \Phi(d_1) + S\varphi(d_1)\frac{\partial d_1}{\partial S} - Ke^{-r\tau}\varphi(d_2)\frac{\partial d_2}{\partial S}. \] Since \(\partial d_1/\partial S = \partial d_2/\partial S = 1/(S\sigma\sqrt\tau)\), the last two terms cancel by the key identity \[ S\varphi(d_1) = Ke^{-r\tau}\varphi(d_2) \] (check by writing out \(\varphi(d_2) = \varphi(d_1 - \sigma\sqrt\tau) = \varphi(d_1)e^{d_1\sigma\sqrt\tau - \sigma^2\tau/2}\) and using the definition of \(d_1\)). Hence \(\Delta = \Phi(d_1)\). The same identity streamlines Vega and Theta computations.

Verifying the identity \(\Theta + rS\Delta + \tfrac12\sigma^2S^2\Gamma = rc\): substitute the closed forms: \[ \underbrace{-\frac{S\varphi(d_1)\sigma}{2\sqrt\tau} - rKe^{-r\tau}\Phi(d_2)}_{\Theta} + rS\Phi(d_1) + \tfrac12\sigma^2S^2\cdot\frac{\varphi(d_1)}{S\sigma\sqrt\tau} = rS\Phi(d_1) - rKe^{-r\tau}\Phi(d_2) = rc , \] because the two \(\varphi(d_1)\)-terms cancel: \(\tfrac12\sigma^2S^2\frac{\varphi(d_1)}{S\sigma\sqrt\tau} = \frac{S\varphi(d_1)\sigma}{2\sqrt\tau}\). This confirms the call satisfies the BSM PDE.

Exam Questions — What They Actually Ask

Exam Pattern
  • "Define Delta, Gamma and Theta for a call and give their signs."
  • "Use the BSM PDE to express Theta in terms of Delta and Gamma" — i.e.\ \(\Theta = rV - rS\Delta - \tfrac12\sigma^2S^2\Gamma\).
  • "Show \(\Theta + rS\Delta + \tfrac12\sigma^2S^2\Gamma = rc\) for the BSM call."
  • "Differentiate put–call parity to relate put and call Greeks."
  • "A bank has sold options with total delta …; how does it delta-hedge? What happens as \(S\) moves (Gamma)?"
  • "How would you make the book gamma- and vega-neutral?" (use options, then fix delta with stock)

Worked Example

August 2024, Question 2 --- Greeks, put-call parity, and the $$--$$ identity

Question. A stock pays a continuous dividend \(\delta\).

(a) Define \(\Delta\), \(\Gamma\), \(\Theta\) of a derivative \(V(S,t)\).

(b) Derive put-call parity: \(c + Ke^{-rT} = p + S_0e^{-\delta T}\).

(c) Hence find \(\Delta_c - \Delta_p\) and show \(\Gamma_c = \Gamma_p\).

(d) For a delta-neutral portfolio \(\Pi\), derive the identity \(\Theta + \tfrac12\sigma^2S^2\Gamma = r\Pi\).

Solution.

(a) \(\Delta = \partial V/\partial S\) (sensitivity to stock price), \(\Gamma = \partial^2 V/\partial S^2\) (convexity or rate of change of delta), \(\Theta = \partial V/\partial t\) (time decay).

(b) Long call, short put replicates a forward struck at \(K\) for maturity \(T\): payoff \(S_T - K\) in all states. Its value today is \(S_0e^{-\delta T} - Ke^{-rT}\), so \(c - p = S_0e^{-\delta T} - Ke^{-rT}\), i.e.\ \(c + Ke^{-rT} = p + S_0e^{-\delta T}\).

(c) Differentiating the parity in \(S_0\): \(\Delta_c - \Delta_p = e^{-\delta T}\). Differentiating again: \(\Gamma_c - \Gamma_p = 0\), so \(\Gamma_c = \Gamma_p\).

(d) The BSM PDE is \(\Theta + \tfrac12\sigma^2S^2\Gamma + (r-\delta)S\Delta - r\Pi = 0\). Setting \(\Delta = 0\): \[ \Theta + \tfrac12\sigma^2 S^2\Gamma = r\Pi. \] Interpretation: a long-option (positive-\(\Gamma\)) portfolio earns from large moves but loses to time decay — \(\Theta < 0\) when \(\Gamma > 0\).

Key marks: (a) exact definitions; (b) payoff identity and no-arbitrage; (c) differentiate parity in \(S\), then twice; (d) substitute \(\Delta=0\) into the PDE — marks for referencing the PDE.

Common Mistakes

Common Mistakes
  • Using \(\Phi(d_1)\) (cdf) where \(\varphi(d_1)\) (density) is needed: \(\Gamma\) and Vega use the density.
  • Sign of Theta: for a long call \(\Theta<0\); a common slip is dropping the leading minus sign.
  • Hedging direction: a written call has delta \(-\Phi(d_1)\), so the hedge buys shares. Getting the sign of the position wrong reverses everything.
  • Trying to gamma-hedge with the stock: the stock's Gamma and Vega are zero; only options can adjust them.
8Martingales, Numeraires and Change of Measure (9/21 exams)

The Core Idea

A martingale is a process whose future expected value, given everything known today, equals today's value — a "fair game" with no drift. Casino intuition: your wealth playing a fair coin-toss game is a martingale; the best forecast of tomorrow's wealth is today's wealth. Risk-neutral pricing can be re-stated elegantly in this language: under the risk-neutral measure \(Q\), every traded asset grows on average at rate \(r\), so once you measure asset values in units of the bank account \(B_t = e^{rt}\) (i.e.\ divide by it), the drift is exactly cancelled and \(S_t/B_t\) is a martingale. "Discounted prices are \(Q\)-martingales" is the modern statement of no-arbitrage.

The deeper insight is that the bank account is just one possible choice of numeraire — the unit in which we count value. Choose any traded asset \(N_t\) with positive price as the measuring stick; then there exists a measure \(Q^N\) (equivalent to \(Q\)) under which every asset price divided by \(N_t\) is a martingale. Changing numeraire is like switching currencies for your accounting: the physics does not change, but some problems become trivial in the right units. The star example: take the numeraire to be the zero-coupon bond \(P(t,T)\); the associated measure \(Q^T\) is the \(T\)-forward measure, and under it the forward price \(F(t,T) = S_t/P(t,T)\) is a martingale — so the forward price is the \(Q^T\)-expectation of \(S_T\). This one trick justifies Black's formula and powers the entire LIBOR market model.

The tool that moves us between measures is Girsanov's theorem: changing to an equivalent measure shifts the drift of a Brownian motion but leaves its volatility untouched. That is why volatilities are "objective" inputs while drifts are measure-dependent bookkeeping.

Key Definitions and Notation

  • \(\F_t\): the information available at time \(t\) (a filtration). \(\mathrm{E}[\,\cdot\,|\F_t]\): expectation given that information.
  • Martingale: \(M_t\) with \(\mathrm{E}[M_T|\F_t] = M_t\) for all \(t\le T\) (and \(\mathrm{E}|M_t|<\infty\)). An Itô process is a martingale iff its drift is zero.
  • \(B_t = e^{rt}\): the money-market account (bank account, value of 1 continuously reinvested at \(r\)).
  • Numeraire \(N_t\): any traded asset with \(N_t>0\) used as unit of account; \(Q^N\): the measure under which \(X_t/N_t\) is a martingale for every traded \(X\). \(Q = Q^B\): risk-neutral measure. \(Q^T := Q^{P(\cdot,T)}\): the \(T\)-forward measure.
  • Radon–Nikodym derivative \(\mathrm{d} Q/\mathrm{d} P\): the state-by-state ratio of probabilities that converts \(P\)-expectations into \(Q\)-expectations: \(\mathrm{E}^Q[X] = \mathrm{E}^P[X\,\mathrm{d} Q/\mathrm{d} P]\).
  • \(F(t,T) = S_t/P(t,T)\): the forward price of \(S\) for delivery at \(T\) (cost-of-carry in bond units).

Central Results

Girsanov's theorem

Let \(z_t\) be a \(P\)-Brownian motion and \(\lambda_t\) an (adapted, suitably integrable) process. Define the new measure \(Q\) by \[ \frac{\mathrm{d} Q}{\mathrm{d} P}\Big|_{\F_T} = \exp\Bigl(-\int_0^T\lambda_s\,\mathrm{d} z_s - \tfrac12\int_0^T\lambda_s^2\,\mathrm{d} s\Bigr). \] Then \(z^Q_t = z_t + \int_0^t\lambda_s\,\mathrm{d} s\) (i.e.\ \(\mathrm{d} z^Q = \mathrm{d} z + \lambda\,\mathrm{d} t\)) is a Brownian motion under \(Q\). Only the drift changes; the volatility is measure-invariant. Taking \(\lambda\) = the market price of risk converts real-world dynamics into risk-neutral dynamics — this is the rigorous version of Section 5.

Risk-neutral valuation as a martingale statement

No arbitrage \(\iff\) there is a measure \(Q\sim P\) such that discounted traded-asset prices \(S_t/B_t\) are \(Q\)-martingales. Then any attainable claim with payoff \(V_T\) has price \[ V_t = B_t\,\mathrm{E}^Q\Bigl[\frac{V_T}{B_T}\Big|\F_t\Bigr] = e^{-r(T-t)}\mathrm{E}^Q[V_T|\F_t] \quad(\text{constant }r). \] General numeraire version: \(\dfrac{V_t}{N_t} = \mathrm{E}^{Q^N}\Bigl[\dfrac{V_T}{N_T}\Big|\F_t\Bigr]\) for any numeraire \(N\).

The forward measure and Black's formula

With numeraire \(P(t,T)\) (note \(P(T,T)=1\)): \[ V_t = P(t,T)\,\mathrm{E}^{Q^T}[V_T|\F_t], \qquad F(t,T) = \frac{S_t}{P(t,T)} = \mathrm{E}^{Q^T}[S_T|\F_t] . \] The first line says: discounting comes out of the expectation even when rates are stochastic — the great advantage of \(Q^T\). The second: forward prices are \(Q^T\)-martingales and unbiased \(Q^T\)-forecasts of the future spot. If \(F\) is lognormal under \(Q^T\) with volatility \(\sigma\), a call struck at \(K\) costs \(c = P(0,T)[F_0\Phi(d_1) - K\Phi(d_2)]\): Black's formula, now justified with stochastic rates.

Key Derivations

Discounted stock is a \(Q\)-martingale. Under \(Q\), \(\mathrm{d} S = rS\,\mathrm{d} t + \sigma S\,\mathrm{d} z^Q\). Apply Itô to \(G = e^{-rt}S\) (so \(G_t = -re^{-rt}S\), \(G_S = e^{-rt}\), \(G_{SS}=0\)): \[ \mathrm{d} G = \bigl(-re^{-rt}S + rS\,e^{-rt} + 0\bigr)\mathrm{d} t + \sigma S e^{-rt}\,\mathrm{d} z^Q = \sigma\,G\,\mathrm{d} z^Q . \] Zero drift \(\Rightarrow\) \(e^{-rt}S_t\) is a \(Q\)-martingale. (Same computation with any traded asset.)

\(F(t,T) = \mathrm{E}^{Q^T}[S_T|\F_t]\). \(F(t,T) = S_t/P(t,T)\) is a traded asset (\(S\)) divided by the numeraire of \(Q^T\); by the definition of \(Q^T\) it is a \(Q^T\)-martingale. Hence \[ F(t,T) = \mathrm{E}^{Q^T}[F(T,T)|\F_t] = \mathrm{E}^{Q^T}\Bigl[\frac{S_T}{P(T,T)}\Big|\F_t\Bigr] = \mathrm{E}^{Q^T}[S_T|\F_t]. \qquad\blacksquare \]

Exam Questions — What They Actually Ask

Exam Pattern
  • "Define a martingale. Show that the discounted stock price is a martingale under \(Q\)." (Itô on \(e^{-rt}S_t\))
  • "State Girsanov's theorem. What changes under an equivalent measure change, and what does not?" (drift changes, volatility doesn't)
  • "Define the \(T\)-forward measure and show \(F(t,T) = \mathrm{E}^{Q^T}[S_T|\F_t]\)."
  • "Explain the role of the numeraire; state the general pricing formula \(V_t/N_t = \mathrm{E}^{Q^N}[V_T/N_T|\F_t]\)."
  • "Use the forward measure to justify Black's formula when interest rates are stochastic."

Worked Example

June 2019, Question 2 --- Market price of risk and $f/g$ as a martingale

Question. Two non-dividend-paying securities \(f\) and \(g\) have volatilities \(\sigma_f\) and \(\sigma_g\) driven by the same Brownian motion \(z\). The market price of risk is \(\lambda = \sigma_g\).

(a) Write the full processes for \(f\) and \(g\) under \(P\).

(b) Apply Itô to find \(\mathrm{d}\ln f\) and \(\mathrm{d}\ln g\), then show \(\mathrm{d}\ln(f/g) = -\tfrac12(\sigma_f-\sigma_g)^2\,\mathrm{d} t + (\sigma_f-\sigma_g)\,\mathrm{d} z\).

(c) Hence apply Itô to \(f/g\) and show it has zero drift. Interpret.

Solution.

(a) From the MPR argument, \(\mathrm{d} f/f = (r + \sigma_g\sigma_f)\mathrm{d} t + \sigma_f\,\mathrm{d} z\) and \(\mathrm{d} g/g = (r + \sigma_g^2)\mathrm{d} t + \sigma_g\,\mathrm{d} z\).

(b) Itô on \(\ln f\): \(\mathrm{d}\ln f = (r + \sigma_g\sigma_f - \tfrac12\sigma_f^2)\mathrm{d} t + \sigma_f\,\mathrm{d} z\). Likewise \(\mathrm{d}\ln g = (r + \tfrac12\sigma_g^2)\mathrm{d} t + \sigma_g\,\mathrm{d} z\). Subtracting: \[ \mathrm{d}\ln\frac{f}{g} = \Bigl(\sigma_g\sigma_f - \tfrac12\sigma_f^2 - \tfrac12\sigma_g^2\Bigr)\mathrm{d} t + (\sigma_f - \sigma_g)\,\mathrm{d} z = -\tfrac12(\sigma_f-\sigma_g)^2\mathrm{d} t + (\sigma_f-\sigma_g)\,\mathrm{d} z. \]

(c) Apply Itô to \(X = e^{\ln(f/g)}\): the drift picks up \(+\tfrac12(\sigma_f-\sigma_g)^2\) which cancels the \(-\tfrac12(\sigma_f-\sigma_g)^2\) from (b), leaving: \[ \mathrm{d}\!\left(\frac{f}{g}\right) = (\sigma_f-\sigma_g)\frac{f}{g}\,\mathrm{d} z \qquad\text{(zero drift)}. \] So \(f/g\) is a martingale under \(P\) when \(\lambda=\sigma_g\). This is the change-of-numeraire theorem: \(f/g\) is a martingale under the probability measure where \(g\) serves as numeraire.

Key marks: (a) include the \(\lambda\sigma_i\) drift term; (b) subtract the two \(\mathrm{d}\ln\) equations carefully; (c) the exponential adds \(+\tfrac12(\cdots)^2\) which must cancel the negative term — show this explicitly.

Common Mistakes

Common Mistakes
  • "The stock price is a \(Q\)-martingale." False — the discounted stock price is. Under \(Q\) the stock itself drifts at \(r\).
  • Believing a measure change alters volatility. Girsanov moves drifts only; \(\sigma\) is common to \(P\), \(Q\) and \(Q^T\).
  • Interpreting \(F(t,T) = \mathrm{E}^{Q^T}[S_T]\) as a real-world forecast. The expectation is under the forward measure.
  • Using \(e^{-rT}\) discounting together with stochastic rates: with random \(r\) use \(V_t = P(t,T)\mathrm{E}^{Q^T}[V_T|\F_t]\) (discounting outside), not \(\mathrm{E}^Q\) with a constant discount factor.
9Interest Rate Derivatives: Caps, Floors, Swaptions (10/21 exams)

The Core Idea

A company with a floating-rate loan fears rising rates. A cap is the insurance product: at the end of every accrual period it reimburses the excess of the floating rate over a cap rate \(K\) — effectively a strip of call options on the interest rate, one per period (each called a caplet). A floor is the mirror image (puts on the rate, protecting a depositor against falling rates), and cap–floor parity mimics put–call parity: long cap \(-\) long floor \(=\) payer swap at rate \(K\).

There is a beautiful duality to internalise: an option on an interest rate is an option on a bond, inside out. Rates and bond prices move inversely (\(P = 1/(1+\delta L)\) over one period), so a caplet — a call on the rate — is equivalent to a put on a zero-coupon bond, and a floorlet to a call on a bond. Similarly, a swaption (an option to enter a swap at a fixed rate \(s_K\)) is an option on a coupon bond: a receiver swaption (right to receive fixed) pays off when rates have fallen, i.e.\ when the coupon bond is worth more than par — it is a call on the coupon bond struck at par; a payer swaption is the corresponding put. These translations matter because bond options are exactly what short-rate models (Section 10) can price in closed form.

For everyday quoting, the market bypasses full models and uses Black's formula on each caplet or swaption: assume the relevant forward rate (or forward swap rate) is lognormal under its natural forward measure, and plug into the Black-76 formula from Section 6. Section 12 (LMM) shows this practice is internally consistent.

Key Definitions and Notation

  • Tenor structure \(T_0 < T_1 < \dots < T_n\) with accrual factors \(\delta_k = T_{k+1}-T_k\) (e.g.\ 0.5 for semiannual). Notional \(N\) (set \(N=1\) below).
  • \(L_k = L(T_k, T_{k+1})\): the floating (LIBOR-type) simple rate fixed at \(T_k\) for the period \([T_k, T_{k+1}]\): \(1 + \delta_kL_k = 1/P(T_k,T_{k+1})\).
  • \(F_k(t)\): the forward rate seen at \(t\) for that period: \(F_k(t) = \dfrac{P(t,T_k) - P(t,T_{k+1})}{\delta_k\,P(t,T_{k+1})}\); note \(F_k(T_k) = L_k\).
  • Caplet \(k\): pays \(\delta_k(L_k - K)^+\) at \(T_{k+1}\) (fixed in advance at \(T_k\), paid in arrears). Cap = sum of caplets; floorlet/floor: \(\delta_k(K - L_k)^+\).
  • Swap rate \(s(t)\): the fixed rate making a swap over \([T_0,T_n]\) worth zero: \(s(t) = \bigl(P(t,T_0) - P(t,T_n)\bigr)/A(t)\), where \(A(t) = \sum_{k=1}^{n}\delta_{k-1}P(t,T_k)\) is the annuity (PV of 1 paid on each fixed date).
  • Payer swaption: right, at \(T_0\), to enter a swap paying fixed \(s_K\); receiver swaption: right to receive fixed \(s_K\).

Central Results

Black's formulas for caplets and swaptions (market standard)

Caplet \(k\) with cap rate \(K\), forward-rate volatility \(\sigma_k\): \[ \text{caplet}_k(0) = \delta_k\,P(0,T_{k+1})\,\bigl[F_k(0)\Phi(d_1) - K\Phi(d_2)\bigr], \qquad d_{1,2} = \frac{\ln(F_k(0)/K) \pm \tfrac12\sigma_k^2T_k}{\sigma_k\sqrt{T_k}} . \] (Floorlet: \(\delta_kP(0,T_{k+1})[K\Phi(-d_2) - F_k(0)\Phi(-d_1)]\).) Note the volatility accrues only to the fixing date \(T_k\), while discounting runs to the payment date \(T_{k+1}\).

Payer swaption with strike \(s_K\), forward swap-rate volatility \(\sigma\), exercise \(T_0\): \[ \text{PS}(0) = A(0)\,\bigl[s(0)\Phi(d_1) - s_K\Phi(d_2)\bigr], \qquad d_{1,2} = \frac{\ln(s(0)/s_K) \pm \tfrac12\sigma^2T_0}{\sigma\sqrt{T_0}} , \] and the receiver swaption is \(\text{RS}(0) = A(0)[s_K\Phi(-d_2) - s(0)\Phi(-d_1)]\). The annuity \(A(0)\) is the natural numeraire: under \(Q^A\) the swap rate \(s(t)\) is a martingale, which is what legitimises Black here.

The rate-option / bond-option dictionary

\[ \text{caplet } = (1+\delta_kK)\ \text{\textbf{puts} on } P(\cdot,T_{k+1})\ \text{with strike } \tfrac{1}{1+\delta_kK},\ \text{expiry } T_k ; \] \[ \text{floorlet } = (1+\delta_kK)\ \text{\textbf{calls} on the same bond, same strike} ; \] \[ \text{receiver swaption } = \text{\textbf{call} on } B_c \text{ struck at par (1)}; \] \[ \text{payer swaption } = \text{\textbf{put} on } B_c \text{ struck at par}, \] where \(B_c = \sum_{k=1}^n s_K\delta_{k-1}P(T_0,T_k) + P(T_0,T_n)\) is the coupon bond (fixed leg plus notional) valued at exercise \(T_0\). Cap–floor parity: \(\text{cap} - \text{floor} = \text{payer swap at rate }K\) (value \(= (\text{float leg}) - K\!\cdot\!A(0)\)).

Key Derivations

Caplet \(=\) bond put (the exam favourite).
  1. The caplet pays \(\delta_k(L_k - K)^+\) at \(T_{k+1}\). Discount it to the fixing date \(T_k\) with the just-fixed rate: value at \(T_k\) is \[ \frac{\delta_k(L_k-K)^+}{1+\delta_kL_k} . \]
  2. Substitute \(P := P(T_k,T_{k+1}) = \dfrac{1}{1+\delta_kL_k}\), i.e.\ \(\delta_kL_k = 1/P - 1\): \[ \frac{\delta_k(L_k-K)^+}{1+\delta_kL_k} = \bigl(1 - (1+\delta_kK)P\bigr)^+ = (1+\delta_kK)\Bigl(\frac{1}{1+\delta_kK} - P\Bigr)^{\!+}. \]
  3. This is the \(T_k\)-payoff of \((1+\delta_kK)\) European puts on the zero-coupon bond maturing at \(T_{k+1}\), struck at \(K^* = 1/(1+\delta_kK)\). The identical computation with \((K-L_k)^+\) shows a floorlet is \((1+\delta_kK)\) bond calls. Hence a floor is a portfolio of call options on zero-coupon bonds (and a cap a portfolio of puts).

Receiver swaption \(=\) call on a coupon bond. At exercise \(T_0\), receiving fixed \(s_K\) and paying float is worth \[ V(T_0) = \underbrace{\textstyle\sum_{k=1}^n s_K\delta_{k-1}P(T_0,T_k) + P(T_0,T_n)}_{\text{coupon bond } B_c\ (\text{fixed leg} + \text{notional})} - \underbrace{1}_{\text{float leg + notional}}, \] because a floating leg plus notional repayment always values to par at a reset date. The receiver swaption pays \((V(T_0))^+ = (B_c - 1)^+\): a call on the coupon bond struck at 1. Similarly the payer swaption pays \((1 - B_c)^+\), a put. This is exactly the payoff Jamshidian's trick (Section 10) decomposes into zero-coupon bond options.

Exam Questions — What They Actually Ask

Exam Pattern
  • "Define a caplet / cap and show a caplet can be priced with Black's formula" (lognormal \(F_k\) under \(Q^{T_{k+1}}\), discount from \(T_{k+1}\), vol to \(T_k\)).
  • "Show a cap (floor) is equivalent to a portfolio of puts (calls) on zero-coupon bonds." (the derivation above)
  • "Define payer and receiver swaptions and show the receiver swaption is a call on a coupon bond."
  • "State cap–floor parity."
  • "Why is the annuity the right numeraire for swaptions?" (\(s(t) = (P_0 - P_n)/A\) is a ratio of traded portfolios to \(A\))

Worked Example

June 2015, Question 3 --- Floor, floorlet as bond call, Black's formula

Question. A floor with principal \(N\), floor rate \(L_K\), and reset dates \(t_0

(a) Define a floorlet and describe all floor payments.

(b) Show that the floorlet for period \(k\) can be viewed as a European call on a zero-coupon bond.

(c) State the assumptions under which the Black formula \(\text{Floorlet}(0,k) = NP(0,t_{k+1})\tau_k[L_K\Phi(-d_2) - F_k\Phi(-d_1)]\) is valid.

Solution.

(a) The floorlet for period \(k\) is the single payment \(N\tau_k\max(L_K-L_k,0)\) at \(t_{k+1}\). The floor is the portfolio of all \(n\) such floorlets.

(b) The floorlet payoff at \(t_{k+1}\) can be rewritten (multiply/divide by \(1+L_k\tau_k = 1/P(t_k,t_{k+1})\)): \[ \frac{N\tau_k}{1+L_k\tau_k}\max(L_K-L_k,0) = \max\!\Bigl(N(1+L_K\tau_k)\underbrace{\frac{1}{1+L_k\tau_k}}_{=P(t_k,t_{k+1})} - N,\;0\Bigr). \] This is the payoff of a call at \(t_k\) on a zero-coupon bond paying \(N(1+L_K\tau_k)\) at \(t_{k+1}\), with strike \(N\).

(c) The formula is valid under the assumption that the forward rate \(F_k\) (the expectation of \(L_k\) under the \(t_{k+1}\)-forward measure \(Q^{k+1}\)) is log-normal: \(\ln F_k \sim N(\ln F_k - \tfrac12\sigma_k^2 t_k,\,\sigma_k^2 t_k)\). Under \(Q^{k+1}\), \(F_k\) is a martingale (\(\mathrm{E}^{k+1}[L_k] = F_k\)). Then: \[ d_1 = \frac{\ln(F_k/L_K) + \tfrac12\sigma_k^2 t_k}{\sigma_k\sqrt{t_k}}, \qquad d_2 = d_1 - \sigma_k\sqrt{t_k}. \]

Key marks: (b) the algebra converting the floorlet payoff to a bond call with specific coupon/notional; (c) naming the forward measure \(Q^{k+1}\) and the log-normality assumption for \(F_k\) under that measure.

Common Mistakes

Common Mistakes
  • Using \(T_{k+1}\) in \(d_{1,2}\): the option is decided at the fixing date, so the variance is \(\sigma_k^2T_k\). Discounting, however, goes to the payment date \(T_{k+1}\).
  • Swapping the dictionary: caplet \(=\) bond put, floorlet \(=\) bond call; receiver swaption \(=\) call on the coupon bond. Remember: options that pay when rates fall are bond calls.
  • Forgetting the accrual factor \(\delta_k\) in the payoff and in Black's formula.
  • Valuing the floating leg by forecasting rates: at any reset date float leg \(+\) notional \(=\) par — no forecasting needed.
10Short-Rate Models: Vasicek, CIR, Ho–Lee, Hull–White (16/21 exams)

The Core Idea

To price bonds and bond options consistently we model the short rate \(r_t\) — the instantaneous interest rate — as a stochastic process, and derive every bond price from it: a \(T\)-bond is worth \(P(t,T) = \mathrm{E}^Q[e^{-\int_t^T r_s\mathrm{d} s}|\F_t]\), the expected discounted value of 1. Rates behave differently from stocks: they do not wander off to infinity but are pulled back to a long-run level by policy and macro forces. Hence the workhorse dynamics are mean-reverting: in the Vasicek model \(\mathrm{d} r = a(b-r)\mathrm{d} t + \sigma\mathrm{d} z\), the drift \(a(b-r)\) acts like a spring — when \(r\) is above the long-run level \(b\) it is pulled down at speed \(a\), and vice versa. The CIR model adds the realistic feature that volatility \(\sigma\sqrt r\) shrinks as rates approach zero, keeping rates nonnegative.

The pricing logic is a rerun of Black–Scholes with one crucial twist: the short rate is not a traded asset — you cannot buy "one unit of \(r\)". So instead of hedging a bond with the underlying, we hedge one bond with another bond of different maturity: both are driven by the same \(\mathrm{d} z\), so a suitable long–short combination is riskless. The market-price-of-risk argument of Section 5 then applies verbatim and delivers the bond-pricing PDE, in which the real-world drift is replaced by the risk-neutral drift \(m - \lambda s\) (\(\lambda\) = market price of interest-rate risk, not pinned down by no-arbitrage — exactly the non-traded case discussed earlier).

The reason Vasicek/CIR are beloved: the PDE has affine solutions \(P = A(t,T)e^{-B(t,T)r}\) — an exponential in \(r\) with deterministic coefficient functions. Substituting the guess turns the PDE into two ordinary differential equations for \(A\) and \(B\), solvable in closed form. One number (\(r_t\)) then generates the whole yield curve. Equilibrium models (Vasicek, CIR) fit that curve only approximately; no-arbitrage models (Ho–Lee, Hull–White) make a parameter time-dependent, \(\theta(t)\), and calibrate it to reproduce today's observed term structure exactly — essential when your job is to price derivatives relative to today's curve.

Key Definitions and Notation

  • \(r_t\): short rate; generic risk-neutral dynamics \(\mathrm{d} r = m(r,t)\,\mathrm{d} t + s(r,t)\,\mathrm{d} z^Q\).
  • \(P(r,t) = P(t,T)\): price of a zero-coupon bond paying 1 at \(T\), viewed as a function of the state \(r\) and time.
  • \(\lambda\): market price of interest-rate risk (real-world drift \(m^P\) relates via \(m = m^P - \lambda s\)).
  • Yield: \(R(t,T) = -\dfrac{\ln P(t,T)}{T-t}\); the yield curve is \(T\mapsto R(t,T)\). Instantaneous forward rate \(f(t,T) = -\partial_T\ln P(t,T)\).
