  • Vasicek: \(\mathrm{d} r = a(b - r)\mathrm{d} t + \sigma\mathrm{d} z^Q\) (\(a\) = mean-reversion speed, \(b\) = long-run level, \(\sigma\) = absolute vol).
  • CIR: \(\mathrm{d} r = a(b-r)\mathrm{d} t + \sigma\sqrt r\,\mathrm{d} z^Q\); Ho–Lee: \(\mathrm{d} r = \theta(t)\mathrm{d} t + \sigma\mathrm{d} z^Q\); Hull–White: \(\mathrm{d} r = (\theta(t) - ar)\mathrm{d} t + \sigma\mathrm{d} z^Q\) ("Vasicek with a time-dependent level").
  • Affine ansatz: \(P(t,T) = A(t,T)\,e^{-B(t,T)\,r_t}\) with terminal conditions \(A(T,T)=1\), \(B(T,T)=0\) (so that \(P(T,T)=1\)).

Central Results

The bond-pricing (term-structure) PDE

For risk-neutral short-rate dynamics \(\mathrm{d} r = m\,\mathrm{d} t + s\,\mathrm{d} z^Q\), every interest-rate claim \(P(r,t)\) satisfies \[ \frac{\partial P}{\partial t} + m\,\frac{\partial P}{\partial r} + \tfrac12 s^2\frac{\partial^2P}{\partial r^2} = rP , \] with \(P(r,T) = 1\) for a zero-coupon bond. (In real-world terms \(m = m^P - \lambda s\): the market price of risk enters exactly because \(r\) is not traded.) Same structure as BSM — only the state variable and the boundary condition changed.

Vasicek bond price

\(P(t,T) = A(t,T)e^{-B(t,T)r_t}\) with \(\tau = T-t\) and \[ B(t,T) = \frac{1 - e^{-a\tau}}{a}, \qquad \ln A(t,T) = \frac{\bigl(B(t,T) - \tau\bigr)\bigl(a^2b - \sigma^2/2\bigr)}{a^2} - \frac{\sigma^2B(t,T)^2}{4a} . \] Yields are affine in \(r\): \(R(t,T) = -\frac{\ln A}{\tau} + \frac{B}{\tau}r_t\); as \(\tau\to\infty\), \(R\to b - \frac{\sigma^2}{2a^2}\) (a constant long yield). Bond volatility is \(\sigma_P = \sigma B(t,T)\) (from Itô: \(\partial P/\partial r = -BP\)). Curve shapes: increasing, decreasing or humped — but rates can go negative (normal model).

CIR, Ho--Lee, Hull--White in one view

CIR: also affine, \(P = Ae^{-Br}\) with \[ B(t,T) = \frac{2(e^{\gamma\tau}-1)}{(\gamma+a)(e^{\gamma\tau}-1) + 2\gamma}, \qquad \gamma = \sqrt{a^2 + 2\sigma^2}, \] and \(A = \Bigl[\frac{2\gamma e^{(a+\gamma)\tau/2}}{(\gamma+a)(e^{\gamma\tau}-1)+2\gamma}\Bigr]^{2ab/\sigma^2}\). The \(\sqrt r\) diffusion keeps \(r\ge0\) (strictly positive if \(2ab\ge\sigma^2\)).

Ho–Lee (first no-arbitrage model): \(\theta(t) = \dfrac{\partial f(0,t)}{\partial t} + \sigma^2t\) — the drift tracks the slope of today's forward curve, so the model reproduces the observed term structure exactly; but no mean reversion and \(r\) can go negative.

Hull–White: mean reversion and exact fit: \(\theta(t) = \dfrac{\partial f(0,t)}{\partial t} + af(0,t) + \dfrac{\sigma^2}{2a}(1-e^{-2at})\); bond prices again affine with the Vasicek \(B\).

Jamshidian's trick (options on coupon bonds)

In any one-factor model where bond prices are decreasing in \(r\): to price a European option (expiry \(T\), strike \(K\)) on a coupon bond \(B_c(r) = \sum_i c_i\,P(T,s_i;r)\):

  1. Find \(r^*\) solving \(B_c(r^*) = K\) (one equation, monotone — unique root).
  2. Set \(K_i = P(T,s_i;r^*)\), so \(\sum_i c_iK_i = K\).
  3. Because all bond prices fall together as \(r\) rises, \(B_c(r) > K \iff r < r^* \iff P(T,s_i;r) > K_i\) for every \(i\). Hence \[ \bigl(B_c(r) - K\bigr)^+ = \sum_i c_i\bigl(P(T,s_i;r) - K_i\bigr)^+ : \] the coupon-bond option equals a portfolio of zero-coupon bond options, each priced in closed form. Combined with Section 9 this prices swaptions analytically in Vasicek/CIR/Hull–White.

Key Derivations

Deriving the bond PDE by hedging bond against bond: take two bonds \(P_1 = P(t,T_1)\), \(P_2 = P(t,T_2)\). Itô gives \(\mathrm{d} P_i = \mu_iP_i\mathrm{d} t + \sigma_iP_i\mathrm{d} z\) with \(\mu_iP_i = \partial_tP_i + m^P\partial_rP_i + \tfrac12s^2\partial_{rr}P_i\) and \(\sigma_iP_i = s\,\partial_rP_i\). The two-asset MPR argument of Section 5 forces \((\mu_i - r)/\sigma_i = \lambda\), common to all maturities. Substitute \(\mu_i = r + \lambda\sigma_i\) back: \[ \partial_tP + m^P\partial_rP + \tfrac12s^2\partial_{rr}P = rP + \lambda s\,\partial_rP \;\Longrightarrow\; \partial_tP + (m^P - \lambda s)\partial_rP + \tfrac12s^2\partial_{rr}P = rP . \]

Vasicek: from PDE to \(A\) and \(B\) (separation of variables) — see the worked example for the full computation. The skeleton: substitute \(P = Ae^{-Br}\); divide by \(P\); the equation is affine in \(r\); since it must hold for all \(r\), the coefficient of \(r\) and the constant term must vanish separately, giving \[ \frac{\partial B}{\partial t} = aB - 1 \quad (B(T,T)=0), \qquad \frac{1}{A}\frac{\partial A}{\partial t} = abB - \tfrac12\sigma^2B^2 \quad (A(T,T)=1), \] two ODEs solved by the boxed formulas.

Exam Questions — What They Actually Ask

Exam Pattern
  • "In the Vasicek model \(\mathrm{d} r = a(b-r)\mathrm{d} t + \sigma\mathrm{d} z\): (a) interpret the parameters; (b) derive the PDE for \(P(r,t)\); (c) show \(P = A e^{-Br}\) solves it and find \(A,B\)."
  • "Same for CIR; compare Vasicek and CIR" (negative rates vs.\ \(\sqrt r\) vol; both affine).
  • "Explain the market price of risk term in the bond PDE" (\(r\) non-traded — Section 5).
  • "Find the process of the yield \(R(t,T) = -\ln P/(T-t)\) using Itô" (Section 4 toolbox).
  • "What distinguishes equilibrium from no-arbitrage models? How is Ho–Lee/Hull–White calibrated to the initial term structure?"
  • "Explain Jamshidian's trick and what it is used for." (coupon-bond options \(\to\) ZCB options; prices swaptions)

Worked Example

June 2019, Question 1 --- Vasicek: affine bond price and Itô dynamics

Question. In Vasicek's model the risk-neutral short rate follows \(\mathrm{d} r = a(b-r)\mathrm{d} t + \sigma\mathrm{d} z\). The zero-coupon bond price is \(P(t,T) = A(t,T)e^{-B(t,T)r(t)}\).

(a) Characterise the model qualitatively.

(b) Show that \(\partial P/\partial r = -B(t,T)P(t,T)\) and interpret \(B(t,T)\).

(c) Use Itô to derive \(\mathrm{d} P = r(t)P\,\mathrm{d} t - \sigma B(t,T)P\,\mathrm{d} z\).

Solution.

(a) The short rate mean-reverts to \(b\) at speed \(a\): if \(r>b\) the drift pulls it down, and vice versa. The term \(\sigma\mathrm{d} z\) adds normally distributed shocks, so \(r\) can go negative (a tractable drawback). The model yields closed-form bond prices (affine in \(r\)) and Jamshidian's formula for coupon-bond options.

(b) Differentiating \(P = Ae^{-Br}\) with respect to \(r(t)\): \[ \frac{\partial P}{\partial r} = A\cdot(-B)e^{-Br} = -B\,P(t,T). \] \(B(t,T)\) is the duration of the bond: a 1-unit rise in \(r\) reduces \(\ln P\) by \(B\), so longer bonds (large \(B\)) are more rate-sensitive.

(c) Apply Itô to \(P = f(r,t)\): \[ \mathrm{d} P = \Bigl(\partial_t P + a(b-r)\partial_r P + \tfrac12\sigma^2\partial_{rr}P\Bigr)\mathrm{d} t + \sigma\partial_r P\,\mathrm{d} z. \] Substituting \(\partial_r P = -BP\): the \(\mathrm{d} z\) coefficient is \(-\sigma BP\). Under \(Q\) the drift of a traded bond must equal \(rP\); accepting this (it pins down \(A\) and \(B\) via ODEs), we get \(\mathrm{d} P = rP\,\mathrm{d} t - \sigma B P\,\mathrm{d} z\).

Key marks: (a) mean reversion description and negative-rate possibility; (b) chain rule on \(Ae^{-Br}\), plus interpretation as duration; (c) write all three Itô terms, substitute \(\partial_r P = -BP\), invoke risk-neutral drift condition.

Common Mistakes

Common Mistakes
  • Writing the PDE right-hand side as \(rP\) but then treating \(r\) as a constant when grouping — \(r\) is the state variable; the whole point is matching its coefficients.
  • Losing the minus sign in \(\partial P/\partial r = -BP\) (and hence the sign of the \(a(b-r)B\) term).
  • Quoting Vasicek's \(B\) with \(T-t\) and \(t-T\) confused: \(B\ge0\) always; check \(B(T,T)=0\).
  • Saying no-arbitrage determines \(\lambda\): with a non-traded short rate it does not; \(\lambda\) is exogenous/calibrated (compare Section 5).
  • Mixing up model classes: Vasicek/CIR = equilibrium (fit curve approximately), Ho–Lee/Hull–White = no-arbitrage (fit exactly via \(\theta(t)\)).
11The HJM Framework (7/21 exams)

The Core Idea

Short-rate models compress the entire yield curve into one number, \(r_t\) — efficient, but restrictive. Heath, Jarrow and Morton flipped the perspective: model the whole forward curve at once. The state variable is the instantaneous forward rate \(f(t,T)\) — the rate you can lock in at time \(t\) for borrowing at the future instant \(T\) — and every maturity \(T\) gets its own stochastic differential equation: \[ \mathrm{d} f(t,T) = \alpha(t,T)\,\mathrm{d} t + \sigma(t,T)\,\mathrm{d} z . \] Think of the forward curve as a guitar string vibrating in time: HJM lets you specify how each point of the string jiggles.

The punchline — and the exam question — is that you are not free to choose the drift \(\alpha\). Bond prices are traded assets, and under the risk-neutral measure every traded asset must drift at the short rate \(r\). Since bonds are (exponentials of integrals of) forward rates, this discipline propagates back to the forward-rate drifts and forces \[ \alpha(t,T) = \sigma(t,T)\int_t^T\sigma(t,u)\,\mathrm{d} u . \] Volatility determines drift. Once you pick the volatility structure \(\sigma(t,T)\), the model is complete: there is nothing else to specify (and today's curve \(f(0,T)\) is an input, so calibration to the observed term structure is automatic). Every no-arbitrage short-rate model is a special case: Ho–Lee is HJM with \(\sigma(t,T) = \sigma\) constant, Hull–White is HJM with \(\sigma(t,T) = \sigma e^{-a(T-t)}\).

Key Definitions and Notation

  • \(f(t,T)\): instantaneous forward rate at time \(t\) for date \(T\); linked to bond prices by \[ f(t,T) = -\frac{\partial\ln P(t,T)}{\partial T} \qquad\Longleftrightarrow\qquad P(t,T) = \exp\Bigl(-\int_t^Tf(t,u)\,\mathrm{d} u\Bigr). \]
  • Short rate: \(r_t = f(t,t)\) (the forward rate for "right now").
  • \(\alpha(t,T),\ \sigma(t,T)\): drift and volatility of the forward rate for maturity \(T\) (under \(Q\) unless stated otherwise).
  • \(\Sigma(t,T) := \displaystyle\int_t^T\sigma(t,u)\,\mathrm{d} u\): the volatility of the bond \(P(t,T)\) (up to sign), the accumulated forward-rate vol.

Central Results

The HJM drift condition

Under the risk-neutral measure \(Q\), absence of arbitrage forces \[ \alpha(t,T) = \sigma(t,T)\int_t^T\sigma(t,u)\,\mathrm{d} u = \sigma(t,T)\,\Sigma(t,T), \] and the bond price dynamics are \(\dfrac{\mathrm{d} P(t,T)}{P(t,T)} = r_t\,\mathrm{d} t - \Sigma(t,T)\,\mathrm{d} z^Q\). Shape: the drift is "volatility times cumulative volatility" — a convexity compensation that keeps discounted bond prices driftless. Consequences: (i) an HJM model is fully specified by \(\sigma(t,T)\) and the initial curve \(f(0,\cdot)\); (ii) general HJM is non-Markovian (the short rate alone does not summarise the state) — one reason practitioners moved on to the LMM.

Special cases (checkable)

Constant \(\sigma(t,T) = \sigma\): \(\alpha = \sigma^2(T-t)\), and \(r_t = f(0,t) + \sigma^2t^2/2 + \sigma z_t\) — the Ho–Lee model with its calibrated drift \(\theta(t) = \partial_Tf(0,t) + \sigma^2t\). Exponentially damped \(\sigma(t,T) = \sigma e^{-a(T-t)}\) reproduces Hull–White.

Key Derivations

Deriving the drift condition (the whole exam question; also the worked example below, with all steps):
  1. Write \(\ln P(t,T) = -\int_t^Tf(t,u)\,\mathrm{d} u\) and differentiate in \(t\). Two effects: the lower limit moves (releasing \(f(t,t) = r_t\)) and each \(f(t,u)\) diffuses: \[ \mathrm{d}\ln P(t,T) = r_t\,\mathrm{d} t - \int_t^T\mathrm{d} f(t,u)\,\mathrm{d} u = \Bigl(r_t - \int_t^T\alpha(t,u)\mathrm{d} u\Bigr)\mathrm{d} t - \Sigma(t,T)\,\mathrm{d} z . \]
  2. Convert to \(\mathrm{d} P/P\) with Itô's lemma in reverse (\(P = e^{\ln P}\) adds \(+\tfrac12(\text{vol})^2\)): \[ \frac{\mathrm{d} P}{P} = \Bigl(r_t - \int_t^T\alpha(t,u)\mathrm{d} u + \tfrac12\Sigma(t,T)^2\Bigr)\mathrm{d} t - \Sigma(t,T)\,\mathrm{d} z . \]
  3. Under \(Q\), the traded bond must drift at \(r_t\); the bracket must equal \(r_t\) for every \(T\): \[ \int_t^T\alpha(t,u)\,\mathrm{d} u = \tfrac12\Sigma(t,T)^2 . \]
  4. Differentiate with respect to \(T\) (fundamental theorem of calculus on the left, chain rule on the right): \[ \alpha(t,T) = \Sigma(t,T)\,\frac{\partial\Sigma(t,T)}{\partial T} = \sigma(t,T)\int_t^T\sigma(t,u)\,\mathrm{d} u . \qquad\blacksquare \]

Exam Questions — What They Actually Ask

Exam Pattern
  • "In the HJM framework \(\mathrm{d} f(t,T) = \alpha(t,T)\mathrm{d} t + \sigma(t,T)\mathrm{d} z\), derive the no-arbitrage drift condition." (steps 1–4 above)
  • "Explain the relation between \(f(t,T)\) and \(P(t,T)\), and identify the bond's volatility." (\(-\Sigma(t,T)\))
  • "Show that \(\sigma(t,T) = \sigma\) constant reproduces Ho–Lee" (compute \(\alpha = \sigma^2(T-t)\) and integrate).
  • "What are the advantages/drawbacks of HJM versus short-rate models?" (exact fit + flexible vol vs.\ non-Markovian, unobservable instantaneous forwards)

Worked Example

August 2019, Question 2 --- HJM drift condition and the Ho--Lee special case

Question. In HJM, instantaneous forward rates satisfy \(\mathrm{d} f(t,T) = m(t,T)\mathrm{d} t + s(t,T)\mathrm{d} z\).

(a) State the no-arbitrage drift condition and comment; specify the probability measure.

(b) The short rate in HJM is in general non-Markov. Explain what this means and why it is a computational complication.

(c) For constant volatility \(s(t,T) = s\), derive the process for \(f(t,T)\) and comment on the distribution.

Solution.

(a) Under risk-neutral \(Q\): \[ m(t,T) = s(t,T)\int_t^T s(t,u)\,\mathrm{d} u. \] The volatility structure fully determines the drift — once \(s(t,T)\) and the initial curve \(f(0,T)\) are given, the model is complete. Derived by requiring that every zero-coupon bond drifts at \(r_t\) under \(Q\) (apply Itô to \(\ln P = -\int_t^T f(t,u)\,\mathrm{d} u\) and match the drift).

(b) Non-Markov means the current value \(r_t = f(t,t)\) alone does not determine the future distribution of \(r\); the full history of forward-rate realizations is required. In practice this prevents recombining trees, so only Monte Carlo (path-by-path simulation) is feasible, at high computational cost.

(c) With \(s(t,T)=s\): drift \(m(t,T) = s^2(T-t)\). Integrating: \[ f(t,T) = f(0,T) + s^2\!\int_0^t\!(T-u)\,\mathrm{d} u + sz_t = f(0,T) + \tfrac12 s^2(2Tt - t^2) + sz_t. \] Setting \(T=t\): \(r_t = f(0,t) + \tfrac12 s^2 t^2 + sz_t\), so \(\mathrm{d} r = (\partial_t f(0,t) + s^2 t)\mathrm{d} t + s\,\mathrm{d} z\) — exactly Ho–Lee. Forward rates are normally distributed: positive probability of negative rates.

Key marks: (a) write the exact drift identity; state it holds under \(Q\); sketch the derivation via Itô on \(\ln P\); (b) definition of Markov and path-dependence prevents trees; (c) integrate up \(m\) and \(s\,\mathrm{d} z\), set \(T=t\) to recover Ho–Lee.

Common Mistakes

Common Mistakes
  • Forgetting the boundary term \(r_t\,\mathrm{d} t\) when differentiating \(\int_t^Tf(t,u)\mathrm{d} u\) with respect to \(t\) — the moving lower limit is not optional.
  • Omitting the Itô \(+\tfrac12\Sigma^2\) when exponentiating \(\ln P\), which destroys the drift condition.
  • Writing the drift condition as \(\alpha = \tfrac12\Sigma^2\) (that is the integrated statement); the pointwise condition has \(\sigma(t,T)\) times the integral.
  • Saying HJM "needs to be calibrated to the initial curve": the initial curve is an input; only the vol structure is a modelling choice.
12The LIBOR Market Model (6/21 exams)

The Core Idea

HJM works with instantaneous forward rates — mathematically elegant, but nobody quotes them. Markets quote discrete rates: 3- or 6-month LIBOR-type forwards, cap prices in Black volatilities. The LIBOR Market Model (BGM) models exactly what is quoted: each discrete forward rate \(F_k(t)\) for the accrual period \([T_k,T_{k+1}]\) gets its own lognormal dynamics.

The key insight comes straight from the numeraire machinery of Section 8: the forward rate \(F_k(t)\) is, by construction, a ratio of traded portfolios to the bond \(P(t,T_{k+1})\): \[ F_k(t) = \frac{P(t,T_k) - P(t,T_{k+1})}{\delta_k\,P(t,T_{k+1})}. \] So under the forward measure \(Q^{k+1}\) (numeraire \(P(\cdot,T_{k+1})\)), \(F_k\) is automatically a martingale — no drift to derive, no drift condition to remember. Give it lognormal volatility, \(\mathrm{d} F_k = \sigma_k F_k\,\mathrm{d} z^{k+1}\), and each caplet is priced by Black's formula exactly. This is the theoretical vindication of what traders had been doing all along: the market convention of quoting caps in Black vols is internally consistent. The price of this convenience: each rate is driftless only under its own measure; to simulate all rates together under a single measure, the "foreign" rates pick up drift corrections — computed, once again, with Girsanov.

Key Definitions and Notation

  • Tenor grid \(T_0\(F_k(t)\): simple forward rate for \([T_k,T_{k+1}]\), defined by \(1+\delta_kF_k(t) = P(t,T_k)/P(t,T_{k+1})\); fixes at \(T_k\) into spot rate \(L_k\).
  • \(Q^{k+1}\): the \(T_{k+1}\)-forward measure, numeraire \(P(\cdot,T_{k+1})\); \(z^{k+1}\): its Brownian motion.
  • \(\sigma_k(t)\): (Black) volatility of \(F_k\); flat-vol vs.\ spot-vol quoting for caps; the model is also the basis for swaption approximation formulas.

Central Results

LMM dynamics and cap pricing

Under its own forward measure each forward rate is a driftless lognormal: \[ \mathrm{d} F_k(t) = \sigma_k(t)\,F_k(t)\,\mathrm{d} z^{k+1}, \qquad t\le T_k . \] Caplet \(k\) is then exactly Black: \[ \text{caplet}_k(0) = \delta_kP(0,T_{k+1})\bigl[F_k(0)\Phi(d_1) - K\Phi(d_2)\bigr], \qquad d_{1,2} = \frac{\ln(F_k(0)/K) \pm \tfrac12\bar\sigma_k^2T_k}{\bar\sigma_k\sqrt{T_k}}, \] where \(\bar\sigma_k^2 = \frac{1}{T_k}\int_0^{T_k}\sigma_k(t)^2\mathrm{d} t\) is the average squared vol to the fixing date. A cap is the sum of its caplets (each with its own \(F_k(0)\), discount factor and vol). Calibration: choose \(\sigma_k(\cdot)\) to reproduce quoted cap/caplet Black vols — by construction a perfect fit is possible.

Changing measure between rates

Under \(Q^{k+1}\), a different rate \(F_j\) (\(j\neq k\)) is not driftless. Girsanov gives (one-factor case), e.g.\ under the terminal or rolling measure, drift terms of the form \[ \mathrm{d} F_k = F_k\,\sigma_k\Bigl(\textstyle\sum_{j}\pm\dfrac{\delta_jF_j\sigma_j}{1+\delta_jF_j}\Bigr)\mathrm{d} t + \sigma_kF_k\,\mathrm{d} z , \] summing over the periods between the rate's own measure and the chosen common measure. You need not memorise the exact sum — exams ask you to explain that a common measure induces these drift corrections and that they make the joint model tractable only by simulation (Monte Carlo). Swaptions in the LMM require an approximation (e.g.\ Rebonato's formula relating swap-rate vol to forward-rate vols and correlations).

Key Derivations

\(F_k\) is a \(Q^{k+1}\)-martingale (the exam's part (b)):
  1. By definition of the forward measure, for any traded asset or portfolio \(X_t\), the ratio \(X_t/P(t,T_{k+1})\) is a \(Q^{k+1}\)-martingale.
  2. Take the traded portfolio \(X_t = \dfrac{1}{\delta_k}\bigl(P(t,T_k) - P(t,T_{k+1})\bigr)\) — long \(1/\delta_k\) of the \(T_k\)-bond, short \(1/\delta_k\) of the \(T_{k+1}\)-bond.
  3. Then \(X_t/P(t,T_{k+1}) = \dfrac{P(t,T_k)-P(t,T_{k+1})}{\delta_kP(t,T_{k+1})} = F_k(t)\). A ratio of a traded portfolio to the numeraire: martingale. \(\blacksquare\)
  4. Consequently \(F_k(0) = \mathrm{E}^{k+1}[F_k(T_k)] = \mathrm{E}^{k+1}[L_k]\), and with lognormal dynamics the caplet expectation \(\delta_kP(0,T_{k+1})\mathrm{E}^{k+1}[(L_k - K)^+]\) evaluates to Black's formula (same lognormal integral as BSM).

Exam Questions — What They Actually Ask

Exam Pattern
  • "Define the LIBOR forward rate \(F_k(t)\) in terms of bond prices."
  • "Show that \(F_k\) is a martingale under \(Q^{k+1}\)." (steps 1–3 above — short but must mention "traded portfolio over numeraire")
  • "Hence price a caplet / a cap using Black's formula; why is this exact in the LMM?"
  • "Why do drift terms appear when all rates are modelled under one measure? How are caps used to calibrate the model?"
  • "Compare LMM and HJM." (observable discrete rates + market-consistent Black quotes vs.\ instantaneous rates; both fit the initial curve; LMM needs simulation for joint dynamics)

Worked Example

August 2023, Question 3 --- LMM: forward rate, martingale, and rolling numeraire

Question. Let \(P(t,t_k)\) be zero-coupon bond prices and \(F_k(t)\) the simple forward rate for \([t_k,t_{k+1}]\) with compounding period \(\tau_k\).

(a) Express \(F_k(t)\) in terms of bond prices.

(b) What choice of numeraire makes \(F_k\) a martingale? Write its lognormal dynamics under that measure.

(c) In the full LMM a rolling numeraire is used. Explain in general terms how this works and why drift corrections arise.

Solution.

(a) By the arbitrage relationship \(1 + \tau_k F_k(t) = P(t,t_k)/P(t,t_{k+1})\), so: \[ F_k(t) = \frac{P(t,t_k)-P(t,t_{k+1})}{\tau_k P(t,t_{k+1})}. \] \(F_k\) is the rate at which the \(t_k\)-bond trades forward to \(t_{k+1}\).

(b) The numeraire that makes \(F_k\) driftless is \(P(\cdot,t_{k+1})\) — the \(t_{k+1}\)-forward measure \(Q^{k+1}\). Under \(Q^{k+1}\), \(F_k(t)/P(t,t_{k+1})\) is a ratio of a traded portfolio to the numeraire, hence a martingale. Assigning lognormal volatility \(\zeta_k\): \[ \mathrm{d} F_k(t) = \zeta_k(t)F_k(t)\,\mathrm{d} z^{k+1}, \qquad t \le t_k. \] This gives Black's formula for caplet \(k\) exactly: \(\text{caplet}_k = \tau_k P(0,t_{k+1})[F_k(0)\Phi(d_1) - K\Phi(d_2)]\).

(c) In the full LMM all forward rates are simulated under one common measure (rolling or terminal). When \(F_k\) is expressed under a measure other than \(Q^{k+1}\), Girsanov's theorem introduces drift corrections: each \(F_k\) acquires drift terms involving \(\sum_j \pm\delta_j F_j\zeta_j/(1+\delta_j F_j)\). These make the joint dynamics non-trivial; Monte Carlo is required. Swaption pricing needs an approximation (e.g.\ Rebonato's formula) since no single measure makes the swap rate lognormal.

Key marks: (a) the bond-ratio formula; (b) explicitly name \(Q^{k+1}\), driftless lognormal dynamics, Black's formula for the caplet; (c) change-of-numeraire induces drifts; Monte Carlo needed; swaptions require approximation.

Common Mistakes

Common Mistakes
  • Claiming \(F_k\) is a martingale under \(Q\) (risk-neutral) or under \(Q^{k}\): it is driftless only under \(Q^{k+1}\), the measure of its payment-date bond.
  • Using a single measure for all rates without acknowledging drift corrections.
  • Vol horizon: variance runs to the fixing date \(T_k\); a caplet on a rate fixed at \(1\) and paid at \(1.5\) has \(\sigma^2\times1\), not \(\sigma^2\times1.5\).
13Credit Risk: Hazard Rates, CDS, Merton, Copulas (15/21 exams)

The Core Idea

Everything so far assumed promises are kept. Credit risk asks: what if the counterparty defaults? The workhorse of reduced-form modelling is the hazard rate \(\lambda(t)\) — the instantaneous "mortality rate" of a firm: given survival to \(t\), the probability of default in the next instant \(\mathrm{d} t\) is \(\lambda(t)\mathrm{d} t\). Exactly like radioactive decay or actuarial mortality, this produces exponential survival: the probability of surviving from \(t\) to \(T\) is \(V(t,T) = e^{-\int_t^T\lambda(s)\mathrm{d} s}\). A defaultable bond then discounts at "\(r+\lambda\)" instead of \(r\) — the credit spread is (approximately) the hazard rate times the loss given default: \(s \approx \lambda(1-R)\). That one approximation unlocks half the numerical questions.

The traded instrument is the credit default swap (CDS): insurance on a bond. The protection buyer pays a periodic premium (the spread \(s\)) until default or maturity, whichever comes first; if default occurs, the seller compensates the loss \(1-R\) (notional minus recovery). Pricing is pure no-arbitrage bookkeeping: set (PV of expected premiums) \(=\) (PV of expected protection payment) and solve for \(s\). In the standard notation, with \(A\) = PV of premium payments per unit spread, \(B\) = PV of the accrual paid at default per unit spread, \(C\) = PV of the protection leg, the fair spread is \(s = C/(A+B)\).

The competing structural view (Merton) explains why firms default: a firm's equity is a call option on its assets. Shareholders own whatever asset value exceeds the debt \(D\) due at \(T\) — payoff \((V_T - D)^+\) — and hand over the firm otherwise (limited liability = the option's "walk away" feature). BSM then prices equity and debt off the firm's asset value and volatility, delivering default probabilities and credit spreads from balance-sheet data. Finally, portfolio credit products (CDOs) need default correlation; the market standard is the Gaussian copula, which couples individual default times through correlated normal factors.

Key Definitions and Notation

  • \(\tau\): (random) default time; \(V(t,T)\): survival probability from \(t\) to \(T\) (some texts write \(Q(\tau>T)\)).
  • \(\lambda(t)\): hazard rate (default intensity): \(\Pr(\tau\le t+\mathrm{d} t\,|\,\tau>t) = \lambda(t)\,\mathrm{d} t\).
  • \(R\): recovery rate (fraction of notional recovered at default); \(1-R\): loss given default (LGD).
  • CDS legs (per unit notional, spread \(s\), payment dates \(t_1,\dots,t_n\), accruals \(\delta_i\)): premium leg \(s\cdot A\), accrual-at-default leg \(s\cdot B\), protection leg \(C\).
  • Merton: \(V_t\) = firm asset value (GBM with vol \(\sigma_V\)), \(D\) = face value of zero-coupon debt due \(T\); \(E_t\) = equity value; \(D_t\) = debt value \(= V_t - E_t\); credit spread = defaultable yield \(-\) \(r\).
  • Risk-neutral (\(Q\)) vs.\ real-world (\(P\)) default probabilities: \(Q\)-probabilities are backed out of market spreads and exceed \(P\)-probabilities (historical default rates) because they absorb risk premia. Use \(Q\) for pricing, \(P\) for risk management.
  • Gaussian copula: \(x_i = \sqrt{\rho}\,M + \sqrt{1-\rho}\,Z_i\) with common factor \(M\) and idiosyncratic \(Z_i\) (all \(N(0,1)\)); firm \(i\) defaults by \(T\) iff \(x_i < \Phi^{-1}(p)\), \(p\) = marginal default probability.

Central Results

Survival probability and defaultable bonds

\[ V(t,T) = \exp\Bigl(-\int_t^T\lambda(s)\,\mathrm{d} s\Bigr) \qquad(\text{constant }\lambda:\ V = e^{-\lambda(T-t)}). \] Zero-recovery defaultable ZCB: \(\tilde P(t,T) = P(t,T)\,V(t,T) = e^{-\int(r+\lambda)\mathrm{d} s}\): default risk acts as an extra discount rate. Hence yield spread \(\approx \lambda\) (zero recovery) or, with recovery \(R\), \[ s \approx \lambda\,(1-R) \qquad\Longleftrightarrow\qquad \lambda \approx \frac{s}{1-R}\quad(\text{back out intensities from spreads}). \]

CDS spread

Fair spread equates legs at inception: \(s\,(A + B) = C\), i.e. \[ s = \frac{C}{A+B}, \qquad \begin{aligned} A &= \textstyle\sum_i \delta_i\,P(0,t_i)\,V(0,t_i) && \text{(premiums paid while alive)}\\ B &= \textstyle\sum_i \tfrac{\delta_i}{2}\,P(0,\bar t_i)\,\Delta V_i && \text{(accrual at mid-period default)}\\ C &= (1-R)\textstyle\sum_i P(0,\bar t_i)\,\Delta V_i && \text{(protection: LGD)} \end{aligned} \] with \(\bar t_i\) the mid-point of period \(i\) (a common discretisation of default timing) and \(\Delta V_i := V(0,t_{i-1}) - V(0,t_i)\) = probability of defaulting in period \(i\). Flat hazard, ignoring discounting and accrual, collapses to \(s \approx (1-R)\lambda\).

Merton model

Equity is a call on firm value: \(E_0 = V_0\Phi(d_1) - De^{-rT}\Phi(d_2)\), with \[ d_1 = \frac{\ln(V_0/D) + (r+\sigma_V^2/2)T}{\sigma_V\sqrt T}, \qquad d_2 = d_1 - \sigma_V\sqrt T . \] Risk-neutral default probability \(= Q(V_T < D) = \Phi(-d_2)\). Debt value \(D_0 = V_0 - E_0\); the promised yield is \(y = \frac1T\ln(D/D_0)\) and the credit spread is \(y - r\). (\(V_0\) and \(\sigma_V\) are unobservable; in practice solved from observed \(E_0\) and equity vol via \(E_0\)'s formula and \(\sigma_EE_0 = \Phi(d_1)\sigma_VV_0\).) Debt can equivalently be read as riskless debt minus a put on firm value struck at \(D\) — the put premium is the credit spread's source.

Gaussian copula and CDOs (concepts to reproduce)

Conditional on the market factor \(M\), defaults are independent with probability \[ p(M) = \Phi\!\Bigl(\frac{\Phi^{-1}(p) - \sqrt{\rho}\,M}{\sqrt{1-\rho}}\Bigr), \] rising sharply for bad \(M\) when correlation \(\rho\) is high. A CDO tranches a portfolio's loss: the equity tranche absorbs the first losses, then mezzanine, then senior. Higher \(\rho\) hurts senior tranches (joint disasters become possible) and helps equity (more scenarios with almost no defaults). Quoting tranche prices via the implied \(\rho\) parallels implied volatility.

Key Derivations

Survival probability from the hazard rate: let \(V(t,T)\) be survival from \(t\) to \(T\). Over the next instant, surviving to \(T\) requires surviving \(\mathrm{d} s\) (probability \(1-\lambda(s)\mathrm{d} s\)) and then surviving on. Formally, \(\partial V/\partial T = -\lambda(T)V(t,T)\) with \(V(t,t) = 1\); this linear ODE integrates to \[ V(t,T) = e^{-\int_t^T\lambda(s)\mathrm{d} s}. \qquad\blacksquare \] (Marks for: writing the conditional-probability step, the ODE, and the exponential solution.)

CDS spread: compute the expected discounted cash flows of each leg using default-in-period probabilities \(V_{i-1} - V_i\), set \(s(A+B) = C\), solve \(s = C/(A+B)\) — laid out numerically in the worked example.

Exam Questions — What They Actually Ask

Exam Pattern
  • "Define the hazard rate and derive the survival probability \(V(t,T)\)."
  • "Describe the cash flows of a CDS and derive the fair spread \(s = C/(A+B)\)"; often with a small numerical table.
  • "Explain the relation \(\lambda \approx s/(1-R)\)."
  • "In the Merton model, explain why equity is a call on firm value; derive the default probability and the credit spread."
  • "Why do risk-neutral default probabilities exceed historical ones?" (risk premia in spreads; use \(Q\) for pricing, \(P\) for risk)
  • "Describe the Gaussian copula / how CDO tranches respond to correlation."

Worked Example

June 2022, Question 2 --- Hazard rate, CDS, and $Q$ vs $P$ default probabilities

Question. Let \(V(t)\) be the survival probability to time \(t\), \(V(0)=1\).

(a) Express the conditional default probability over \([t,t+\Delta t]\) and define the hazard rate \(\lambda(t)\).

(b) Define a Credit Default Swap (CDS) and describe its payment structure.

(c) Under what assumptions can \(\lambda\) be inferred from a CDS spread \(s\)? Under which measure? Compare to a hazard rate from rating-agency default frequencies.

(d) Which hazard rate should be used for pricing financial derivatives and why?

Solution.

(a) Conditional default probability over \([t,t+\Delta t]\): \((V(t)-V(t+\Delta t))/V(t)\). Taking \(\Delta t\to0\): \[ \lambda(t) = -\frac{V'(t)}{V(t)} \quad\Longrightarrow\quad V(t) = e^{-\int_0^t\lambda(s)\,\mathrm{d} s}. \]

(b) A CDS has a protection buyer and a protection seller. The buyer pays a periodic spread \(s\) on a notional until maturity or a credit event, whichever comes first. If a credit event occurs, the seller pays \(1-R\) (notional minus recovery) to the buyer.

(c) Under risk-neutral \(Q\), assuming known constant recovery \(R\), set PV of premium leg \(=\) PV of protection leg. For a flat hazard rate, ignoring discounting: \(s \approx \lambda(1-R)\), so \(\lambda \approx s/(1-R)\). A full term-structure of \(\lambda(t)\) can be bootstrapped from the term structure of CDS spreads. This \(\lambda\) is under \(Q\) and embeds risk premia — it is systematically higher than the historical (\(P\)-measure) default frequency published by rating agencies, because risk-averse investors demand compensation for default risk.

(d) The \(Q\)-hazard rate (from market CDS spreads) must be used for pricing derivatives, consistent with discounting at the risk-free rate \(r\). Using \(P\)-measure frequencies would miss the default risk premium embedded in market prices.

Key marks: (a) ODE derivation and exponential solution; (b) both legs described explicitly; (c) \(\lambda \approx s/(1-R)\) and the \(Q\) vs \(P\) distinction clearly stated; (d) \(Q\) for pricing with \(r\) as discount rate.

Common Mistakes

Common Mistakes
  • Confusing the unconditional default density \(\lambda e^{-\lambda t}\) with the hazard rate \(\lambda\) (which conditions on survival).
  • Forgetting the accrual leg \(B\), or putting recovery on the wrong leg: protection pays \(1-R\), premiums are unaffected by \(R\).
  • In Merton, using equity volatility \(\sigma_E\) where asset volatility \(\sigma_V\) belongs (they differ by leverage: \(\sigma_EE_0 = \Phi(d_1)\sigma_VV_0\)).
  • Using historical (\(P\)) default probabilities to price a CDS — pricing needs \(Q\)-probabilities implied by market spreads.

Appendix: The Ten Derivations to Rehearse the Night Before tocsectionAppendix: The Ten Derivations to Rehearse the Night Before

    [label=.]
  1. Itô on \(G = \ln S\) (and be ready for \(S^n\), \(1/S\), \(e^{aS}\), \(Se^{r(T-t)}\), \(\ln P\), \(R = -\ln P/(T-t)\)): three partials \(\to\) Itô \(\to\) simplify. (Sec.\ 4)
  2. Market price of risk: \(\Pi = \sigma_g g\,f - \sigma_f f\,g\), show \(\mathrm{d} z\) cancels, set \(\mathrm{d}\Pi = r\Pi\mathrm{d} t\), rearrange. (Sec.\ 5)
  3. Binomial: replicate, \(q = (a-d)/(u-d)\), backward induction; state prices \(\phi_u = e^{-r\Delta t}q\). (Sec.\ 3)
  4. Forward price both arbitrage directions; existing-forward value \(f_t = (F_t - K)e^{-r(T-t)}\). (Sec.\ 2)
  5. BSM PDE: Itô on \(V\), hedge \(\Pi = V - V_S S\), kill \(\mathrm{d} z\) (and \(\mu\)), riskless \(\Rightarrow r\), collect. (Sec.\ 6)
  6. Greeks identity: \(\Theta + rS\Delta + \tfrac12\sigma^2S^2\Gamma = rV\) is the PDE; verify with closed forms via \(S\varphi(d_1) = Ke^{-r\tau}\varphi(d_2)\). (Sec.\ 7)
  7. Martingale toolkit: Itô on \(e^{-rt}S_t\) under \(Q\); Girsanov statement; \(F(t,T) = \mathrm{E}^{Q^T}[S_T|\F_t]\) from \(F = S/P\), \(P(T,T)=1\). (Sec.\ 8)
  8. Vasicek: bond PDE (via MPR since \(r\) not traded), affine ansatz, split by powers of \(r\), solve \(B_t = aB - 1\); Jamshidian for coupon-bond options. (Sec.\ 10)
  9. HJM drift: \(\mathrm{d}\ln P\) with the moving-limit term \(r_t\mathrm{d} t\), exponentiate (+\(\tfrac12\Sigma^2\)), drift \(= r\), differentiate in \(T\). (Sec.\ 11)
  10. Credit: hazard ODE \(\to V = e^{-\int\lambda}\); CDS legs \(\to s = C/(A+B)\) (check \(\approx(1-R)\lambda\)); Merton: equity \(=\) call, default prob \(\Phi(-d_2)\). (Sec.\ 13)

Good luck — and remember the examiner's three magic sentences: "choose the position so the \(\mathrm{d} z\) terms cancel", "a riskless portfolio must earn the risk-free rate, otherwise arbitrage", and "this holds for all (states/maturities), so match coefficients". Most derivations in this course are those three sentences plus algebra.

📋 Exam Format
3 hours · Written · No aids permitted — all formulas must be memorised.
Typical: Q1 Itô + forward/FX  |  Q2 BSM / Binomial / Greeks / Credit  |  Q3 Short rate / HJM / LMM / numeraire

First 60 seconds on any problem: Identify (1) state variable: \(S, r, F, f(t,T)\)?  (2) Model: GBM, BSM, Vasicek, HJM?  (3) Measure: \(\mathbb{P}, \mathbb{Q}, \mathbb{Q}^T\)?  (4) Dividend/foreign rate/storage?  (5) Terminal condition: what does the derivative pay?
Jump to:
GBM & Itô's Lemma 18/21 exams
▼

Every pricing formula in this course starts from an assumption about how the underlying asset moves. Think of a stock price like a leaf floating down a river: there is a steady current pulling it downstream (the drift \(\mu\)) and random turbulence knocking it left and right (the volatility \(\sigma\)). Geometric Brownian Motion (GBM) is the standard mathematical description of this: over each tiny instant the percentage return of the stock is normally distributed,

📋 Nøgleformler
Geometric Brownian Motion (under \(P\)) \[dS_t = \mu S_t\,dt + \sigma S_t\,dz_t\] Symbols: \(S_t\) = asset price; \(\mu\) = annualised expected instantaneous return (drift); \(\sigma\) = annualised volatility; \(z_t\) = Brownian motion, \(dz_t \sim N(0,dt)\). Equivalent return form: \(dS/S = \mu\,dt + \sigma\,dz \sim N(\mu\,dt,\ \sigma^2 dt)\).
Box-algebra (multiplication rules) — used in every Itô computation: \[(dt)^2 = 0,\qquad dt\cdot dz = 0,\qquad (dz)^2 = dt\] Hence for GBM: \((dS)^2 = \sigma^2 S^2\,dt\). For two correlated Brownian motions: \(dz_1\,dz_2 = \rho\,dt\).
Itô's lemma (general Itô process) — if \(dx = a(x,t)\,dt + b(x,t)\,dz\) and \(G(x,t)\) is smooth: \[dG = \Bigl(\frac{\partial G}{\partial t} + a\frac{\partial G}{\partial x} + \tfrac12 b^2\frac{\partial^2 G}{\partial x^2}\Bigr)dt + b\frac{\partial G}{\partial x}\,dz\] Symbols: \(a\) = drift of \(x\); \(b\) = diffusion coefficient of \(x\); the \(\tfrac12 b^2 G_{xx}\) term is the Itô correction. This is the most-used formula in the whole exam.
🎯 Fremgangsmåde til eksamen

When you see: "Let \(dS=\mu S\,dt+\sigma S\,dz\). Use Itô's lemma to find the process for \(G(S,t)\)"

Step 1 — Compute the three partials. Write out \(G_t\), \(G_S\), \(G_{SS}\) explicitly. (Most marks are lost here on differentiation slips.)
Step 2 — State Itô's lemma. \[dG = \bigl(G_t + \mu S G_S + \tfrac12\sigma^2 S^2 G_{SS}\bigr)dt + \sigma S G_S\,dz\] Always quote the lemma before plugging in — it shows the examiner you know the tool.
Step 3 — If asked, justify \((dS)^2=\sigma^2S^2dt\). \[(dS)^2 = \mu^2S^2(dt)^2 + 2\mu\sigma S^2\,dt\,dz + \sigma^2S^2(dz)^2 \to \sigma^2S^2\,dt\] using \((dt)^2 = dt\,dz = 0\), \((dz)^2 = dt\).
Step 4 — Substitute and simplify. Collect one \(dt\)-bracket and one \(dz\)-bracket.
Step 5 — Rewrite in terms of \(G\). If possible express drift and diffusion as multiples of \(G\) (shows whether \(G\) is itself a GBM). State the final answer: \[\boxed{dG = [\text{drift}]\,dt + [\text{diffusion}]\,dz}\]

Variant A: "Solve the SDE / find the distribution of \(S_T\)"

Step 1 — Apply Itô to \(\ln S\): \(d\ln S = (\mu-\tfrac12\sigma^2)dt + \sigma dz\).
Step 2 — Integrate: \(\ln S_T = \ln S_0 + (\mu-\tfrac12\sigma^2)T + \sigma z_T\).
Step 3 — Conclude: \[\boxed{S_T = S_0e^{(\mu-\frac12\sigma^2)T+\sigma z_T},\qquad \ln S_T\sim N\bigl(\ln S_0+(\mu-\tfrac12\sigma^2)T,\ \sigma^2T\bigr)}\] Add \(E[S_T]=S_0e^{\mu T}\) if expectations are asked (MGF trick).

Variant B: "Is \(G\) a martingale?"

Rule: compute \(dG\) via Itô; \(G\) is a martingale exactly when the \(dt\)-term is identically zero. E.g. \(e^{\sigma z_t - \frac12\sigma^2 t}\) is a martingale; \(e^{\sigma z_t}\) is not (drift \(\tfrac12\sigma^2\)).

Variant C: "Discuss the shortcomings of GBM"

Mention: continuous paths (no jumps), constant \(\sigma\) (vs. smile/clustering — stochastic-vol models), constant \(\mu\) (no mean reversion — vs. rates), lognormal thin tails vs. empirical fat tails, independent increments. Name at least one fix per shortcoming (Merton jump-diffusion, Heston/SABR, Vasicek/CIR).
Forward Pricing 16/21 exams
▼

A forward contract is a binding agreement made today to buy (long) or sell (short) an asset at a future date \(T\) for a price fixed today. No money changes hands at inception. The beautiful thing: its fair price does not depend on anyone's forecast of where the asset is going. It depends only on the cost of carrying the asset from today to \(T\).

📋 Nøgleformler
Spot–forward parity (no income, no costs) \[F(t,T) = \frac{S_t}{P(t,T)} = S_t e^{r(T-t)}\] \(F(t,T)\) = forward price agreed at \(t\) for delivery at \(T\); \(S_t\) = spot price; \(P(t,T)=e^{-r(T-t)}\) = zero-coupon bond price (discount factor); \(r\) = continuously compounded risk-free rate. The single most-used formula in exams.
Value of an existing forward with delivery price \(K\) \[V_K(t,T) = S_t - K\,P(t,T) = P(t,T)\bigl(F(t,T)-K\bigr)\] Positive for the long if the current forward price exceeds the locked-in \(K\). At inception \(K=F(t,T)\) so \(V_K=0\).
Cost of carry — the general forward price \[F(t,T) = S_t\,e^{(r + c^s - \delta - c^y)(T-t)}\] \(r\) = financing cost; \(c^s\) = storage/insurance cost rate; \(\delta\) = continuous income (dividend) yield; \(c^y\) = convenience yield. Special cases:
UnderlyingForward price
Non-dividend stock\(S_te^{r(T-t)}\)
Stock, dividend yield \(\delta\)\(S_te^{(r-\delta)(T-t)}\)
Stock, discrete dividends PV \(=I\)\((S_t-I)e^{r(T-t)}\)
Currency (foreign rate \(r_f\))\(S_te^{(r-r_f)(T-t)}\)
Commodity, storage cost \(c^s\)\(S_te^{(r+c^s)(T-t)}\)
🎯 Fremgangsmåde til eksamen

When you see: "Determine the forward price / show the arbitrage"

Step 1 — Identify the carry components. Income (\(\delta\) or discrete dividends), storage \(c^s\), convenience \(c^y\), foreign rate \(r_f\). Choose the right special case of \(F = Se^{(r+c^s-\delta-c^y)(T-t)}\).
Step 2 — State the replication argument (two ways to own the asset at \(T\)), then compute: \[\boxed{F(t,T) = S_te^{(r-\delta)(T-t)}\quad\text{(or the relevant variant)}}\]
Step 3 — If the quoted forward differs, construct the arbitrage table. Quoted \(F^{mkt}\gt F\): today — borrow \(S_t\), buy asset, short forward (net cash 0). At \(T\) — deliver asset, receive \(F^{mkt}\), repay loan \(S_te^{r(T-t)}\). Profit \(= F^{mkt} - S_te^{r(T-t)} \gt 0\) risk-free. Reverse all legs if \(F^{mkt}\lt F\). Always present it as a two-row cash-flow table (\(t\) and \(T\)) summing to (0, profit).

Variant A: "Value an existing forward contract"

Step 1 — Compute today's forward price \(F(t,T)\).
Step 2 — Discount the difference to the locked-in delivery price \(K\): \[\boxed{V_K(t,T) = P(t,T)\bigl(F(t,T)-K\bigr) = S_t - Ke^{-r(T-t)}}\] (Long position; flip the sign for the short.)

Variant B: "Find the process followed by the forward price"

Step 1 — \(F_t = S_te^{r(T-t)}\); apply Itô (see GBM topic): \[dF = (\mu - r)F\,dt + \sigma F\,dz\]
Step 2 — Comment: under \(Q\) (\(\mu\to r\)) the drift vanishes — \(F\) is a martingale, consistent with \(F(t,T) = E^Q_t[S_T]\) for constant \(r\), and with the \(T\)-forward-measure result \(F = E^T_t[S_T]\) in general.

Variant C: "Verify the forward value satisfies the BSM PDE"

With \(f = S - Ke^{-r(T-t)}\): \(f_t^{(t)} = -rKe^{-r(T-t)}\), \(f_S = 1\), \(f_{SS} = 0\). Then \[f_t^{(t)} + rSf_S + \tfrac12\sigma^2S^2f_{SS} = rS - rKe^{-r(T-t)} = rf \quad\checkmark\]
Binomial Model 14/21 exams
▼

Take one period, one stock that can only go up to \(uS_0\) or down to \(dS_0\), and a bank account growing by factor \(e^{rT}\). This toy world (Hull's fig. 13.1: \(S_0=20\), up to 22 or down to 18, \(K=21\)) already contains the two deepest ideas of derivatives pricing:

📋 Nøgleformler
One-period replication / hedge ratio \[\Delta = \frac{V_u - V_d}{uS_0 - dS_0} = \frac{V_u-V_d}{S_0(u-d)}\] \(V_u,V_d\) = derivative payoffs after up/down move; \(u,d\) = gross up/down factors. \(\Delta\) = shares held per short derivative to make the portfolio riskless. Interpretation: the slope of the payoff across the two states.
Risk-neutral probability and one-period price (memorise!) \[V_0 = e^{-r\Delta t}\bigl(q_uV_u + (1-q_u)V_d\bigr),\qquad q_u = \frac{a-d}{u-d}\] \(a\) = risk-neutral one-period growth factor of the underlying:
Underlying\(a\)
Non-dividend stock\(e^{r\Delta t}\)
Stock with dividend yield \(\delta\)\(e^{(r-\delta)\Delta t}\)
Currency with foreign rate \(r_f\)\(e^{(r-r_f)\Delta t}\)
Futures contract\(1\)
Commodity, storage cost \(s\)\(e^{(r+s)\Delta t}\)
No-arbitrage condition: \(d \lt a \lt u\) ⟺ \(0 \lt q_u \lt 1\).
CRR calibration to volatility \[u = e^{\sigma\sqrt{\Delta t}},\qquad d = e^{-\sigma\sqrt{\Delta t}} = 1/u,\qquad p_u = \frac{e^{\mu\Delta t}-d}{u-d}\ (\text{real-world}),\ \ q_u = \frac{e^{r\Delta t}-d}{u-d}\] Matching conditions: mean \(p_uu + (1-p_u)d = e^{\mu\Delta t}\) and variance \(p_uu^2+(1-p_u)d^2 - (p_uu+(1-p_u)d)^2 = \sigma^2\Delta t\). Three parameters, two equations — CRR closes it with \(u=1/d\); alternatives: Jarrow-Rudd \(q_u=\tfrac12\), Tian (third moment).
🎯 Fremgangsmåde til eksamen

When you see: "Price this option in a binomial tree"

Step 1 — Tree parameters. If \(u,d\) given, use them; otherwise \(u = e^{\sigma\sqrt{\Delta t}}\), \(d=1/u\). Identify the correct growth factor \(a\): \(e^{r\Delta t}\) stock, \(e^{(r-\delta)\Delta t}\) dividend yield, \(e^{(r-r_f)\Delta t}\) currency, \(1\) futures.
Step 2 — Risk-neutral probability. \[q_u = \frac{a-d}{u-d}\] Check \(0\lt q_u\lt 1\) (equivalently \(d\lt a\lt u\)) and say "hence no arbitrage".
Step 3 — Terminal payoffs. Fill in \(S_0u^jd^{n-j}\) at the end nodes and apply the payoff \(g\).
Step 4 — Backward induction. Per node: \(V = e^{-r\Delta t}(q_uV_{up} + (1-q_u)V_{down})\). For a 2-step European you may jump straight to \[\boxed{V_0 = e^{-2r\Delta t}\bigl(q_u^2V_{uu} + 2q_u(1-q_u)V_{ud} + (1-q_u)^2V_{dd}\bigr)}\]
Step 5 — American check (if asked). At every interior node compare with intrinsic value and take the max; state at which node(s) early exercise happens and why (dividends for calls, financing/interest for puts).
Step 6 — Delta (often a follow-up). \(\Delta_0 = \frac{V_u-V_d}{S_0(u-d)}\); note it differs node by node ⇒ dynamic hedging.

Variant A: "Show the price does not depend on the real probability \(p\)"

Run the replication derivation: choose \(\Delta = \frac{V_u-V_d}{S_0(u-d)}\), show the hedged portfolio has the same value in both states, discount at \(r\), solve for \(V_0\). Point out that \(p\) never entered; only \(u,d,r\) (the possible states and prices) matter. Girsanov remark: \(p\to q\) changes drift, not volatility.

Variant B: "Find the state prices / verify FTAP"

Steps: write the payoff matrix \(D\) and price vector \(S\); solve \(S = D\psi\) for \(\psi\); confirm \(\psi\gg0\) ⟺ no arbitrage. In the binomial case conclude \[\boxed{\psi_u = e^{-rT}q_u,\qquad \psi_d = e^{-rT}(1-q_u)}\] Mention completeness: 2 independent assets, 2 states ⇒ every claim replicable ⇒ unique \(\psi\) and unique price.

Variant C: "American put — where is early exercise optimal?"

Compute continuation vs. intrinsic at each node from the back. Typical result: exercise early deep in the money at low stock prices (interest on \(K\) beats time value). For an American call on a non-dividend stock: never exercise early — prove via \(c_t \ge S_t - Ke^{-r(T-t)} \gt S_t - K\).
Market Price of Risk 12/21 exams
▼

Suppose many different derivatives all depend on the same single source of randomness — a state variable \(\theta\) driven by one Brownian motion \(dz\) (think: the oil price driving all oil derivatives, or the short rate driving all bonds). Each derivative \(f_i\) has its own expected return \(\mu_i\) and its own volatility \(\sigma_i\). The market price of risk result says these cannot be arbitrary: in an arbitrage-free market the excess return per unit of risk must be identical across all of them,

📋 Nøgleformler
Setup — one common risk factor \[\frac{d\theta}{\theta} = m\,dt + s\,dz,\qquad \frac{df_i}{f_i} = \mu_i\,dt + \sigma_i\,dz\quad (i=1,2)\] \(\theta\) = state variable (need not be traded); \(f_i\) = prices of traded securities depending only on \(\theta\) and \(t\), no payouts; \(\mu_i,\sigma_i\) = their drift and volatility; single Brownian motion \(dz\).
Market price of risk (THE formula) \[\lambda = \frac{\mu_1 - r}{\sigma_1} = \frac{\mu_2 - r}{\sigma_2}\qquad\Longleftrightarrow\qquad \mu_i = r + \lambda\sigma_i\] \(\lambda\) = expected excess return per unit of volatility (continuous-time Sharpe ratio), common to all securities driven by this \(dz\). Appears in ~half of all exams, often as "show that…".
Drift restated with \(\lambda\) \[\frac{df}{f} = (r + \lambda\sigma_f)\,dt + \sigma_f\,dz\] Risk-neutral world: \(\lambda = 0\) ⇒ \(df/f = r\,dt + \sigma_f\,dz\). The measure change \(P\to Q\) shifts the drift by \(-\lambda\sigma_f\) and leaves \(\sigma_f\) unchanged (Girsanov).
🎯 Fremgangsmåde til eksamen

When you see: "Show that two derivatives on the same source of uncertainty have the same market price of risk"

Step 1 — Write both processes: \(df_i = \mu_if_i\,dt + \sigma_if_i\,dz\), stressing the common \(dz\).
Step 2 — Form \(\Pi = \sigma_2f_2f_1 - \sigma_1f_1f_2\) (i.e. \(\sigma_2f_2\) units of 1, short \(\sigma_1f_1\) units of 2) and show the \(dz\)-terms cancel.
Step 3 — Riskless ⇒ \(d\Pi = r\Pi\,dt\); write out both sides.
Step 4 — Divide by \(f_1f_2\), rearrange: \[\boxed{\frac{\mu_1-r}{\sigma_1} = \frac{\mu_2-r}{\sigma_2} = \lambda}\] Conclude: \(\lambda\) is a property of the risk factor, so every asset satisfies \(\mu = r + \lambda\sigma\).

Variant A: "Given \(\mu\), \(\sigma\), \(r\) — compute \(\lambda\), or the expected return of another derivative"

Steps: \(\lambda = (\mu_1-r)/\sigma_1\); then \(\mu_2 = r + \lambda\sigma_2\). Watch signs: if the second derivative moves opposite to the factor (\(\sigma_2\lt0\)), then \(\mu_2 \lt r\). Box the number.

Variant B: "Write the process under the risk-neutral measure"

Rule: subtract \(\lambda\times(\text{diffusion coefficient})\) from the drift, keep the diffusion: \[dx = m\,dt + s\,dz\ \longrightarrow\ dx = (m - \lambda s)\,dt + s\,dz^Q\] For a traded asset this must give drift \(rf\) — a useful sanity check. For Vasicek: \(b\to b^* = b - \lambda\sigma/a\).

Variant C: "Change of numeraire — what happens to the drift?"

Rule: moving from numeraire \(g\) to numeraire \(h\), each asset's drift changes by \[\boxed{\sum_i(\sigma_i^h - \sigma_i^g)\,\sigma_i^f}\] (the covariance between the numeraire-vol difference and the asset). Under numeraire \(g\), the market price of risk equals \(\sigma_g\), and \(f/g\) is a martingale.
Black-Scholes-Merton 20/21 exams
▼

Black, Scholes and Merton asked: what is a European option worth if the stock follows a GBM? Their answer method is more important than the formula. Because the option price \(V(S,t)\) and the stock \(S\) are driven by the same Brownian motion, a portfolio that is long the option and short \(\Delta = \partial V/\partial S\) shares has no \(dz\)-term at all over the next instant. A riskless portfolio must earn the riskless rate — writing that single sentence as an equation produces the Black-Scholes PDE:

📋 Nøgleformler
The Black-Scholes PDE (no dividends) \[\frac{\partial V}{\partial t} + rS\frac{\partial V}{\partial S} + \tfrac12\sigma^2S^2\frac{\partial^2 V}{\partial S^2} - rV = 0\] \(V(S,t)\) = price of any (path-independent European) derivative; note no \(\mu\). With dividend yield \(\delta\): replace \(rS V_S\) by \((r-\delta)SV_S\). Every derivative on \(S\) solves this; the payoff (boundary condition) selects the solution. Verifications (e.g. \(f = S-Ke^{-r(T-t)}\), or \(V=e^{rt}\)) are standard exam tasks.
BSM formulas (memorise perfectly) \[c_t = S_t\Phi(d_1) - Ke^{-r(T-t)}\Phi(d_2),\qquad p_t = Ke^{-r(T-t)}\Phi(-d_2) - S_t\Phi(-d_1)\] \[d_1 = \frac{\ln(S_t/K) + (r + \tfrac12\sigma^2)(T-t)}{\sigma\sqrt{T-t}},\qquad d_2 = d_1 - \sigma\sqrt{T-t}\] \(\Phi\) = standard normal cdf. \(\Phi(d_2) = P^Q(S_T \gt K)\); \(\Delta_{call} = \Phi(d_1)\).
The lognormal lemma (the engine behind the formula) If \(V_T\) is lognormal with \(E[V_T] = F\) and \(\operatorname{sd}(\ln V_T) = w\): \[E[\max(V_T - K, 0)] = F\,\Phi\!\Bigl(\frac{\ln(F/K) + w^2/2}{w}\Bigr) - K\,\Phi\!\Bigl(\frac{\ln(F/K) - w^2/2}{w}\Bigr)\] Set \(F = S_0e^{rT}\), \(w = \sigma\sqrt T\), discount by \(e^{-rT}\) ⇒ BSM. The same lemma yields Black's formula and caplet/swaption prices — learn it once, use it five times.
🎯 Fremgangsmåde til eksamen

When you see: "Compute the Black-Scholes price"

Step 1 — Identify inputs \(S, K, r, \sigma, T-t\) and the underlying type (dividends? currency? futures?). Adjust: \(S\to Se^{-\delta(T-t)}\), or Black's \(F\)-form.
Step 2 — Compute \(d_1 = \frac{\ln(S/K)+(r+\frac12\sigma^2)(T-t)}{\sigma\sqrt{T-t}}\), then \(d_2 = d_1 - \sigma\sqrt{T-t}\). Keep 4 decimals.
Step 3 — Look up / state \(\Phi(d_1), \Phi(d_2)\).
Step 4 — \[\boxed{c = S\Phi(d_1) - Ke^{-r(T-t)}\Phi(d_2)}\] For a put use \(p = Ke^{-r(T-t)}\Phi(-d_2) - S\Phi(-d_1)\), or PCP as a cross-check: \(c - p = S - Ke^{-r(T-t)}\).

Variant A: "Derive the Black-Scholes PDE"

Reproduce Derivation 1 in five lines: (i) Itô on \(V\); (ii) \(\Pi = V - \Delta S\); (iii) \(\Delta = V_S\) kills \(dz\); (iv) \(d\Pi = r\Pi dt\); (v) rearrange. Always end with: "note that \(\mu\) does not appear — hence risk-neutral valuation is valid". That sentence earns marks.

Variant B: "Show that [function] satisfies the PDE"

Compute \(V_t, V_S, V_{SS}\); plug into \(V_t + rSV_S + \tfrac12\sigma^2S^2V_{SS}\); show it equals \(rV\). Check the boundary condition at \(t = T\) too — that identifies which derivative it is.

Variant C: "Limits / boundary behaviour"

Method: examine \(d_1, d_2\) as the parameter moves, conclude \(\Phi\to 0\) or \(1\). E.g. \(\sigma\downarrow0\) with \(S \gt Ke^{-r(T-t)}\): \(\ln(S/K)+r(T-t)\gt0\) so \(d_{1,2}\to+\infty\), \(c\to S - Ke^{-r(T-t)}\); if \(S \lt Ke^{-r(T-t)}\), \(d_{1,2}\to-\infty\), \(c\to0\). Combine: \(c\to\max(S-Ke^{-r(T-t)},0)\).

Variant D: "Price via risk-neutral expectation"

Write \(c = e^{-rT}E^Q[\max(S_T-K,0)]\); state the \(Q\)-distribution of \(\ln S_T\); apply the lognormal lemma with \(F = S_0e^{rT}\), \(w = \sigma\sqrt T\). Quote the lemma explicitly — it is on the slides and using it is the intended solution path.
Put-Call Parity & Greeks 15/21 exams
▼

Buy a call, sell a put (same \(K\), same \(T\)). At maturity you get \(\max(S_T-K,0) - \max(K-S_T,0) = S_T - K\) in every state — precisely a forward contract with delivery price \(K\). Since the payoffs match state by state, prices must match today:

📋 Nøgleformler
Put-call parity (European, model-free) \[c_t - p_t = S_t - Ke^{-r(T-t)}\] Variants: dividends with PV \(I\): \(c - p = S - I - Ke^{-r(T-t)}\); dividend yield: \(c-p = Se^{-\delta(T-t)} - Ke^{-r(T-t)}\); currency: \(c-p = Se^{-r_f(T-t)} - Ke^{-r(T-t)}\); futures (Black): \(c - p = e^{-r(T-t)}(F - K)\). American call (no dividends, \(r\ge0\)): \(c_t \ge S_t - Ke^{-r(T-t)} \ge S_t - K\) ⇒ \(C_t = c_t\), never exercised early.
The key identity behind all Greeks \[S\,\Phi'(d_1) = Ke^{-r(T-t)}\,\Phi'(d_2),\qquad \Phi'(x) = \tfrac{1}{\sqrt{2\pi}}e^{-x^2/2}\] Makes cross-terms cancel when differentiating BSM. Derive it by expanding \(\Phi'(d_2 + \sigma\sqrt{T-t})\).
Greeks of European options (no dividends) — the exam table
GreekCallPut
\(\Delta = \frac{\partial V}{\partial S}\)\(\Phi(d_1)\)\(\Phi(d_1) - 1\)
\(\Gamma = \frac{\partial^2V}{\partial S^2}\)\(\dfrac{\Phi'(d_1)}{S\sigma\sqrt{T-t}}\) (same, always \(\gt0\) long)
\(\Theta = \frac{\partial V}{\partial t}\)\(-\dfrac{S\Phi'(d_1)\sigma}{2\sqrt{T-t}} - rKe^{-r(T-t)}\Phi(d_2)\)\(-\dfrac{S\Phi'(d_1)\sigma}{2\sqrt{T-t}} + rKe^{-r(T-t)}\Phi(-d_2)\)
Vega \(= \frac{\partial V}{\partial\sigma}\)\(S\sqrt{T-t}\,\Phi'(d_1)\) (same)
\(\rho = \frac{\partial V}{\partial r}\)\(K(T-t)e^{-r(T-t)}\Phi(d_2)\)\(-K(T-t)e^{-r(T-t)}\Phi(-d_2)\)
With dividend yield \(\delta\): multiply \(\Delta,\Gamma,\)Vega's \(S\)-terms by \(e^{-\delta(T-t)}\) (e.g. \(\Delta_c = e^{-\delta(T-t)}\Phi(d_1)\)).
🎯 Fremgangsmåde til eksamen

When you see: "Check for arbitrage using put-call parity"

Step 1 — Compute both sides: LHS \(= c - p\), RHS \(= S - Ke^{-r(T-t)}\) (adjust \(S\) for dividends/currency).
Step 2 — If LHS \(\gt\) RHS: the call is rich — sell call, buy put, buy stock, borrow \(Ke^{-r(T-t)}\). If LHS \(\lt\) RHS: reverse every leg.
Step 3 — Show the cash-flow table: today's net inflow \(= |c - p - S + Ke^{-r(T-t)}|\), at \(T\) all payoffs cancel to zero in both states. \[\boxed{\text{Arbitrage profit} = |c - p - S + Ke^{-r(T-t)}|\ \text{today, risk-free}}\]

Variant A: "Compute and interpret the Greeks"

Step 1 — Compute \(d_1, d_2\) as in a BSM pricing question.
Step 2 — Read off: \(\Delta_c = \Phi(d_1)\), \(\Gamma = \frac{\Phi'(d_1)}{S\sigma\sqrt{T-t}}\), Vega \(= S\sqrt{T-t}\Phi'(d_1)\), \(\Theta_c = -\frac{S\Phi'(d_1)\sigma}{2\sqrt{T-t}} - rKe^{-r(T-t)}\Phi(d_2)\), \(\rho_c = K(T-t)e^{-r(T-t)}\Phi(d_2)\).
Step 3 — Interpret each in one sentence ("if \(S\) rises 1, the option gains ≈ \(\Delta\)"; "delta moves by ≈ \(\Gamma\) per unit of \(S\)"; "1 vol-point up ⇒ price up Vega/100"…). Interpretation sentences carry marks.

Variant B: "Delta-hedge this position / rebalance"

Step 1 — Portfolio delta: \(\Delta_\Pi = \sum n_i\Delta_i\) (short options count negative).
Step 2 — Trade \(-\Delta_\Pi\) shares to make \(\Delta = 0\); finance via the bank account.
Step 3 — Next period: recompute \(\Delta\) at new \(S\) and \(t\); the difference is the rebalancing trade. Comment: rebalancing frequency ↑ when \(\Gamma\) is high (ATM, short-dated).

Variant C: "Make the book delta- and gamma-neutral"

Order matters: (1) buy \(n_{opt} = -\Gamma_\Pi/\Gamma_{traded}\) of the traded option (fixes gamma); (2) then trade shares to zero the new delta (shares have \(\Gamma = 0\) so gamma stays fixed). \[\boxed{n_{opt} = -\frac{\Gamma_\Pi}{\Gamma_{traded}},\qquad n_{shares} = -(\Delta_\Pi + n_{opt}\Delta_{traded})}\]

Variant D: "Explain the theta–gamma relation"

Numeraire & Measure Change 12/21 exams
▼

A price is always "units of something per something else". Normally we measure in cash — but nothing stops us measuring the option in shares, in zero-coupon bonds, or in units of an annuity. A numeraire is any strictly positive traded price process \(g_t\) used as the unit of account. The deep result of this chapter: for each numeraire \(g\) there is a probability measure under which every traded asset's price, measured in units of \(g\), is a martingale — the "forward risk neutral" measure w.r.t. \(g\). Pricing then collapses to computing one expectation:

📋 Nøgleformler
Numeraire (definition) Any strictly positive \(\mathcal F_t\)-adapted traded price process \(g_t\) with no intermediate payments. Relative price: \(f^g_t = f_t/g_t\).
Central theorem — numeraire ⟺ market price of risk Let \(df = (r+\lambda\sigma_f)f\,dt + \sigma_ff\,dz\) and \(dg = (r+\lambda\sigma_g)g\,dt+\sigma_gg\,dz\). If \(\lambda = \sigma_g\), then \[d\Bigl(\frac fg\Bigr) = (\sigma_f-\sigma_g)\frac fg\,dz\] — zero drift ⇒ \(f/g\) is a martingale. The measure with \(\lambda = \sigma_g\) is the forward-risk-neutral measure w.r.t. \(g\).
The universal pricing formula \[\frac{f_0}{g_0} = E^g\!\Bigl[\frac{f_T}{g_T}\Bigr]\qquad\Longleftrightarrow\qquad f_0 = g_0\,E^g\!\Bigl[\frac{f_T}{g_T}\Bigr]\] Every concrete pricing formula in the course is a special case of this line.
🎯 Fremgangsmåde til eksamen

When you see: "Show that \(f/g\) is a martingale when the market price of risk is \(\sigma_g\)"

Step 1 — Write the two processes with \(\lambda=\sigma_g\): drifts \(r+\sigma_g\sigma_f\) and \(r+\sigma_g^2\).
Step 2 — Itô on \(\ln f\) and \(\ln g\); subtract to get \(d\ln(f/g) = -\tfrac12(\sigma_f-\sigma_g)^2dt + (\sigma_f-\sigma_g)dz\).
Step 3 — Itô on \(e^x\): \[\boxed{d(f/g) = (\sigma_f-\sigma_g)(f/g)\,dz}\] Zero drift ⇒ martingale. This exact derivation is a recurring exam question — practise until you can do it in 4 lines.

Variant A: "Price a claim using a suitable numeraire"

Step 1 — Look at the payoff. Pure \(T\)-payoff \(g(\cdot)\) with stochastic rates ⇒ ZCB numeraire; swap-rate payoff ⇒ annuity; payoff homogeneous in \(S\) ⇒ consider stock numeraire.
Step 2 — Write the universal formula \(f_0 = g_0E^g[f_T/g_T]\) and simplify \(g_T\) (e.g. \(P(T,T)=1\)).
Step 3 — State which quantity is a martingale (hence what its \(E^g\) is) and, if lognormality is assumed, apply the lognormal lemma / Black formula. \[\boxed{f_0 = P(0,T)\,E^T[f_T]\quad\text{or}\quad f_0 = A_0\,E^A[\text{payoff}]}\]

Variant B: "Show the forward rate/forward price is a martingale under the appropriate measure"

Express the object as (traded portfolio)/(numeraire): forward price \(F = S/P(t,T)\); forward rate \(F = \frac1\tau\frac{P(t,T_S)-P(t,T_E)}{P(t,T_E)}\); swap rate \(s = \frac{P_0 - P_N}{A}\). Then invoke: any traded price over the numeraire is a martingale under that numeraire's measure. Conclude \(F_0 = E^{T}[\cdot]\).

Variant C: "What happens to the drift when we change numeraire?"

\[\boxed{\Delta\text{drift} = \sum_i(\sigma_i^{new} - \sigma_i^{old})\,\sigma_i^f}\] Volatility unchanged (Girsanov). Cite the LMM as the standard application.
Martingales 11/21 exams
▼

A martingale is a process whose best forecast of tomorrow is today's value: \(E_t[X_T] = X_t\) for \(t\le T\). No drift, no free lunch — a "fair game". For Itô processes the test is brutally simple: zero \(dt\)-term. \(dX = \sigma\,dz\) is a martingale; anything with a drift is not. (Technically we also need integrability — for this course, checking the drift suffices.)

📋 Nøgleformler
Martingale (definition) \[E_t[X_T] = X_t\quad\text{for all }t\le T\] (w.r.t. a filtration \((\mathcal F_t)\) and a measure). Itô-process test: \(X\) is a martingale ⟺ its \(dt\)-coefficient is 0 (plus integrability): \(dX = \sigma_X\,dz\).
Equivalent martingale measure & the pricing formula \[S_t = E^Q_t\bigl[e^{-r(T-t)}S_T\bigr],\qquad \frac{S_t}{B_t} = E^Q_t\Bigl[\frac{S_T}{B_T}\Bigr],\quad B_t = e^{\int_0^t r\,ds}\] FTAP: no arbitrage ⟺ an EMM exists (given a numeraire). Completeness ⟺ EMM unique. General numeraire \(g\): \(f_t/g_t = E^g_t[f_T/g_T]\).
Girsanov's theorem (working version) If \(z^P\) is a \(P\)-Brownian motion and \(\lambda_t\) a (suitably integrable) process, then \[z^Q_t = z^P_t + \int_0^t\lambda_s\,ds\] is a Brownian motion under the measure \(Q\) with density \(\frac{dQ}{dP} = \exp\bigl(-\int_0^T\lambda\,dz^P - \tfrac12\int_0^T\lambda^2ds\bigr)\). Consequence: drift changes by \(-\lambda\times\)diffusion; volatility unchanged; distribution type preserved.
🎯 Fremgangsmåde til eksamen

When you see: "Show that X is a martingale (under measure M)"

Step 1 — Write the dynamics of the ingredients under that measure (e.g. under \(Q\): all traded assets drift at \(r\); under \(Q^T\): prices relative to \(P(t,T)\) driftless).
Step 2 — Apply Itô to \(X\).
Step 3 — Show the \(dt\)-term cancels: \(\boxed{dX = (\cdots)\,dz \Rightarrow X\text{ martingale}}\). If it does not cancel, say explicitly "not a martingale; drift = …".

Variant A: "Price the claim with payoff \(g(S_T)\)"

Steps: (1) quote \(V_0 = e^{-rT}E^Q[g(S_T)]\); (2) state the \(Q\)-distribution \(\ln S_T\sim N(\ln S_0+(r-\frac12\sigma^2)T, \sigma^2T)\); (3) compute the expectation (lognormal lemma for \(\max\)-payoffs; MGF for power payoffs: \(E^Q[S_T^n] = S_0^ne^{(nr+\frac12n(n-1)\sigma^2)T}\)); \[\boxed{V_0 = e^{-rT}E^Q[g(S_T)]}\]

Variant B: "Explain / apply Girsanov"

State: \(dz^Q = dz^P + \lambda dt\) is a \(Q\)-BM; drift shifts by \(-\lambda\sigma\), volatility invariant, measures equivalent. Apply: choose \(\lambda = (\mu-r)/\sigma\) to send drift to \(r\). One-sentence intuition: "reweight the probabilities of paths so that risk carries no premium; the reweighting cannot change which paths are possible, hence volatility is untouched."

Variant C: "State the FTAP / relate deflators and EMMs"

FTAP: arbitrage-free ⟺ ∃ strictly positive linear pricing functional ⟺ ∃ state-price deflator \(\pi\) ⟺ ∃ EMM \(Q\) (given numeraire). Unique ⟺ complete market. Bridge: \(\pi_T/\pi_t = e^{-r(T-t)}(dQ/dP)\). Mention: fails with infinite horizon (Ponzi/doubling) without extra conditions.
IR Derivatives (Caps/Swaptions) 10/21 exams
▼

Interest-rate derivatives are the world's largest derivatives market, and structurally trickier than equity options: (1) the underlying is a whole curve, not one number; (2) the rate you option on is also the rate you discount with; (3) volatilities differ along the curve. The market's pragmatic solution: for each product, pick the one forward rate (or swap rate) that matters, assume it is lognormal under the measure that makes it a martingale, and apply a Black-Scholes-type formula — Black's model. The numeraire machinery is what makes this legitimate rather than a hack.

📋 Nøgleformler
Rates from bond prices (get these straight first) \[F(t;T_S,T_E) = \frac{1}{\tau}\Bigl(\frac{P(t,T_S)}{P(t,T_E)}-1\Bigr),\qquad f(t,T) = -\frac{\partial\ln P(t,T)}{\partial T},\qquad r(t) = f(t,t)\] \[P(t,T) = e^{-\int_t^Tf(t,s)ds}\] Simple forward rate \(F\), instantaneous forward \(f\), short rate \(r\), ZCB price \(P\), accrual fraction \(\tau\).
Swap rate \[s(t) = \frac{P(t,T_0) - P(t,T_N)}{\sum_{i=0}^{N-1}\tau_iP(t,T_{i+1})} = \frac{P(t,T_0)-P(t,T_N)}{A(t)}\] \(A(t)\) = annuity (PV of 1 per year on the fixed leg dates). Fixed leg PV = \(s\,A\); floating leg PV = \(P(t,T_0) - P(t,T_N)\).
Caplet payoff and Black formula (exam workhorse) Payoff at \(t_{k+1}\): \(N\tau_k\max(L_k - L_K, 0)\), \(L_k\) fixed at \(t_k\). \[\text{Caplet} = N\,P(0,t_{k+1})\,\tau_k\bigl(F_k\Phi(d_1) - L_K\Phi(d_2)\bigr)\] \[d_1 = \frac{\ln(F_k/L_K) + \tfrac12\sigma_k^2t_k}{\sigma_k\sqrt{t_k}},\qquad d_2 = d_1 - \sigma_k\sqrt{t_k}\] \(F_k\) = today's forward rate for \([t_k,t_{k+1}]\); \(\sigma_k\) = forward-rate vol. Note: vol to \(t_k\) (fixing), discount to \(t_{k+1}\) (payment). Floorlet: \(N P(0,t_{k+1})\tau_k(L_K\Phi(-d_2) - F_k\Phi(-d_1))\). Cap = sum of caplets.
🎯 Fremgangsmåde til eksamen

When you see: "Price a caplet / cap with Black's formula"

Step 1 — Extract per caplet: forward rate \(F_k\) (from the curve: \(F_k = \frac1{\tau_k}(\frac{P(0,t_k)}{P(0,t_{k+1})}-1)\)), strike \(L_K\), vol \(\sigma_k\), fixing \(t_k\), payment \(t_{k+1}\), accrual \(\tau_k\), notional \(N\).
Step 2 — \(d_1 = \frac{\ln(F_k/L_K)+\frac12\sigma_k^2t_k}{\sigma_k\sqrt{t_k}}\), \(d_2 = d_1 - \sigma_k\sqrt{t_k}\). Vol uses \(t_k\), not \(t_{k+1}\)!
Step 3 — \[\boxed{\text{Caplet} = N\tau_kP(0,t_{k+1})\bigl(F_k\Phi(d_1) - L_K\Phi(d_2)\bigr)}\] Cap = sum over caplets (skip the first period if the rate is already fixed and below strike — or include its deterministic payoff).
Step 4 — If asked "why is this consistent?": one sentence — "under the \(t_{k+1}\)-forward measure the forward rate is a lognormal martingale and \(f_0 = P(0,t_{k+1})E^{k+1}[\text{payoff}]\)".

Variant A: "Price a European swaption"

Steps: (1) forward swap rate \(s_0 = \frac{P(0,T_0)-P(0,T_N)}{A(0)}\), annuity \(A(0) = \sum\tau_iP(0,T_{i+1})\); (2) Black \(d_1,d_2\) with \(\sigma\sqrt T\) (\(T\) = option expiry); (3) \[\boxed{PS = N\,A(0)\,(s_0\Phi(d_1) - s_K\Phi(d_2))}\] Receiver: swap the \(\Phi\)-signs. Justification sentence: annuity numeraire, swap rate martingale.

Variant B: "Show cap − floor = swap" / "caplet = bond put"

Cap−floor: per-period identity \(\max(a-b,0)-\max(b-a,0) = a-b\), then sum and take PV. Caplet=put: discount payoff to \(t_k\), manipulate into \(\max(N - \text{ZCB value},0)\). Both are 4-line answers — write them cleanly.

Variant C: "Cap vs. swaption — compare"

Cap = portfolio of independent options (one per period, each exercised separately); swaption = single option on the whole payment stream. Hence: cap ≥ corresponding swaption-based structure; caps depend only on individual forward-rate vols, swaptions also on correlations between forward rates. Also: cap-lognormal and swaprate-lognormal are mutually inconsistent assumptions — market convention, not one coherent model (the LMM makes the cap side rigorous).
Short Rate Models 14/21 exams
▼

Short-rate models compress the whole yield curve into a single state variable: the instantaneous short rate \(r(t)\), modelled directly under the risk-neutral measure \(Q\) as \(dr = m(r,t)\,dt + s(r,t)\,dz\). Once you know the \(Q\)-dynamics of \(r\), every zero-coupon bond price follows from the martingale pricing formula \(P(t,T) = E^Q_t[e^{-\int_t^T r\,ds}]\) — and from bond prices, the whole term structure \(R(t,T)\), plus bond options, caps, and swaptions.

📋 Nøgleformler
General setup (under \(Q\)) \[dr = \bigl(m(r,t) - \lambda(r,t)s(r,t)\bigr)dt + s(r,t)\,dz,\qquad P(t,T) = E^Q_t\bigl[e^{-\int_t^Tr(s)ds}\bigr]\] \(m\) = real-world drift, \(s\) = diffusion, \(\lambda\) = market price of interest-rate risk (calibrated, not derived).
Bond-pricing PDE (Feynman-Kac) \[\frac{\partial V}{\partial t} + \tfrac12 s^2\frac{\partial^2V}{\partial r^2} + (m-\lambda s)\frac{\partial V}{\partial r} - rV = 0,\qquad V(r,T,T)=1\] Same PDE (different boundary condition) for European bond options. With continuous coupon \(C(r,t)\): add \(+C\) on the left.
Affine models \[m - \lambda s = m_1(t) - m_2(t)r,\qquad s = \sqrt{s_1(t) + s_2(t)r}\ \Longrightarrow\ P(t,T) = A(t,T)e^{-B(t,T)r(t)}\] ODE system (from substituting into the PDE, with \(A_1 = \ln A\)): \[\frac{\partial A_1}{\partial t} = -\tfrac12 s_1B^2 + m_1B,\qquad \frac{\partial B}{\partial t} = \tfrac12 s_2B^2 + m_2B - 1,\qquad A_1(T,T)=B(T,T)=0\] Yields are affine in \(r\): \(R(t,T) = -\frac{\ln A}{T-t} + \frac{B}{T-t}r(t)\).
🎯 Fremgangsmåde til eksamen

When you see: "Solve the Vasicek/OU process" or "find the distribution of \(r(t)\)"

Step 1 — Multiply by the integrating factor: consider \(Y = e^{at}r\); show \(dY = abe^{at}dt + \sigma e^{at}dz\) (the \(r\)-terms cancel — state this).
Step 2 — Integrate and divide by \(e^{at}\): \[r_t = r_0e^{-at} + b(1-e^{-at}) + \sigma\int_0^te^{-a(t-u)}dz_u\]
Step 3 — Conclude normality and give moments: \[\boxed{E[r_t] = r_0e^{-at}+b(1-e^{-at}),\qquad \operatorname{Var}[r_t] = \frac{\sigma^2}{2a}(1-e^{-2at})}\] Comment on limits \(t\to\infty\) (stationary \(N(b,\sigma^2/2a)\)) and \(a\to0\) (Ho-Lee-like, variance \(\sigma^2t\)).

Variant A: "Derive the bond-pricing PDE"

Reproduce the two-bond hedge: portfolio \(\Pi = V_1 - \Delta V_2\), \(\Delta = \frac{\partial V_1/\partial r}{\partial V_2/\partial r}\), riskless ⇒ earns \(r\), separate maturities ⇒ common function \(\lambda\), PDE with drift \(m - \lambda s\). Close with: "since \(r\) is not traded, \(\lambda\) is exogenous/calibrated — unlike BSM where the underlying is traded."

Variant B: "Show \(P = Ae^{-Br}\) works / derive \(B(t,T)\)"

Steps: compute \(P_t, P_r, P_{rr}\) from the ansatz; substitute; collect powers of \(r\); obtain the two ODEs; solve \(\dot B = aB - 1\), \(B(T,T)=0\): \[\boxed{B(t,T) = \frac{1-e^{-a(T-t)}}{a}}\] (Ho-Lee/Merton special case \(a=0\): \(B = T-t\), \(P = e^{\frac{\sigma^2}{6}\tau^3 - \frac{\theta}{2}\tau^2 - \tau r}\).)

Variant C: "Compute a bond price / yield; find bond volatility or duration"

Bond price: plug \(r\), \(A\), \(B\) into \(P = Ae^{-Br}\). Yield: \(R(t,T) = -\frac{\ln P}{T-t} = -\frac{\ln A}{T-t} + \frac{B}{T-t}r\). Duration to \(r\): \(\hat D = B(t,T)\). Bond dynamics by Itô: \(dP = rP\,dt - sBP\,dz\) under \(Q\) (drift must be \(r\) — good check).

Variant D: "Compare models / discuss"

Structure your answer: (1) equilibrium (Vasicek, CIR — curve is output) vs. no-arbitrage (Ho-Lee, HW, BDT — curve is input); (2) Gaussian (analytic, negative rates) vs. square-root (positive rates, \(\chi^2\)) vs. lognormal (trees only); (3) one factor ⇒ perfectly correlated curve moves — motivates HJM/LMM and multi-factor HW; (4) calibration: HW to today's curve + cap or swaption vols.
HJM Model 9/21 exams
▼

Short-rate models drive the curve from one number \(r(t)\) — parsimonious but rigid (all yields perfectly correlated, calibration indirect). Heath-Jarrow-Morton (1992) flipped the perspective: model the entire instantaneous forward curve \(f(t,T)\), one process per maturity \(T\), simultaneously. Today's curve \(f(0,T)\) is the initial condition by construction — perfect fit for free. The question becomes: what dynamics can the forward curve have without creating arbitrage?

📋 Nøgleformler
Setup: bond prices under \(Q\) \[dP(t,T) = r(t)P(t,T)\,dt + v(t,T,\Omega_t)P(t,T)\,dz\] \(v(t,T,\Omega)\) = (possibly state-dependent) proportional volatility of the \(T\)-maturity ZCB; drift is \(r\) because discounted bond prices are \(Q\)-martingales. Boundary: \(v(t,t) = 0\) (a maturing bond has no risk — "pull to par").
Forward rate definitions \[f(t,T_1,T_2) = \frac{\ln P(t,T_1) - \ln P(t,T_2)}{T_2 - T_1},\qquad f(t,T) = -\frac{\partial\ln P(t,T)}{\partial T}\]
HJM forward-rate dynamics + THE drift condition (under \(Q\)) \[df(t,T) = m(t,T)\,dt + s(t,T)\,dz,\qquad \boxed{m(t,T) = s(t,T)\int_t^T s(t,\tau)\,d\tau}\] Equivalently \(df(t,T) = v(t,T)\,v_T(t,T)\,dt + v_T(t,T)\,dz\) with \(s = v_T = \partial v/\partial T\), \(v(t,T) = \int_t^Ts(t,\tau)d\tau\). The most cited formula of this chapter.
🎯 Fremgangsmåde til eksamen

When you see: "State/derive the HJM drift condition"

Step 1 — Write \(dP = rP\,dt + vP\,dz\) under \(Q\) and justify drift \(= r\) (discounted bonds are \(Q\)-martingales).
Step 2 — Itô: \(d\ln P = (r - \tfrac12v^2)dt + v\,dz\).
Step 3 — Forward rate = scaled difference of log bond prices; take the \(\Delta T\to0\) limit.
Step 4 — With \(s = v_T\) and \(v(t,t)=0\): \[\boxed{df(t,T) = \Bigl(s(t,T)\int_t^Ts(t,\tau)d\tau\Bigr)dt + s(t,T)\,dz}\] Add the interpretive sentence: "under \(Q\), specifying the volatility structure fully determines the drift — no arbitrage leaves no freedom."

Variant A: "Given \(s(t,T) = \ldots\), find the drift / the model"

Integrate: \(m = s(t,T)\int_t^Ts(t,\tau)d\tau\). Constants: \(s=\sigma \Rightarrow m = \sigma^2(T-t)\) (Ho-Lee). Exponential: \(s = \sigma e^{-a(T-t)} \Rightarrow m = \frac{\sigma^2}{a}e^{-a(T-t)}(1-e^{-a(T-t)})\) (Hull-White). Box the result and name the model.

Variant B: "Verify the drift condition in the Merton/Ho-Lee model"

From \(P = \exp[\frac{\sigma^2}6\tau^3 - \frac\theta2\tau^2 - \tau r]\): compute \(f = -\partial_T\ln P = -\frac{\sigma^2}2\tau^2 + \theta\tau + r\); differentiate in \(t\), insert \(dr\); show \[\boxed{df = \sigma^2(T-t)dt + \sigma dz}\] and remark that \(\theta\) drops out — "the drift depends only on volatility".

Variant C: "Discuss HJM: pros, cons, implementation"

Pros: fits today's curve by construction; unifying framework (all models = vol choices); multi-factor natural. Cons: models unobservable instantaneous forwards; generally non-Markov (\(t\) inside and outside the stochastic integral) ⇒ non-recombining trees ⇒ Monte Carlo; lognormal vols explode. Markov exceptions: Ho-Lee/Hull-White vol structures. Practice: short-rate models or LMM cover the useful cases; HJM is the reference theory.
LMM / LIBOR Market Model 8/21 exams
▼

HJM's state variable — the instantaneous forward rate — is a theoretical idealisation; nobody quotes it. The LIBOR Market Model (BGM) instead models the discrete, simply-compounded forward rates \(F_k(t) = F(t; t_k, t_{k+1})\) actually observable in FRAs and swaps, on a tenor grid \(t_0, t_1, \ldots\) with accruals \(\tau_k = t_{k+1} - t_k\). Its killer feature: it is the rigorous model behind the market's Black formula habit. Market practice prices caplets with Black's formula, implicitly assuming lognormal forward rates. No short-rate model reproduces that exactly. The LMM does — by design.

📋 Nøgleformler
Objects and notation Tenor dates \(t_0\lt t_1\lt\cdots\lt t_{n+1}\), accruals \(\tau_k = t_{k+1}-t_k\); \(m(t)\) = index of next reset date after \(t\). \[F_k(t) = F(t;t_k,t_{k+1}) = \frac{1}{\tau_k}\Bigl(\frac{P(t,t_k)}{P(t,t_{k+1})}-1\Bigr),\qquad F_k(t_k) = L(t_k,t_{k+1})\ \text{(spot rate at fixing)}\] \[1 + \tau_kF_k(t) = \frac{P(t,t_k)}{P(t,t_{k+1})}\] \(\zeta_k(t)\) = instantaneous vol of \(F_k\); \(\nu_k(t)\) = vol of \(P(t,t_k)\).
Core dynamics: each rate driftless under its own measure \[dF_k(t) = \zeta_k(t)F_k(t)\,dz\qquad\text{under }Q^{k+1}\ (\text{numeraire }P(t,t_{k+1})),\ t\le t_k\] Deterministic \(\zeta_k\) ⇒ \(F_k(t_k)\) lognormal ⇒ Black caplet formula exact.
Black caplet formula in LMM notation \[\text{Caplet}_k = N\,P(0,t_{k+1})\,\tau_k\bigl(F_k(0)\Phi(d_1) - R_K\Phi(d_2)\bigr),\quad d_1 = \frac{\ln(F_k/R_K)+\tfrac12\sigma_k^2t_k}{\sigma_k\sqrt{t_k}}\] \[\sigma_k^2\,t_k = \int_0^{t_k}\zeta_k(t)^2\,dt\] \(\sigma_k\) = implied (Black) caplet vol; flat vols (one per cap) vs. spot vols (one per caplet, bootstrapped).
🎯 Fremgangsmåde til eksamen

When you see: "Explain the LMM / why is Black's caplet formula consistent?"

Step 1 — Define the objects: tenor grid, \(F_k(t) = \frac1{\tau_k}(\frac{P(t,t_k)}{P(t,t_{k+1})}-1)\), \(F_k(t_k) = L(t_k,t_{k+1})\).
Step 2 — Martingale argument: \(F_k\) = traded portfolio / numeraire \(P(t,t_{k+1})\) ⇒ driftless under \(Q^{k+1}\): \(dF_k = \zeta_kF_k\,dz\).
Step 3 — Assume deterministic \(\zeta_k\) ⇒ lognormal \(F_k(t_k)\) with total variance \(\sigma_k^2t_k = \int_0^{t_k}\zeta_k^2dt\); apply \(f_0 = P(0,t_{k+1})E^{k+1}[\text{payoff}]\) + lognormal lemma: \[\boxed{\text{Caplet} = N\tau_kP(0,t_{k+1})(F_k\Phi(d_1) - R_K\Phi(d_2))}\]

Variant A: "Derive/state the drift under the rolling forward risk-neutral measure"

Steps: (1) change-of-numeraire drift rule \((\sigma^h-\sigma^g)\sigma^f\); (2) vol relation \(\nu_i - \nu_{i+1} = \frac{\tau_i\zeta_iF_i}{1+\tau_iF_i}\) from \(\ln(1+\tau_iF_i)\); (3) telescope: \[\boxed{\frac{dF_k}{F_k} = \zeta_k\sum_{i=m(t)}^k\frac{\tau_i\zeta_iF_i}{1+\tau_iF_i}\,dt + \zeta_k\,dz}\] Comment: stochastic drift ⇒ Monte Carlo; driftless again once \(m(t) = k+1\).

Variant B: "Compare LMM with HJM / short-rate models"

Key contrasts: observable simple rates vs. unobservable instantaneous ones; Black caplet formula exact vs. impossible; vol parameters directly interpretable (caplet vols) vs. needing recalibration mapping; both HJM and LMM fit today's curve by construction and are simulated by MC; short-rate models remain useful for trees/PDE and American-style products. Drift conditions mirror each other: integral (HJM) ↔ sum (LMM).

Variant C: "Calibration / implementation question"

Calibrate \(\zeta_k\) to caplet (spot) vols via \(\sigma_k^2t_k = \int_0^{t_k}\zeta_k^2dt\); correlations / factor structure to swaption vols; reduce dimensionality with parametric vol forms or PCA factors. Implementation: log-Euler Monte Carlo of all rates jointly under one measure (rolling CD numeraire); price cash flows pathwise. Mention: lognormal ⇒ no negative rates ⇒ shifted-lognormal variants in low-rate environments.
Credit Risk 13/21 exams
▼

Everything so far assumed cash flows arrive for sure. Credit risk drops that assumption: a borrower may default, and we must price both the probability of that event and the loss given default. Two modelling philosophies exist, and exams test both:

📋 Nøgleformler
Hazard rate and survival probability \[\frac{\partial V(t,T)}{\partial T} = -\lambda(t,T)V(t,T)\quad\Longrightarrow\quad V(t,T) = V(0,t)\,e^{-\int_t^T\lambda(t,\tau)d\tau}\] \(V(t,T)\) = survival probability to \(T\) seen at \(t\); \(\lambda\) = hazard rate (default intensity, forward default rate). Equivalently \(\lambda(t,T) = -\frac{\partial}{\partial T}\ln V(t,T)\). Analogy: \(\lambda \leftrightarrow f(t,T)\), \(V \leftrightarrow P(t,T)\).
Cumulative default probability \[Q(t,T) = 1 - V(t,T) = 1 - e^{-\bar\lambda(t,T)(T-t)}\ \ (\text{from }t,\ V(0,t)=1)\] \(\bar\lambda\) = average hazard rate. Constant hazard: \(Q(t) = 1 - e^{-\lambda t}\). Unconditional PD over \([T, T+\Delta T]\): \(V(T) - V(T+\Delta T)\); conditional: \(\frac{V(T)-V(T+\Delta T)}{V(T)}\).
Credit spread ↔ hazard rate (the workhorse) \[\lambda \approx \frac{s}{1-R}\] \(s\) = credit spread (per year), \(R\) = recovery rate. Zero-recovery risky ZCB: \(B_0 = P(0,T)V(0,T)\), so spread \(= \bar\lambda\). Most-used numeric formula in credit exam questions.
🎯 Fremgangsmåde til eksamen

When you see: "Compute default probabilities from spreads / hazard rates"

Step 1 — Back out the hazard rate: \(\lambda = \dfrac{s}{1-R}\) (state the approximation).
Step 2 — Survival to \(t\): \(V(t) = e^{-\lambda t}\); cumulative PD: \(Q(t) = 1 - e^{-\lambda t}\).
Step 3 — For a specific year: unconditional PD\((t_1,t_2) = V(t_1) - V(t_2)\); conditional PD \(= 1 - e^{-\lambda(t_2-t_1)}\). Read the question carefully — conditional vs. unconditional is the trap. \[\boxed{Q(t) = 1 - e^{-st/(1-R)}}\]

Variant A: "Price a CDS / find the fair spread"

Steps: (1) survival curve \(V(t_i)\) from given \(\lambda\); (2) \(A = \sum\tau_iP(0,t_i)V(t_i)\); (3) \(C = (1-R)\sum P(0,t^{mid}_i)(V(t_{i-1})-V(t_i))\); (4) \(B\) = accrual (half-period) if required; \[\boxed{s = \frac{C}{A+B}}\] MTM of existing CDS: \((s_{mkt}-s_{contract})\times(A+B)\), sign per position.

Variant B: "Merton model — value equity/debt, find PD and spread"
