Consider a derivative with current value \(f\) and values \((f_u, f_d)\) one step forward in the tree depending on whether the stock price moves up to \(S_u = uS\) or down to \(S_d = dS\), when the current stock price is \(S\). Construct a portfolio of \(\Delta\) stocks. If you choose \[\Delta = \frac{f_u - f_d}{S_u - S_d}\] it will cost \(\Delta S\) at the current time. The position of a short derivative position and long the delta stock position will be risk free, i.e. have the same payoff one period later, namely \(f_u - \frac{f_u - f_d}{u-d}u = f_d - \frac{f_u - f_d}{u-d}d\). The current cost of this risk free position of the derivative and delta stock is \(f - \frac{f_u - f_d}{u-d}\), which can be borrowed and repaid by a factor of \(a := e^r\) one period later. Thus to avoid arbitrage opportunities we must have \[\Bigl[f - \frac{f_u - f_d}{u-d}u\Bigr]e^{-r} = f - \frac{f_u - f_d}{u-d}\] Solve this for \(f\) to get: \(f = a^{-1}\Bigl(\frac{a-d}{u-d}f_u + \frac{u-a}{u-d}f_d\Bigr)\). Define \(q = \frac{a-d}{u-d}\) and then \(f = e^{-r}[qf_u + (1-q)f_d]\). Using backward induction you can find an explicit formula for the call premium at time 0 as \[f_0 = a^{-2}\bigl(q^2 f_{uu} + 2q(1-q)f_{ud} + (1-q)^2 f_{dd}\bigr)\] where \(f_{uu} = \max[0;\, u^2S_0 - K]\), \(f_{ud} = \max[0;\, udS_0 - K]\), and \(f_{dd} = \max[0;\, d^2S_0 - K]\). The real world probability does not enter directly into the formula because that information is already embedded in the prices of the traded instruments that do enter into the formula.
(b) The state prices are the discounted risk neutral probabilities i.e. \(\psi_u = e^{-r}q\) and \(\psi_d = e^{-r}(1-q)\) (thus \(f = \psi_u f_u + \psi_d f_d\)).
(c) We will analyze whether an early exercise is optimal at time \(t = 1\). Let us take outset in the case that this is after an initial up-move (as the argument below will show this is not essential). The analysis is only relevant if the option is in the money, e.g. \(uS_0 > K\). We have just found that the value of the call in this situation is \(f_u = e^{-r}[qf_{uu} + (1-q)f_{ud}]\) and this value can, if wanted, be hedged by a short stock position to a risk free position with this resulting value (or it can be sold). Thus an exercise is only optimal if \(uS_0 - K > e^{-r}[qf_{uu} + (1-q)f_{ud}]\). Now \(S_u = uS_0 = e^{-r}(qS_{uu} + (1-q)S_{ud})\) so this requirement is equivalent to \(qS_{uu} + (1-q)S_{ud} - e^r K > qf_{uu} + (1-q)f_{ud}\). If the option is in the money in both the uu and the ud state then the requirement is \(qS_{uu} + (1-q)S_{ud} - e^r K > q(S_{uu}-K) + (1-q)(S_{ud}-K)\) or \(e^r K < K\), i.e. not with positive interest rates. If the option is in the money only in the uu-state i.e. \(S_{ud} < K\) then the requirement becomes \((1-q)S_{ud} > (e^r - q)K > (e^r - q)S_{ud}\) implying \(1 > e^r\), which also does not hold with a positive rate. Thus with a positive interest rate an early exercise of an American option on a stock with no dividends is not optimal.

► Show step-by-step solution
STEP-BY-STEP
📖 Study Guide: Binomial Model   |   Flashcards: review these topics in the Flashcards tab

(a) European call in 2-period recombining binomial tree

Step 1 — Risk-neutral probability: \(q = \dfrac{e^r - d}{u - d}\)

Step 2 — Terminal stock prices at T=2: \[S_{uu}=u^2S_0,\quad S_{ud}=udS_0,\quad S_{dd}=d^2S_0\]

Step 3 — Call payoffs at T=2: \[C_{uu}=\max(u^2S_0-K,0),\quad C_{ud}=\max(udS_0-K,0),\quad C_{dd}=0\]

Step 4 — Work backwards to T=0: \[c = e^{-2r}\bigl[q^2C_{uu}+2q(1-q)C_{ud}+(1-q)^2C_{dd}\bigr]\] \[\boxed{c = e^{-2r}\bigl[q^2\max(u^2S_0-K,0)+2q(1-q)\max(udS_0-K,0)\bigr]}\]

Real-world \(p\) does not appear: risk-neutral pricing uses \(q\) derived from no-arbitrage, not from subjective beliefs about up/down moves.

(b) State prices (Arrow-Debreu prices) at \(t=0\) for payments at \(t=1\)

Step 1 — Definition: \(\psi_u\) = price at \(t=0\) of £1 paid in the up-state at \(t=1\), £0 otherwise. Similarly \(\psi_d\).

Step 2 — System of equations: Bond (pays 1 in both states): \(e^{-r} = \psi_u + \psi_d\)
Stock (pays \(uS_0\) up, \(dS_0\) down): \(S_0 = u S_0\psi_u + d S_0\psi_d\)

Step 3 — Solve: From bond: \(\psi_u + \psi_d = e^{-r}\). Divide stock by \(S_0\): \(u\psi_u + d\psi_d = 1\). Subtract \(d\times\) bond from stock equation: \((u-d)\psi_u = 1 - de^{-r}\) \[\boxed{\psi_u = \frac{1-de^{-r}}{u-d} = e^{-r}q,\qquad \psi_d = e^{-r}(1-q) = \frac{ue^{-r}-1}{u-d}}\]

(c) American call vs European call — no-arbitrage argument

Step 1 — Value of unexercised call: At any node, the continuation value \(e^{-r}[qC_u+(1-q)C_d]\) exceeds the intrinsic value \(S-K\) when \(r\geq 0\).

Step 2 — Argument: Instead of exercising early (getting \(S_t - K\)), hold the call. The unexercised call is worth at least \(S_t - Ke^{-r(T-t)} \geq S_t - K\) when \(r\geq 0\). Early exercise sacrifices the interest earned on \(K\). \[\boxed{\text{American call} = \text{European call for non-dividend-paying stock when }r\geq 0}\]

Question 2

Consider European call and put options on the same futures contract. Both options have strike price \(K\) and expiry at time \(T\). The futures contract expires after that time. Assume that there is a constant, continuously compounded, risk free interest rate of \(r\). Let the futures price at time \(t\) be \(F_t\) (You may ignore margining and treat the futures as forward contracts).
(a) Derive the put-call parity for the futures options for time \(t < T\).
(b) Use the put-call parity to find the relationship between
1. The delta of the European futures call and the delta of the European futures put (use the delta with respect to the futures price rather than the spot price)
2. The gamma of the European futures call and the gamma of the European futures put
3. The theta of the European futures call and the theta of the European futures put and comment on the results.

► Show official solution
OFFICIAL MARKING GUIDE

(a) See Hull section 17.4 and 18.4. Let \(t \leq T\) and let \(c_t\) and \(p_t\) be the prices of the call and the put, respectively. Consider the portfolio (A) of the call and \(e^{-r(T-t)}K\) placed at interest and the portfolio (B) of a put, a long position in the future with expiry at \(T\) and \(e^{-r(T-t)}F_t\) placed at interest. At maturity the portfolios will have the payouts: \[A: \max[F_T - K;\, 0] + K\] \[B: \max[K - F_T;\, 0] + (F_T - F_t) + F_t\] which are identical. Barring arbitrage we thus must have \[c_t + e^{-r(T-t)}K = p_t + e^{-r(T-t)}F_t\]
(b) See Hull chapter 19. The different Greeks shall be defined as partial derivatives, and the put-call-parity gives the relationships. The Delta of an option is the partial derivative with respect to (in this case) the futures price. The relationship between the Deltas for otherwise identical calls and puts is \[\Delta_c - \Delta_p = e^{-r(T-t)}\] i.e. dependent on the interest rate (and thus slightly different from the case with the spot price delta, where it is dependent on the dividend rate). The Gamma of an option is the second partial derivative with respect to the underlying. As can be seen from the result on Deltas, the Gammas on the two options will be identical. The Theta of an option is the partial derivative with respect to calendar time (not the expiry date). The relationship between the Thetas for otherwise identical calls and puts is \[\Theta_c - \Theta_p = re^{-r(T-t)}(F_t - K)\] i.e. the Thetas are identical for an at-the-money call and similar put and generally close for low interest rates.

► Show step-by-step solution
STEP-BY-STEP
📖 Study Guide: Put-Call Parity  ·  Forward Pricing   |   Flashcards: review these topics in the Flashcards tab

(a) Put-call parity for futures options

Step 1 — Portfolio A vs Portfolio B at time \(t \leq T\)
Portfolio A: long call + \(e^{-r(T-t)}K\) cash.
Portfolio B: long put + long futures position + \(e^{-r(T-t)}F_t\) cash.

Step 2 — Payoffs at expiry T \[A: \max(F_T-K,0) + K\] \[B: \max(K-F_T,0) + (F_T - F_t) + F_t = \max(K-F_T,0) + F_T\] Both equal \(\max(F_T, K)\). Identical payoffs.

Step 3 — No-arbitrage \[\boxed{c_t + Ke^{-r(T-t)} = p_t + F_t e^{-r(T-t)}}\] or \(c_t - p_t = (F_t - K)e^{-r(T-t)}\).

(b) Greeks from put-call parity for futures

Step 1 — Differentiate \(c - p = (F_t-K)e^{-r(T-t)}\) w.r.t. \(F_t\) \[\Delta_c - \Delta_p = e^{-r(T-t)}\]

Step 2 — Differentiate again \[\Gamma_c = \Gamma_p\]

Step 3 — Differentiate w.r.t. \(t\) \[\Theta_c - \Theta_p = r(F_t-K)e^{-r(T-t)}\]

Question 3

(a) In the Ho-Lee Model in continuous time the (instantaneous) short term interest rate \(r\) is described by the process: \[dr = \theta(t)\,dt + \sigma\,dz\] where \(\theta(t)\) is a function of \(t\), \(\sigma\) is constant, and \(dz\) the increment of a Brownian motion. What does this mean for the behaviour of the short term interest rate?
(b) Here as in other models of the short term interest rate \(r\) the drift rate is made a function of calendar time. What is the purpose of the extra flexibility compared to the CIR (or Vasicek) type of models?
(c) In this model the solution for the price of a zero-coupon bond can be written \[P(t,T) = A(t,T)e^{-r(t)(T-t)}\] Use Itô's lemma to derive the volatility of \(P(t,T)\) and comment on the result.
(d) Derive the duration of the bond (using the sensitivity to the short rate rather than the yield of the bond).

► Show official solution
OFFICIAL MARKING GUIDE

(a) Cfr. Hull section 32.1. It should be noted that the short term rates at any time are normally distributed with a constant volatility, but time-varying mean. In particular it does not rule out negative interest rates.
(b) The time-dependent drift term in these models is introduced to be able to incorporate a given, initial term structure, making the values derived from it “arbitrage-free” in relation to the existing securities priced on the current term structure (assuming these to be arbitrage free). This is in contrast to the CIR and Vasicek models of the “Equilibrium”-type that put restrictions on the possible initial term structure (as it is a function with few parameters and of the current spot rate only).
(c) Using the form of the solution for the zero coupon bond price we find that \[\frac{\partial P}{\partial r} = -(T-t)P\] Thus (from Itô's lemma) the volatility term process for \(dP\) is \((T-t)\sigma P\) (as the sign does not matter). The volatility decreases linearly to zero with the remaining time to maturity on the Zero Coupon Bond.
(d) Cf. Hull Section 31.2. With one factor rate models we can define a duration measure with respect to the short rate rather (as most often) the yield of the bond. You then get \[D = -\frac{\partial P/\partial r}{P} = (T-t)\] (In this case this is equal to the traditional duration measure of a Zero Coupon Bond).

► Show step-by-step solution
STEP-BY-STEP
📖 Study Guide: Short Rate Models   |   Flashcards: review these topics in the Flashcards tab

(a) Ho-Lee model — behaviour of short rate

Step 1 — Model: \(dr = \theta(t)\,dt + \sigma\,dz\). Drift = time-varying \(\theta(t)\), constant volatility \(\sigma\). No mean reversion. Normal increments → can go negative.

Step 2 — Role of \(\theta(t)\): Calibrated to fit the current observed term structure exactly. \[\boxed{\theta(t) = f^M_t(0,t) + \sigma^2 t}\]

(b) Purpose of time-dependent drift

Step 1: Vasicek/CIR (equilibrium) use constant parameters → cannot match current market term structure.

Step 2: Ho-Lee/Hull-White (no-arbitrage/fitting) choose \(\theta(t)\) to exactly reproduce current bond prices. Makes model arbitrage-free w.r.t. currently observed yield curve. \[\boxed{\text{No-arbitrage model: fits initial term structure exactly. Equilibrium model: may not.}}\]

(c) Volatility of \(P(t,T)\) via Itô's lemma

Step 1 — Bond price form for Ho-Lee: \(P(t,T) = A(t,T)e^{-r(t)(T-t)}\). The \(r\)-sensitivity factor is \(B(t,T) = T-t\) (no mean reversion, so \(B=T-t\)).

Step 2 — Apply Itô's lemma to \(P(r,t)\) with \(dr = \theta(t)dt + \sigma dz\): \[dP = \frac{\partial P}{\partial t}dt + \frac{\partial P}{\partial r}dr + \tfrac{1}{2}\frac{\partial^2 P}{\partial r^2}(dr)^2\] Substitute \(dr = \theta(t)\,dt + \sigma\,dz\) and \((dr)^2 = \sigma^2\,dt\); the question only asks for the volatility, so collect just the \(dz\)-term (all \(dt\)-terms — from \(\partial P/\partial t\), from \(\partial P/\partial r\cdot\theta(t)\), and from the \(\tfrac{1}{2}\partial^2P/\partial r^2\cdot\sigma^2\) term — are drift and can be left aggregated): \[dP = (\text{drift terms})\,dt + \frac{\partial P}{\partial r}\cdot\sigma\,dz\] Diffusion (stochastic) term only: \(\dfrac{\partial P}{\partial r}\cdot\sigma\,dz = -(T-t)P\cdot\sigma\,dz\)

Step 3 — Volatility of \(P\): \[\boxed{\sigma_P = \sigma(T-t)P}\] The percentage volatility of the bond price = \(\sigma(T-t)\). This grows with time-to-maturity: longer bonds are more volatile, consistent with intuition.

(d) Duration of the Ho-Lee bond

Step 1 — From \(\partial P/\partial r = -(T-t)P\): \[D = -\frac{1}{P}\frac{\partial P}{\partial r} = T-t\] \[\boxed{D = T-t}\] Same as standard Macaulay duration for a zero-coupon bond. Unlike Vasicek, there is no mean reversion to dampen interest rate sensitivity — duration equals time-to-maturity.

August 2023 — Re-Exam

Pricing of Financial Assets · University of Copenhagen · 3 questions · 3 hours · No aids permitted

Question 1

Assume that the price of a non-dividend paying stock \(S\) can be modelled (under the original probability measure \(P\)) by the geometric Brownian motion \[dS = \mu S\,dt + \sigma S\,dz\] where \(\mu\) and \(\sigma > 0\) are constants, and where \(dt\) and \(dz\) are the standard shorthand notations for a small time-step and a Brownian increment.
(a) Describe the qualitative characteristics of this model, and discuss its possible shortcomings as a model of a stock.
(b) Consider the transformation \(G\) of \(S\) given by the natural logarithm (ln), i.e. \(G(x) = \ln(x)\). Use Itô's lemma to find the process followed by \(G(S)\).
(c) At time \(t\) consider a forward contract on the stock maturing at time \(T > t\). Compare the pricing of a call option on the stock with a pricing of a call on the forward contract on the stock both maturing at \(T\) and having the same strike \(K\).

► Show official solution
OFFICIAL MARKING GUIDE

(a) The model describes the stochastic movement of the stock price with a drift proportional to the stock price with a constant drift parameter \(\mu\) and with the stochastic behaviour driven by the Brownian increment that is giving a variance rate that is also proportional to the stock price with a constant volatility parameter \(\sigma\). The nature of the Brownian increment implies, inter alia, that the sample paths of prices are (with probability one) continuous. They are also very “wiggly”. The resulting price distribution will be lognormal and the distribution of return normal; the latter with expected return and variance proportional to the elapsed time. The student may also note that the model does not adhere to empirical sample paths — especially for a single stock — may show large jumps (e.g. at the publication of new information), and that realized returns empirically tend to show heteroscedasticity at odds with the given normal distribution. The student may also note that empirical return data tend to show negative skewness and excess kurtosis, but this is not directly covered by the syllabus (but at the lectures and in other parts of Hull).
(b) The use of Itô's lemma is straightforward. The partial derivative of the \(G\) w.r.t. time is 0, and w.r.t. \(x\) you find \(\frac{\partial G}{\partial x} = 1/x\) and \(\frac{\partial^2 G}{\partial x^2} = -1/x^2\). Thus with the given process for the underlying \(S\) you get from Itô's lemma: \[dG(S) = \bigl(\mu - \sigma^2/2\bigr)dt + \sigma\,dz\] thus taking the geometric Brownian motion for the stock price to a Brownian motion (with a constant drift rate and a constant variance rate) for the stock return (on this transformation, see Hull section 14.7).
(c) First the answer should note the definition of a forward contract, in particular that it has no payments before maturity. Then the answer should cover the forward price that will be agreed barring arbitrage: With an assumption of a constant risk free interest rate this is for the forward price \(F_t\) contracted at time \(t \leq T\), where \(T\) is the maturity of the contract, \[F_t = E^Q[S_T] = S_t e^{r(T-t)}\] where we only for the last equation use the assumption of no dividends. Last the answer should note that \(F\) follows a Geometric Brownian Motion similar to that derived earlier for \(S\), but that the drift rate of returns is \(\mu - r\) rather than the \(\mu\) for the stock. Under the Q-measure the drift rates will be 0 and \(r\), respectively. Thus comparing two call options with the same strike, but where the first is on the forward contract and the second is on the stock, \[c^F = e^{-r(T-t)}E^Q\bigl[\max(F_T - K;\, 0)\bigr] = e^{-r(T-t)}E^Q\bigl[\max(S_T - K;\, 0)\bigr] = c^S\] since they will have the same payout at maturity, even if the forward price initially (with a positive interest rate) is higher than the spot price. This may be verified by looking at the formulae for call prices on stocks and forwards (if you e.g. substitute the relation between forward and spot prices into the Black formula you get the Black-Scholes formula for a call), (see Hull 17.3).

► Show step-by-step solution
STEP-BY-STEP
📖 Study Guide: GBM  ·  Forward Pricing   |   Flashcards: review these topics in the Flashcards tab

(a) GBM — qualitative characteristics and shortcomings

Characteristics:
Continuous sample paths. Proportional drift (\(\mu\)) and volatility (\(\sigma\)) — both constant.
Lognormal prices, normal returns. Returns are independent and identically distributed (Markov).
Mean and variance of returns proportional to elapsed time.

Shortcomings:
1. Real prices can jump — continuous paths are an approximation.
2. Constant volatility — empirically, volatility clusters and varies (implied vol smiles).
3. Normal returns — empirical returns have fat tails and negative skewness.
4. No dividends incorporated by default.

(b) Itô's lemma on \(G(S_t) = \ln S_t\)

Step 1 — Partial derivatives \[\frac{\partial G}{\partial S} = \frac{1}{S},\quad \frac{\partial^2 G}{\partial S^2} = -\frac{1}{S^2},\quad \frac{\partial G}{\partial t} = 0\]

Step 2 — Apply Itô, use \((dS)^2 = \sigma^2 S^2\,dt\) \[d\ln S = \frac{1}{S}(\mu S\,dt + \sigma S\,dz) + \tfrac{1}{2}\left(-\frac{1}{S^2}\right)\sigma^2 S^2\,dt\] \[\boxed{d\ln S = \bigl(\mu - \tfrac{1}{2}\sigma^2\bigr)dt + \sigma\,dz}\]

Step 3 — Integrate: distribution of \(\ln S_T\) \[\boxed{\ln S_T \sim N\!\left(\ln S_0 + (\mu-\tfrac{1}{2}\sigma^2)T,\; \sigma^2 T\right)}\]

(c) Forward price for stock following GBM — no dividends

Step 1 — Definition: Forward price \(F_t\) = price agreed at \(t\) for delivery at \(T\), zero value at inception.

Step 2 — Under Q, expected stock price: \(E^Q[S_T] = S_t e^{r(T-t)}\)

Step 3 — No-arbitrage forward price: \[\boxed{F_t = S_t\,e^{r(T-t)}}\] Value of existing forward with delivery price \(K\): \(V = e^{-r(T-t)}(F_t - K)\).

Question 2

Consider a Credit Default Swap (CDS).
(a) Give a precise description of the CDS contract its payment structure.
(b) What is the difference between a cash CDO (Collateralized Debt Obligation) and a synthetic CDO?
(c) Explain the payment structure of a tranched CDS or CDO, and define the terms attachment point and detachment point.
(d) Consider a tranched CDS or CDO on a large portfolio on underlying reference bonds (names). For a given average level of credit risk, e.g. expressed by the average credit spread on the underlying portfolio, explain how different levels of the assessed correlation of defaults of the issuers will influence the relative pricing of the tranches.

► Show official solution
OFFICIAL MARKING GUIDE

(a) Ref. Hull Section 25.1. A formal definition including key concepts is: Definition (Credit Default Swap). A Credit Default Swap (CDS) is a contract between a protection seller and a protection buyer based on a specified Nominal Principal. The protection buyer pays a running premium (the CDS spread) until maturity of the contract or a Credit Event on the Reference Entity of the CDS. At a Credit Event the protection seller pays a Specified Amount (Fixed or (1-Recovery) times the Nominal Principal) to the protection buyer. The Reference Entity is typically a company or sovereign state (with reference to specific loans or bonds issued). The Credit Event of the definition can be bankruptcy, a failure to pay, or a debt restructuring. The payment in a Credit is meant to reflect a situation where the protection buyer delivers the defaulted bonds or loans to the protection seller and receives the Notional Principal. Usually the CDS specifies a number of different bonds or loans that can be delivered, which gives the protection buyer a “Cheapest-to-Deliver” option. In practise there is often specified an auction procedure to determine the value of cheapest-to-deliver bonds or loans, and the (1-Recovery) is paid as a net cash transfer rather than through delivery. Also the (1-Recovery) may be specified as a Fixed amount in the contract.
(b) A cash CDO is an example of an Asset Backed Security where the underlying assets are bonds. A synthetic CDO (or a tranched CDS) is based on a portfolio of CDS-contracts where protection is sold. In both cases the credit risk from the underlying or referenced portfolio (of bonds or derivatives) is transferred into different instruments (tranches) of different credit risk (or “seniority”), as explained below.
(c) Ref. Hull Section 25.8–10. The cash flows to the debt tranches in the CDO (or tranched CDS) is defined through a waterfall structure, so that the most senior debt tranches of the CDO are most likely to receive promised interest payments and repayment of principal. The most junior debt tranche is called the “Equity”-tranche and receives the residual if and when all other tranches have been repaid in full. The Equity-tranche is typically not rated.
(d) This discussion is covered in Hull section 25.9. Note that the credit risk on the underlying portfolio is assumed constant. Thus the correlation will only influence the distribution of losses between tranches. A low level of correlation will make it likely that the losses can be absorbed by the tranches low in the capital structure in most states of the world whereas a high correlation will make it more likely that even high tranches occasionally will be hit. Thus for a given average level of credit risk the equity tranche will benefit from an increase in the perceived correlation and the highest (super senior) tranche will suffer. The effect on mezzanine tranches can be ambiguous.

► Show step-by-step solution
STEP-BY-STEP
📖 Study Guide: Credit Risk   |   Flashcards: review these topics in the Flashcards tab

(a) Credit Default Swap (CDS)

Step 1 — Define the contract
CDS: bilateral contract on a reference entity with nominal \(N\).
Two parties: protection buyer and protection seller.

Step 2 — Payment structure
Before credit event: buyer pays CDS spread \(s \times N\) periodically.
At credit event (default, failure-to-pay, restructuring): seller pays \((1-R)\times N\) to buyer.
\[\boxed{\text{CDS spread} \approx \lambda(1-R) \text{ where } \lambda = \text{hazard rate}}\]

(b) Cash CDO vs synthetic CDO

Step 1 — Cash CDO: SPV buys reference assets (bonds/loans) directly. Tranches funded by issuing notes backed by these assets. Physical transfer of assets.

Step 2 — Synthetic CDO: SPV does NOT hold assets. Credit risk transferred via CDS contracts. No physical transfer — only risk is tranched and sold. Cheaper to create.

(c) Tranched CDS/CDO — structure

Step 1 — Waterfall loss allocation
Losses flow bottom-up: equity → mezzanine → senior.
Attachment point: loss % at which tranche starts absorbing losses.
Detachment point: loss % at which tranche is fully wiped out.
Example: equity 0–3%, mezzanine 3–7%, senior 7–100%.

(d) Correlation effect on tranches

Step 1 — Low correlation: defaults diversified → junior absorbs losses, senior safe → senior spread low.

Step 2 — High correlation: lumpy losses → senior also hit → senior spread rises.

Question 3

Let \(P(t,t_k)\) denote the price at \(t\) of a zero coupon bond that matures at time \(t_k\). Let \(F_k(t)\) be the simple forward rate between times \(t_k\) and \(t_{k+1}\) that can be contracted for at time \(t \leq t_k < t_{k+1}\), and let \(\tau_k\) be the compounding period between \(t_k\) and \(t_{k+1}\).
(a) Consider a forward rate \(F_k\). How can this be expressed as a function of prices of zero coupon bonds?
(b) Assume that the forward rates are driven by one factor. What choice of numeraire will lead to the forward rate \(F_k(t)\) being a martingale, e.g. of the form \[dF_k(t) = \zeta_k(t)F_k(t)\,dz\] where \(dz\) is the standard shorthand notation for the change of a Brownian motion.
(c) The market models of interest rates describes the evolution of the term structure of simple interest rates. In the LMM-model a rolling numeraire is used. Explaining in general terms how this is constructed (explicit formulas are not required, but you should discuss change of numeraires).

► Show official solution
OFFICIAL MARKING GUIDE

(a) We define or see as an arbitrage relationship \[1 + \tau_k F_k = \frac{P(t,t_k)}{P(t,t_{k+1})}\] One interpretation is that the (simple) forward rate at time \(t\) for future dates \(t_k < t_{k+1}\) is the interest rate that you implicitly can contract for bringing a value at time \(t_k\) forward with that interest rate (linearly, or simply multiplied by the length of the time interval) to a value at time \(t_{k+1}\).
(b) Ref. Hull, Section 31.7 (8th ed). The numeraire must be the zero coupon bond that is used for discounting when there is the payment on the forward rate contract, i.e. the numeraire \(P(t,t_{k+1})\). This is the choice that is said to be forward risk neutral with respect to this numeraire.
(c) In the market models there is a rolling change of numeraire where we use the next reset date of discretely compounded, i.e. simple, short term rates. If current date is \(t\) we use the notation \(m(t)\) for the next reset date. Using \(P(t,t_{m(t)})\) as a numeraire the forward rate will not be a martingale if \(m(t) < k\). But we know that the change in drift is determined by the difference in volatilities of the two numeraires (Hull, chapters 27 and 31, 8th ed). This constitutes a full answer. One may however also note that if we assume that zero coupon prices are driven by geometric Brownian motions, and let \(v_t\) denote the volatility of the zero coupon bond maturing at \(t\) we have \[dF_k(t) = \zeta_k(t)\bigl[v_{m(t)}(t) - v_{k+1}(t)\bigr]F_k(t)\,dt + \zeta_k(t)F_k(t)\,dz\]

► Show step-by-step solution
STEP-BY-STEP
📖 Study Guide: LIBOR Market Model   |   Flashcards: review these topics in the Flashcards tab

(a) Forward rate \(F_k(t)\) from zero-coupon bond prices

Step 1 — No-arbitrage: investing from \(t_k\) to \(t_{k+1}\)
Either invest at forward rate \(F_k\) (simple compounding) or roll bonds: \[1 + \tau_k F_k(t) = \frac{P(t,t_k)}{P(t,t_{k+1})}\]

Step 2 — Solve for \(F_k\) \[\boxed{F_k(t) = \frac{1}{\tau_k}\left(\frac{P(t,t_k)}{P(t,t_{k+1})} - 1\right)}\]

(b) Numeraire for \(F_k\) — LIBOR Market Model

Step 1 — Choose numeraire: Use \(P(t,t_{k+1})\) (bond maturing when rate is paid).

Step 2 — Result under \(t_{k+1}\)-forward measure \(Q^{k+1}\)
\(F_k(t)\) is a martingale (driftless): \[\boxed{dF_k = \sigma_k F_k\,d\tilde{z}^{k+1}}\] This is the key LMM result: forward rates are lognormal under their natural measure.

(c) Cap pricing in LMM — Black's formula

Step 1 — Cap = sum of caplets. Each caplet pays \(N\tau_k\max(F_k(t_k)-K,0)\) at \(t_{k+1}\).

Step 2 — Under \(Q^{k+1}\): \(F_k\) is lognormal → Black's formula \[\text{Caplet}_k = N\tau_k P(0,t_{k+1})\bigl[F_k(0)\Phi(d_1) - K\Phi(d_2)\bigr]\]

Step 3 — Where \[d_1 = \frac{\ln(F_k/K)+\tfrac{1}{2}\sigma_k^2 t_k}{\sigma_k\sqrt{t_k}},\quad d_2 = d_1-\sigma_k\sqrt{t_k}\] \[\boxed{\text{Cap} = \sum_k N\tau_k P(0,t_{k+1})\bigl[F_k\Phi(d_1^k)-K\Phi(d_2^k)\bigr]}\]

June 2022 — Ordinary Exam

Pricing of Financial Assets · University of Copenhagen · 3 questions · 3 hours · No aids permitted

Question 1

Let the time \(t\) spot exchange rate for a foreign currency be \(S_t\), denoting the value of one unit of the foreign currency as measured in the domestic currency. Assume that the exchange rate can be modelled (under the original probability measure \(P\)) by the geometric Brownian motion \[dS = \mu_S S\,dt + \sigma_S S\,dz\] where \(\mu_S\) and \(\sigma_S > 0\) are constants, and where \(dt\) and \(dz\) are the standard shorthand notations for a small time-step and a Brownian increment. Assume that the (continuously compounded) domestic and foreign risk free interest rates are constants \(r\) and \(r_f\), respectively.
(a) What will the drift rate of the exchange rate be under the domestic risk neutral probability measure (\(Q\)) and a no-arbitrage assumption? Comment on the influence of the difference of domestic and foreign interest rates on the result.
(b) Consider at time \(t < T\) a forward contract on the foreign currency for payment and delivery at time \(T\). What is the arbitrage free forward price \(F_t^T\)?
(c) Use Itô's lemma to show how the volatility of forward prices \(F_t^T\) evolves as time \(t\) approaches maturity \(T\).

► Show official solution
OFFICIAL MARKING GUIDE

(a) (Refer to Hull section 17.3, 17.5 and also Practise Question 17.2) The drift rate will be \((r - r_f)S\), i.e. determined by the domestic risk free interest rate less the foreign interest rate (analogous to a stock paying a continuous dividend rate). Under \(Q\) the currency with a high interest rate is expected to depreciate at a rate corresponding to the interest rate differential (Not named as such in the syllabus, but this is the uncovered interest rate parity).
(b) Consider two strategies to end with the foreign currency at time \(T\): A) Enter into a forward contract at time \(t\). You pay the agreed forward price \(F_t^T\) at time \(T\). B) Borrow \(S_t e^{-r_f(T-t)}\) at the domestic risk free rate and buy the currency spot placing on deposit at the foreign rate. At \(T\) you have accumulated one unit of the foreign currency at the deposit. You repay the loan that have accumulated to \(S_t e^{(r-r_f)(T-t)}\). In the absence of arbitrage opportunities the forward price contracted at \(t\) thus must be \(F_t^T = S_t e^{(r-r_f)(T-t)}\) (the covered interest rate parity).
(c) There are two different approaches to answering the question, either of which will be considered as valid: For a family of forward contracts of continually shortened maturity the volatility can be found from the previous formula by Itô's lemma to be \(\frac{\partial F_t^T}{\partial S_t}\sigma_S S_t = e^{(r-r_f)(T-t)}S_t\sigma_S\). Thus it goes to the volatility of the currency as the maturity of the forward goes to zero. For a forward contract contracted at time \(t\) at the forward price \(K = F_t^T\) the value of that contract will develop as \(V(S,t;K) = Se^{-r_f(T-t)} - Ke^{-r(T-t)}\) with volatility only arising from the first term. Also this goes to the volatility of the currency as the maturity of the forward goes to zero.

► Show step-by-step solution
STEP-BY-STEP
📖 Study Guide: GBM  ·  Forward Pricing   |   Flashcards: review these topics in the Flashcards tab

(a) Drift of exchange rate \(S\) under domestic Q

Step 1 — Analogy with dividend-paying stock
Foreign currency earns \(r_f\) continuously (like a dividend yield). Under Q: drift + \(r_f = r\). \[\boxed{dS = (r-r_f)S\,dt + \sigma_S S\,dz^Q}\] If \(r > r_f\): currency expected to appreciate. If \(r < r_f\): depreciate.

(b) Forward exchange rate — arbitrage

Step 1 — Strategy A: Enter forward, pay \(F_t^T\) at \(T\), receive 1 unit foreign.

Step 2 — Strategy B: Borrow \(S_t e^{-r_f(T-t)}\) at rate \(r\). Buy \(e^{-r_f(T-t)}\) foreign units, invest at \(r_f\). At \(T\): 1 unit. Repay \(S_t e^{(r-r_f)(T-t)}\).

Step 3 — No-arbitrage: \[\boxed{F_t^T = S_t\,e^{(r-r_f)(T-t)}}\]

(c) Volatility of forward price \(F_t^T\) as \(t \to T\)

Step 1 — Express \(F_t^T\) as function of \(S_t\): \[F_t^T = S_t\,e^{(r-r_f)(T-t)}\] The factor \(e^{(r-r_f)(T-t)}\) is deterministic (depends only on time, not on \(S\)).

Step 2 — Apply Itô's lemma: since \(F = S\cdot g(t)\) with \(g(t)=e^{(r-r_f)(T-t)}\) deterministic: \[dF_t^T = g(t)\,dS_t + S_t\,dg\] \[= g(t)\bigl(\mu_S S_t\,dt + \sigma_S S_t\,dz\bigr) + S_t g(t)\bigl(-(r-r_f)\bigr)dt\] \[= \bigl(\mu_S - (r-r_f)\bigr)F_t^T\,dt + \sigma_S F_t^T\,dz\]

Step 3 — Volatility coefficient: \[\boxed{\text{Volatility of }F_t^T = \sigma_S F_t^T}\] Same percentage volatility as spot \(S_t\). As \(t\to T\): \(F_t^T \to S_T\) and the forward volatility converges to the spot volatility \(\sigma_S\).

Question 2

Let the probability of a borrower not defaulting at or before time \(t\) be given by \(V(t)\), \(V(0) = 1\).
(a) What is the probability that the borrower will default between time \(t\) and \(t + \Delta t\) conditional on not being in default at time \(t\)? Use this to define the continuously compounded default hazard rate \(\lambda(t)\).
(b) Define a Credit Default Swap (CDS) and describe the payment structure.
(c) How, and under which assumptions, may we estimate the hazard rate from a CDS spread \(s\) on the relevant borrower? Under what probability measure would we say this estimate is derived? Compare this to a hazard rate that is derived from default frequencies and recovery rates published by a rating agency.
(d) Which type of derived hazard rate should be applied for pricing financial derivatives, and what kind of discounting is then consistent with this approach?

► Show official solution
OFFICIAL MARKING GUIDE

(a) Refer to Hull section 24.2. The conditional default probability from \(t\) to \(t + \Delta t\) is \(\frac{V(t) - V(t+\Delta t)}{V(t)}\). Given \(V(t)\) the hazard rate is the rate of decay of survivors. Assuming differentiability you may define it as \[\lambda(t) = -\frac{\partial V(t)/\partial t}{V(t)}\] This can be motivated by considering the above discrete time step and letting the time step approach zero.
(b) Refer to Hull section 25.1. Definition (Credit Default Swap). A Credit Default Swap (CDS) is a contract between a protection seller and a protection buyer based on a specified Nominal Principal. The protection buyer pays a running premium (the CDS spread) until maturity of the contract or a Credit Event on the Reference Entity of the CDS. At a Credit Event the protection seller pays a Specified Amount (Fixed or (1-Recovery) times the Nominal Principal) to the protection buyer.
(c) Refer to Hull section 24.4–5. Under a risk neutral probability measure the hazard rate (also called default intensity) may be derived directly from CDS-spreads if you make an appropriate assumption on the recovery rate \(R\). Assume a given recovery rate of \(R\) (this may be fixed in the contract), and that the CDS-spread is \(s\) for a given maturity. Then the implied average hazard rate for this maturity is \(\bar{\lambda} = \frac{s}{1-R}\). You may similarly bootstrap a hazard rate structure from a term structure of CDS spreads. This analysis is conducted under a risk neutral (“Q”) measure, so that \((\lambda, R)\) will not be the same as frequencies and averages published by rating agencies, i.e. under real world probabilities (“P”).
(d) The Q-approach should be applied for pricing when using the short term risk free rate for discounting, being consistent with the numeraire that is the cumulative return from the risk free rate.

► Show step-by-step solution
STEP-BY-STEP
📖 Study Guide: Credit Risk   |   Flashcards: review these topics in the Flashcards tab

(a) Default hazard rate

Conditional default probability between \(t\) and \(t+\Delta t\), given survival to \(t\): \[\frac{V(t)-V(t+\Delta t)}{V(t)}\] Let \(\Delta t\to 0\): \[\boxed{\lambda(t) = -\frac{V'(t)}{V(t)} = -\frac{d\ln V}{dt}}\] Integrating: \(V(t) = e^{-\int_0^t \lambda(s)\,ds}\). If \(\lambda\) constant: \(V(t) = e^{-\lambda t}\).

(b) Credit Default Swap

CDS: contract between protection buyer and seller on reference entity, nominal \(N\).
Buyer pays CDS spread \(s \times N\) periodically until maturity or credit event.
At credit event: seller pays \((1-R)\times N\) to buyer (\(R\) = recovery rate).
CDS isolates and transfers credit risk.

(c) Estimating hazard rate from CDS spread

Step 1 — No-arbitrage: PV(protection leg) = PV(premium leg) Under risk-neutral measure with constant hazard rate \(\lambda\) and recovery \(R\):
PV(protection) = \((1-R)\int_0^T e^{-rt}\lambda e^{-\lambda t}\,dt\)
PV(premium) = \(s\int_0^T e^{-rt}e^{-\lambda t}\,dt\)

Step 2 — Solve Setting equal and simplifying: \[\boxed{s \approx \lambda(1-R)}\] So \(\lambda \approx s/(1-R)\). The hazard rate is approximately the credit spread divided by loss-given-default.

(d) Why use Q-measure (risk-neutral) for pricing

When pricing under the risk-neutral measure \(Q\), we discount at the risk-free rate \(r\) — the numeraire is the cumulative risk-free bank account \(e^{rt}\).
Under \(Q\), all traded assets earn the risk-free rate in expectation, so pricing reduces to: \[\text{Price} = e^{-rT}E^Q[\text{payoff}]\] This is consistent: the numeraire and the discount rate match, avoiding arbitrage.

Question 3

Consider an interest rate floor with a life of \(T\), a principal of \(N\), and a floor rate of \(L_K\). Consider reset dates \(0 = t_0 < t_1 < t_2 < \cdots < t_n\), and let \(L_k\) be the Libor rate for the period from \(t_k\) to \(t_{k+1}\) known at \(t_k\).
(a) Describe the payments of the floor, and define a floorlet.
(b) Show that the floor can be considered as a portfolio of European call options on zero-coupon bonds (for notational ease you may assume \(N = 1\)). (Hint: Start by analyzing a single floorlet.)
(c) A standard market practice is to price a floorlet (at \(t = 0\)) with the Black formula: \[\text{FloorletBlack}(0,k,N,L_K,\sigma_k) = NP(0,t_{k+1})\tau_k\bigl(L_K\Phi(-d_2) - F_k\Phi(-d_1)\bigr)\] with \(d_1 = \frac{\ln(F_k/L_K) + 0.5\sigma_k^2 t_k}{\sigma_k\sqrt{t_k}}\), \(d_2 = d_1 - \sigma_k\sqrt{t_k}\), where \(P(t,T)\) is the price at \(t\) of a zero coupon bond maturing at \(T\), \(F_k\) is the forward rate at 0 for the time interval \((t_k, t_{k+1})\) with length \(\tau_k\), and \(\sigma_k\) the volatility of this rate. The function \(\Phi\) is the standard Normal distribution function. What assumptions can justify this formula?

► Show official solution
OFFICIAL MARKING GUIDE

(a) Refer to Hull section 29.2. Definition (Floors). Let the variable (Libor-) rate set at \(t_k\) for the period \((t_k, t_{k+1})\) be \(L_k\). Consider a maturity \(T\), a principal of \(N\) and a Floor Rate of \(L_K\). Let reset dates \(t_1, t_2, \ldots, t_n\) be given and \(t_{n+1} = T\). A Floor is a contract that gives its holder the payments \(N\tau_k \max(L_K - L_k;\, 0)\) at time \(t_{k+1}\) (i.e. in arrears), where \(\tau_k\) is the time length between \(t_k\) and \(t_{k+1}\). The potential payment at each payoff date defines the payment of a single floorlet starting on the previous reset date. Definition (Floorlet). A floorlet is a contract that gives its holder the payments \(\tau_k \max(L_K - L_k;\, 0)\) at time \(t_{k+1}\), where \(\tau_k\) is the time fraction between \(t_k\) and \(t_{k+1}\).
(b) A floor can be seen as a portfolio of calls on properly defined Zero Coupon Bonds (ZCBs). The present value of \(N\) floorlets covering the period from \(t_k\) to \(t_{k+1}\) and with payment at \(t_{k+1}\) is at time \(t_k\) (discounting with the variable rate \(L_k\), simple compounding): \[\frac{N\tau_k}{1 + L_k\tau_k}\max(L_K - L_k;\, 0) = \frac{1 + L_K\tau_k}{1 + L_k\tau_k}\max(N - N;\, 0)\] In the last expression the first part in the first argument under the max-operator is the present value at \(t_k\) of a ZCB with payoff \(N(1 + L_K\tau_k)\) at \(t_{k+1}\); thus the payoff is a call on this particular ZCB with strike \(N\). As the floor can be seen (and valued) as a portfolio of floorlets, it can be valued as a portfolio of such calls.
(c) The application of the Black model is consistent with a world that is forward risk neutral wrt a ZCB maturing at \(t_{k+1}\). Under this probability measure assume \(L_k\) has a lognormal distribution with a standard deviation of \(\ln(L_k)\) of \(\sigma_k\sqrt{t_k}\). We also have under this measure that the expectation of \(L_k\) is the forward rate \(F_k\).

► Show step-by-step solution
STEP-BY-STEP
📖 Study Guide: Interest Rate Derivatives   |   Flashcards: review these topics in the Flashcards tab

(a) Interest rate floor — payments and floorlets

A floor pays at each date \(t_{k+1}\): \(N\tau_k\max(L_K - L_k, 0)\)
where \(L_k\) is the LIBOR rate set at \(t_k\) for period \((t_k,t_{k+1})\), \(\tau_k = t_{k+1}-t_k\).
Each individual payment = one floorlet: pays if LIBOR falls below floor rate \(L_K\).

(b) Floor as portfolio of call options on zero-coupon bonds

Step 1 — Express \(L_k\) in terms of bond price: \[1 + \tau_k L_k = \frac{1}{P(t_k,t_{k+1})} \implies L_k = \frac{1}{\tau_k}\!\left(\frac{1}{P} - 1\right)\]

Step 2 — Rewrite floorlet payoff: \[\tau_k(L_K - L_k) = \tau_k L_K - \frac{1}{P} + 1 = (1 + \tau_k L_K) - \frac{1}{P}\] \[= \frac{1}{P}\bigl(P(1+\tau_k L_K) - 1\bigr)\] So: \(N\tau_k\max(L_K-L_k,0) = \dfrac{N}{P}\max\!\bigl(P(1+\tau_kL_K)-1,\,0\bigr)\)

Step 3 — Define \(K_B = 1/(1+\tau_k L_K)\). Check the factoring: \((1+\tau_kL_K)(P-K_B) = P(1+\tau_kL_K) - (1+\tau_kL_K)K_B = P(1+\tau_kL_K) - 1\) since \((1+\tau_kL_K)K_B = 1\) by definition of \(K_B\). So \(P(1+\tau_kL_K)-1 = (1+\tau_kL_K)(P - K_B)\): \[\frac{N}{P}\max\!\bigl((1+\tau_kL_K)(P-K_B),\,0\bigr) = \frac{N(1+\tau_kL_K)}{P}\max(P-K_B,0)\]

Step 4 — Convert: payoff at \(t_{k+1}\) to value at \(t_k\): The floorlet pays at \(t_{k+1}\). Multiplying by \(P(t_k,t_{k+1})\) converts to a \(t_k\)-value:
Payoff at \(t_{k+1}\) = \(N(1+\tau_kL_K)(P/P)\max(P-K_B,0)\) → at \(t_k\): \[\boxed{\text{Floorlet value at }t_k = N(1+\tau_k L_K)\cdot\max\bigl(P(t_k,t_{k+1}) - K_B,\,0\bigr)}\] This is \(N(1+\tau_kL_K)\) European call options on bond \(P(t_k,t_{k+1})\) with strike \(K_B = \dfrac{1}{1+\tau_kL_K}\). \[\boxed{\text{Floor} = \sum_k N(1+\tau_k L_K)\cdot \text{call on }P(t_k,t_{k+1})\text{ with strike }K_B}\]

(c) Floorlet pricing using Black's model

Under the \(t_{k+1}\)-forward measure, \(L_k\) is lognormal with forward rate \(F_k = E^{Q^{k+1}}[L_k]\).
Step 1 — Black's formula for floorlet (put on rate): \[\text{Floorlet}_k = N\tau_k P(0,t_{k+1})\bigl[L_K\Phi(-d_2) - F_k\Phi(-d_1)\bigr]\]

Step 2 — Where: \[d_1 = \frac{\ln(F_k/L_K) + \tfrac{1}{2}\sigma_k^2 t_k}{\sigma_k\sqrt{t_k}},\quad d_2 = d_1 - \sigma_k\sqrt{t_k}\]

Step 3 — Floor = sum of floorlets: \[\boxed{\text{Floor} = \sum_{k=0}^{n-1} N\tau_k P(0,t_{k+1})\bigl[L_K\Phi(-d_2^k) - F_k\Phi(-d_1^k)\bigr]}\]

August 2022 — Re-Exam

Pricing of Financial Assets · University of Copenhagen · 3 questions · 3 hours · No aids permitted

Question 1

Assume that the price of a non-dividend paying stock \(S_t\) can be modelled (under the original probability measure \(P\)) by the geometric Brownian motion \[dS_t = \mu S_t\,dt + \sigma S_t\,dz\] where \(\mu\) and \(\sigma > 0\) are constants, and where \(dt\) and \(dz\) are the standard short hand notations for a small time-step and a Brownian increment.
(a) Consider the transformation \(G\) of \(S_t\) by the natural logarithm (ln), i.e. \(G(x) = \ln(x)\). Use Itô's lemma to find the process followed by \(G(S_t)\).
(b) Consider a forward contract on the stock maturing at time \(T\). Assume a constant continuously compounded interest rate of \(r\). What is the arbitrage free relation between stock price \(S_t\) and forward price \(F_t^T\)?
(c) First, compare the pricing of a call option on the stock with a pricing of a call on the forward contract on the same stock with both forward contracts maturing at \(T\) and having the same strike \(K\).
(d) Now assume that the stock pays a continuous dividend rate of \(\delta\), i.e. at each time \(t\) proportional to the price of the stock. Does this change the result?

► Show official solution
OFFICIAL MARKING GUIDE

(a) Refer to Hull section 14.7. The use of Itô's lemma is straightforward, using that \(\frac{\partial G}{\partial x} = 1/x\), \(\frac{\partial^2 G}{\partial x^2} = -1/x^2\), \(\frac{\partial G}{\partial t} = 0\). You then get: \[dG = \bigl(\mu - \tfrac{1}{2}\sigma^2\bigr)dt + \sigma\,dz\]
(b) Refer to Hull section 14.6 and 18.3. First the answer should note the definition of a forward contract, in particular that it has no payments before maturity. Then the answer should cover the forward price that will be agreed barring arbitrage: With an assumption of a constant risk free interest rate this is for the forward price \(F_t^T\) contracted at time \(t \leq T\), where \(T\) is the maturity of the contract, \[F_t^T = E^Q[S_T] = S_t e^{r(T-t)}\] where we only for the last equation use the assumption of no dividends. A constructive argument with the same result compares two strategies to achieve ownership of one stock at time \(T\); namely (1) Buy the stock at the price \(S_t\) at time \(t\) and hold it to time \(T\). (2) Enter into the forward contract at time \(t\), and at the same time place the amount \(e^{-r(T-t)}F_t^T\) at interest. At time \(T\) the amount has grown with interest to \(F_t^T\) that can be paid in the forward contract to gain one stock. Since these strategies yield the same result at time \(T\), the outlays at time \(t\) must be the same if there is to be no arbitrage. Last the answer may note that \(F\) follows a Geometric Brownian Motion similar to that derived earlier for \(S\), but that the drift rate of returns is \(\mu - r\) rather than the \(\mu\) for the stock. Under the Q-measure the drift rates will be 0 and \(r\), respectively.
(c) Thus comparing two call options with the same strike, but where the first is on the forward contract and the second is on the stock, \[c^F = e^{-r(T-t)}E^Q\bigl[\max(F_T^T - K;\, 0)\bigr] = e^{-r(T-t)}E^Q\bigl[\max(S_T - K;\, 0)\bigr] = c^S\] since they will have the same payout at maturity, even if the forward price initially (with a positive interest rate) is higher than the spot price. This may be verified by looking at the formulae for call prices on stocks and forwards (if you e.g. substitute the relation between forward and spot prices into the Black formula you get the Black-Scholes formula for a call).
(d) This then also holds for the case of dividends (as earlier dividends are irrelevant for the payments at \(T\)).

► Show step-by-step solution
STEP-BY-STEP
📖 Study Guide: GBM  ·  Forward Pricing   |   Flashcards: review these topics in the Flashcards tab

(a) Itô's lemma on \(G(S_t) = \ln S_t\)

Step 1 — Partial derivatives \[\frac{\partial G}{\partial S} = \frac{1}{S},\quad \frac{\partial^2 G}{\partial S^2} = -\frac{1}{S^2},\quad \frac{\partial G}{\partial t} = 0\]

Step 2 — Apply Itô with \((dS_t)^2 = \sigma^2 S_t^2\,dt\) \[d\ln S = \frac{dS}{S} + \tfrac{1}{2}\left(-\frac{1}{S^2}\right)\sigma^2 S^2\,dt\] Substitute \(dS = \mu S\,dt + \sigma S\,dz\): \[d\ln S = \frac{1}{S}\bigl(\mu S\,dt + \sigma S\,dz\bigr) - \tfrac{1}{2}\sigma^2\,dt = (\mu - \tfrac{1}{2}\sigma^2)dt + \sigma\,dz\] \[\boxed{d\ln S_t = \bigl(\mu - \tfrac{1}{2}\sigma^2\bigr)dt + \sigma\,dz}\]

(b) Forward price \(F_t^T = E^Q[S_T]\)

Step 1 — Under Q: drift of S = r \[d\ln S = (r - \tfrac{1}{2}\sigma^2)dt + \sigma\,dz^Q\] \[\ln S_T \sim N(\ln S_t + (r-\tfrac{1}{2}\sigma^2)(T-t),\; \sigma^2(T-t))\]

Step 2 — Expected value of lognormal \[E^Q[S_T] = S_t e^{r(T-t)}\] \[\boxed{F_t^T = S_t e^{r(T-t)}}\]

(c) Call on futures vs call on stock — same K, T

At maturity \(T\): futures price \(F_T^T = S_T\). So payoff of call on futures = \(\max(S_T-K,0)\) = payoff of call on stock.
Since both are European with same maturity: \[\boxed{c^{\text{futures}} = c^{\text{stock}} = e^{-rT}E^Q[\max(S_T-K,0)]}\] Equal prices despite futures price initially exceeding spot (when \(r>0\)).

(d) With dividends

Same result holds: at maturity \(T\), \(F_T^T = S_T\) regardless of dividends paid before \(T\). So call on futures = call on stock for any dividend policy.

Question 2

(a) In the one-factor Gaussian model of defaults the default of an obligor \(i\) is indicated by \[x_i = a_i F + \sqrt{1 - a_i^2}\,Z_i\] where \(F\) and \(Z_i\), \(i = 1,\ldots,n\), have independent standard normal distributions. Give an interpretation of the model and its parameters. What is the (copula) default correlation between \(x_i\) and \(x_j\) for some \(j \neq i\)?
(b) In this model the indicator \(x_i\) is compared to the probability \(Q_i(T)\) that the obligor has defaulted before or at time \(T\). Suppose that the obligor defaults at time \(t\). Which value of the indicator \(x_i\) does this correspond to?
(c) Explain for a tranched structured product such as a Credit Debt Obligation what the terms attachment point and detachment point refer to.
(d) Is the value of the most senior tranche in general positively or negatively influenced by an increase in the default correlation of obligors?

► Show official solution
OFFICIAL MARKING GUIDE

(a) Refer to Hull sections 24.8 and 25.10. Note that \(F\) is a common factor determining the covariance structure, whereas \(Z_i\) are idiosyncratic factors, and \(a_i\) are sensitivities to the common factor. Note that \(\text{Var}[x_i] = a_i^2 + (1-a_i^2) = 1\) and \(\text{Cov}[x_i,x_j] = a_i a_j\) using that \(F\) and \(Z_i\) are standard normal and independent. In implementation the \(a_i\) are often approximated as the correlation between \(i\)'s equity return and some broad market index (i.e. P-type correlations). They could also be inferred from e.g. prices of tranched CDOs (i.e. Q-type correlations)).
(b) The indicator can be interpreted as corresponding to a value of \(\Phi^{-1}(Q_i(t))\) (where \(\Phi\) is the standard normal distribution function), i.e. we investigate defaults on the inverse normal evaluated on the relevant percentile of the given cumulated probability of default such that an observation of a larger \(x_i\) than this critical value means a default before or at \(t\).
(c) The payoff of the debt instruments underlying a cash CDO (or credit derivatives underlying a synthetic CDO) is distributed to the different tranches of the CDO so that the most senior tranche has priority to the payoffs (in a waterfall structure). Losses thus first hit the most junior tranche, called the equity tranche. There are further arrangements to protect the senior tranches, but the above will be a satisfactory answer.
(d) The most senior tranche will — expected loss and recovery being equal — in general benefit from diversification (and thus from low correlations) since then the stochastic losses will be closer to the expected losses and less likely to hit the most senior tranche.

► Show step-by-step solution
STEP-BY-STEP
📖 Study Guide: Credit Risk   |   Flashcards: review these topics in the Flashcards tab

(a) Gaussian copula model — interpretation and copula correlation

Step 1 — Model: \(x_i = a_i F + \sqrt{1-a_i^2}Z_i\), where \(F,Z_i\sim N(0,1)\) independent.

Step 2 — Interpretation
\(F\) = systematic factor (macro/market). \(Z_i\) = idiosyncratic (firm-specific). \(a_i\) = factor loading.

Step 3 — Compute variance \[\text{Var}(x_i) = a_i^2\text{Var}(F) + (1-a_i^2)\text{Var}(Z_i) = a_i^2 + (1-a_i^2) = 1\]

Step 4 — Compute covariance (\(F,Z_i,Z_j\) all independent): \[\text{Cov}(x_i,x_j) = a_ia_j\cdot\text{Var}(F) = a_ia_j\] \[\boxed{\rho_{ij} = \frac{\text{Cov}(x_i,x_j)}{\sqrt{\text{Var}(x_i)\text{Var}(x_j)}} = a_ia_j}\]

(b) Default threshold and conditional probability

Step 1 — Default rule: default before \(T\) iff \(\Phi(x_i)\leq Q_i(T)\), i.e. \(x_i\leq\Phi^{-1}(Q_i(T))=:C_i\).

Step 2 — Conditional on \(F\): isolate \(Z_i\): \[\sqrt{1-a_i^2}Z_i \leq C_i - a_iF \implies Z_i \leq \frac{C_i-a_iF}{\sqrt{1-a_i^2}}\]

Step 3 — Apply \(\Phi\): \[\boxed{P(\text{default}_i\mid F) = \Phi\!\left(\frac{C_i-a_iF}{\sqrt{1-a_i^2}}\right)}\]

(c)+(d) Tranched structure and correlation effects

Step 1: Losses flow bottom-up. Attachment/detachment points define each tranche's exposure.

Step 2 — Low \(\rho\): diversified losses → equity absorbs, senior safe → senior spread low.

Step 3 — High \(\rho\): lumpy losses → senior exposed → senior spread rises.

Question 3

(a) Define interest rate receiver and payer swaptions (also called swap options) and explain their payoffs.
(b) Give an argument that a receiver swaption can be seen as a call option on a properly defined fixed rate bond. How will the coupon and principal of such a bond then be characterised?
(c) Let \(PS_K\) denote the value at time \(t\) of a swaption to pay a fixed rate \(s_K\) and receive a variable (Libor-) rate between times \(T_1\) and \(T_2\). Let \(RS_K\) denote the value at time \(t\) of a swaption to receive a fixed rate of \(s_K\) and pay variable (Libor-) rate between times \(T_1\) and \(T_2\). Finally, let \(RFS_K\) denote the value at time \(t\) of a forward starting swap that receives a fixed rate of \(s_K\) and pays the variable (Libor-) rate between times \(T_1\) and \(T_2\), \(t < T_1 < T_2\). Assume that there are no arbitrage opportunities and (i) show that \(PS_K + RFS_K = RS_K\) and (ii) deduce that \(PS_K = RS_K\) when \(s_K\) equals the forward swap rate at time \(t\) for a swap running from \(T_1\) to \(T_2\).

► Show official solution
OFFICIAL MARKING GUIDE

(a) Refer to Hull section 29.3. Definition (Swaption). A payer (receiver) swaption or swap option gives the holder the right to enter into a payer (receiver) Interest Rate Swap with a specified swap rate \(s_K\) at some future date \(T_1\) with the swap running from \(T_1\) to \(T_2\), \(T_2 > T_1\). Some swaptions pay the value of the underlying swap at exercise. The value of the underlying swap will be the value of the cashflow that is the difference of the fixed rate leg calculated with the strike rate \(s_K\) and calculated with the market swap rate at the maturity of the swaption \(s_{T_1}\), i.e. an annuity. Note that the decision of whether to exercise the swaption will depend on the level of the market swap rate compared with the strike rate.
(b) Consider a bond with a fixed rate coupon equal to the swap rate, the principal is the par value of the bond (barring credit risks, liquidity considerations etc.), where the bond begins paying its fixed coupons in arrears from the maturity of the swaption until the maturity of the underlying swap with coupon dates equal to the payment dates on the swap. The receiver swaption can be seen as an option to receive this bond by delivery of the principal (which we, somewhat idealized, assume will be equal to the continuing value of the variable leg of the swap). We can note that both swaption and bond option will increase in value with lower longer rates.
(c) Consider the payments from \(T_1\) to \(T_2\) depending on the realized level of the swap rate \(s_{T_1}\) at \(T_1\) compared with the strike rate \(s_K\), and let Libor be the running variable rate:
Case \(s_K > s_{T_1}\): \(PS_K\) pays \(s_K\) and receives Libor; \(RFS_K\) receives \(s_K\) and pays Libor; \(RS_K\) has payment 0.
Case \(s_K \leq s_{T_1}\): \(PS_K\) has payment 0; \(RFS_K\) receives \(s_K\) and pays Libor; \(RS_K\) receives \(s_K\) and pays Libor.
In the first case the receiver swaption is not exercised since you would then receive a rate lower than current market swap rate. In the second case the payer swaption is not exercised since you would then pay a rate higher than current market swap rate. From the table it is seen that the payments for the payer swaption and the forward starting swap equals the payments on the receiver swaption. Thus barring arbitrage the equation must hold. If \(s_K\) is the current forward swap rate the value \(RFS_K\) is by definition zero. Thus \(PS_K = RS_K\).

► Show step-by-step solution
STEP-BY-STEP
📖 Study Guide: Interest Rate Derivatives   |   Flashcards: review these topics in the Flashcards tab

(a) Payer and receiver swaptions — definitions

A payer swaption: right to enter an interest rate swap paying fixed rate \(s_K\) and receiving LIBOR, starting at \(T_1\) and ending at \(T_2\). A receiver swaption: right to enter a swap receiving fixed \(s_K\) and paying LIBOR. Exercised when the market swap rate at \(T_1\) is below (receiver) or above (payer) \(s_K\).

(b) Receiver swaption as call on a bond

Receiving a fixed coupon stream from \(T_1\) to \(T_2\) at rate \(s_K\) = owning a fixed-rate coupon bond with coupon \(s_K\). The swaption allows you to "buy" this bond at par (paying floating = par). Therefore: receiver swaption = call option on the coupon bond with strike = par. Coupon = swap rate \(s_K\). Principal = notional (par value).

(c) Swaption put-call parity

(i) Show \(PS_K + RFS_K = RS_K\)

Step 1 — Long receiver swaption + short payer swaption = at \(T_1\): regardless of market rate \(s_{T_1}\), one swaption is exercised and you end up in a receive-fixed-\(s_K\) swap from \(T_1\) to \(T_2\).

Step 2 — A forward-starting receive-fixed swap \(RFS_K\) gives the same payoff with certainty (no optionality). By no-arbitrage: \[RS_K - PS_K = RFS_K\] \[\boxed{PS_K + RFS_K = RS_K}\] where \(RFS_K = \displaystyle\sum_k P(t,t_{k+1})\tau_k(s_K - f_k)\) and \(f_k\) are the current forward rates.

(ii) Deduce \(PS_K = RS_K\) when \(s_K\) = forward swap rate

Step 1 — The forward swap rate \(s^*\) is defined as the fixed rate that makes the receive-fixed swap have zero value: \[RFS_{s^*} = \sum_k P(t,t_{k+1})\tau_k(s^* - f_k) = 0\]

Step 2 — Set \(s_K = s^*\): \[PS_{s^*} + \underbrace{RFS_{s^*}}_{=\,0} = RS_{s^*}\] \[\boxed{PS_{s^*} = RS_{s^*}}\] When the swaption strike equals the forward swap rate, payer and receiver swaptions have equal value — exactly analogous to put-call parity where call = put when option is struck at the forward price.

June 2021 — Ordinary Exam

Pricing of Financial Assets · University of Copenhagen · 3 questions · 3 hours · No aids permitted

Question 1

Consider a stock with price \(S_t\) at time \(t\) and a zero coupon bond maturing at time \(T > t\) with price \(P(t,T)\) at time \(t\).
(a) Assume that the stock pays no dividend. What is the forward price \(F(t,T)\) at \(t\) for the stock with delivery at \(T\)?
(b) Now assume that the stock pays a continuous dividend rate of \(\delta\). What is the forward price in this case?
(c) Let \(c(t,K,T)\) and \(p(t,K,T)\) denote the prices respectively of a call and a put option on the stock, both with exercise price \(K\) and maturity \(T\). What is the put-call-parity for the case with the dividend paying stock?
(d) If the prices of the call, put and stock as defined above are known at \(t\), derive the implied dividend rate.

► Show official solution
OFFICIAL MARKING GUIDE

(a) Consider the following two strategies to get 1 stock at time \(T\): (a) Enter into a forward contract with forward price \(F(t,T)\) to get the stock at \(T\) and at the same time buy \(F(t,T)\) zero coupon bonds. The total payment at \(t\) is \(P(t,T)F(t,T)\). At time \(T\) the zero coupon bond portfolio matures and gives the \(F(t,T)\) to be paid to receive the stock. (b) Buy the stock at time \(t\) and keep it to time \(T\). If there is to be no arbitrage opportunities the two different outlays at time \(t\) must be identical, i.e. \[S(t) = P(t,T)F(t,T)\] \[F(t,T) = S(t)/P(t,T)\]
(b) If there is a continuous dividend rate of \(\delta\) on the stock, in strategy (b) buy only \(e^{-\delta(T-t)}\) of the stock, but reinvest dividends continuously to get 1 stock at time \(T\). Thus \[e^{-\delta(T-t)}S(t) = P(t,T)F(t,T)\] or \[F(t,T) = e^{-\delta(T-t)}S(t)/P(t,T)\]
(c) A long call and a short put with same strike \(K\) and maturity \(T\) is equivalent to a long forward with delivery price \(K\) and maturity \(T\). The value of this forward contract is \(P(t,T)(F(t,T)-K)\). So (abbreviating the notation a little) \[P(t,T)(F(t,T)-K) = c_t - p_t\] or \[e^{-\delta(T-t)}S(t) - P(t,T)K = c_t - p_t\]
(d) Solve \[e^{-\delta(T-t)}S(t) = c_t - p_t + P(t,T)K\] or \[\delta = -\frac{1}{T-t}\ln\frac{c_t - p_t + P(t,T)K}{S(t)}\] (Hull 9th ed, section 17.3–17.4).

► Show step-by-step solution
STEP-BY-STEP
📖 Study Guide: Forward Pricing  ·  Put-Call Parity   |   Flashcards: review these topics in the Flashcards tab

(a) Forward price — no dividend

Step 1 — Two strategies to own 1 stock at T
A) Enter forward at \(F(t,T)\) + buy \(F(t,T)\) zero-coupon bonds. Cost at \(t\): \(P(t,T)F(t,T)\).
B) Buy stock now. Cost: \(S(t)\).

Step 2 — No-arbitrage \[S(t) = P(t,T)F(t,T) \implies \boxed{F(t,T) = \frac{S(t)}{P(t,T)} = S(t)e^{r(T-t)}}\]

(b) Forward price — continuous dividend \(\delta\)

Step 1 — Adjusted strategy B
Buy \(e^{-\delta(T-t)}\) shares now, reinvest dividends. At \(T\): 1 share. Cost: \(S(t)e^{-\delta(T-t)}\).

Step 2 — No-arbitrage \[P(t,T)F(t,T) = S(t)e^{-\delta(T-t)} \implies \boxed{F(t,T) = S(t)e^{(r-\delta)(T-t)}}\]

(c) Put-call parity

Step 1 — Payoff at T: long call, short put \[\max(S_T-K,0) - \max(K-S_T,0) = S_T - K\]

Step 2 — Value this at t=0 \[c - p = e^{-rT}E^Q[S_T - K] = S_0 e^{-\delta T} - Ke^{-rT}\] \[\boxed{c(t,K,T) - p(t,K,T) = S(t)e^{-\delta(T-t)} - Ke^{-r(T-t)}}\]

(d) Implied dividend from put-call parity

Step 1 — Put-call parity with dividend: \[c - p = S(t)e^{-\delta(T-t)} - Ke^{-r(T-t)}\]

Step 2 — Solve for \(\delta\): \[e^{-\delta(T-t)} = \frac{c-p+Ke^{-r(T-t)}}{S(t)}\] \[\boxed{\delta = -\frac{1}{T-t}\ln\frac{c-p+Ke^{-r(T-t)}}{S(t)}}\]

This allows you to back out the implied dividend yield from observed option prices.

Question 2

Consider a situation with credit risk, and let the probability of a borrower not defaulting at or before time \(t\) be given by \(V(t)\), \(V(0) = 1\).
(a) What is the probability that the borrower will default between time \(t\) and \(t+\Delta t\) conditional on not being in default at time \(t\)? Use this to define the continuously compounded default hazard rate \(\lambda\).
(b) How, and under which assumptions, may we estimate the hazard rate from the interest rate spreads on bonds issued by the borrower? Under what probability measure would we say this estimate is derived? Compare this to a hazard rate that is derived from default frequencies and recovery ratios published by a rating agency.
(c) In one model by Merton the value of a claim on a company with limited liability is modelled using a variation of the Black-Scholes-Merton option model. What is the option features embedded in such a claim? What parameters that are not directly observable must be determined to price the claim in this model? Comment on the model.

► Show official solution
OFFICIAL MARKING GUIDE

(a) The conditional default probability from \(t\) to \(t+\Delta t\) is \[\frac{V(t) - V(t+\Delta t)}{V(t)}\] Given \(V(t)\) the hazard rate is the rate of decay of survivors. Assuming differentiability you may define it as \[\lambda(t) = -\frac{\partial V(t)/\partial t}{V(t)}\] This can be motivated by considering the above discrete time step and letting the time step approach zero (Hull 9th ed, section 24.2–24.4).
(b) Under a risk neutral probability measure the hazard rate may be derived from prices of traded assets, e.g. bonds from the company in question. Assuming that the recovery rate \(\text{Rec}\) is known you may estimate the average hazard rate as the hazard rate that makes the discounted expected payoff (i.e. taking defaults into account) to be the price of a risk free bond. Assuming the risky bond pays a spread of \(s\) over the risk free bond for a given maturity \(T\) you can estimate the average hazard rate over the full period by \[\bar{\lambda}(T) = \frac{s(T)}{1-\text{Rec}}\] You may then use this to bootstrap a hazard rate structure from a term structure of credit spreads. This analysis should, however, be seen as conducted under a risk neutral (“Q”) measure, so that \((\lambda, \text{Rec})\) will not be the same as frequencies and averages published by rating agencies, real world probabilities (“P”) (Hull 9th ed., section 24.5).
(c) In the Merton-model the value of risky debt issued by a limited liability entity is modelled as the value of the assets of the entity less the value of the equity held by the owners of the company. Due to the limited liability the owners can be seen to hold a call option on the assets. Assuming there is only one form of debt, a zero coupon debt of face value \(D\) maturing at time \(T\), the Black-Scholes-Merton Model can be applied. The underlying asset for that model would be the value of the entity assets \(V_t\), which is typically unknown, as is its volatility, that is assumed constant \(\sigma_V\). Note first that the value of equity at time \(T\) is \(E_T = \max(V_T - D, 0)\) where you can use the BSM-call price formula if you know asset values and their volatility. Secondly, when both assets value and equity value are described by geometric Brownian motions you can use Itô's lemma to derive the relationship between the equity volatility \(\sigma_E\) and the asset volatility: \[\sigma_E E_t = \frac{\partial E}{\partial V}\sigma_V V_t\] Assuming that the value of the equity and the equity volatility is known from markets, you have two equations in two unknowns \((V_t, \sigma_V)\) that can be solved numerically for the value of the debt, using \(D_t = V_t - E_t\). A problem with Merton's model is that values are assumed to follow Itô-processes, i.e. with continuous sample paths. This makes the likelihood of a default a short time step ahead very small (no “jump-to-default”) and credit spreads to go to zero as the maturity of risky bonds goes to zero, which is not in line with empirical evidence (Hull 9th ed, section 24.6).

► Show step-by-step solution
STEP-BY-STEP
📖 Study Guide: Credit Risk   |   Flashcards: review these topics in the Flashcards tab

(a) Default hazard rate \(\lambda(t)\)

Step 1 — Conditional default probability in \([t, t+\Delta t]\) \[\frac{V(t)-V(t+\Delta t)}{V(t)}\]

Step 2 — Let \(\Delta t\to 0\) \[\lambda(t) = -\frac{V'(t)}{V(t)} = -\frac{d\ln V}{dt}\]

Step 3 — Integrate from 0 to \(t\) Separate variables and integrate both sides, using \(V(0)=1\) so \(\ln V(0) = 0\): \[\int_0^t -\lambda(s)\,ds = \int_0^t \frac{d\ln V(s)}{ds}\,ds = \ln V(t) - \ln V(0) = \ln V(t)\] Exponentiating both sides gives: \[\boxed{V(t) = e^{-\int_0^t\lambda(s)\,ds}}\] Constant \(\lambda\): \(V(t)=e^{-\lambda t}\).

(b) Hazard rate from bond spreads — Q-measure

Step 1: Risky bond yield = risk-free yield + spread \(s\). Under risk-neutral Q with recovery rate \(R\): \[s \approx \lambda(1-R) \implies \boxed{\lambda \approx \frac{s}{1-R}}\] This is under Q (risk-neutral), so \(\lambda\) is the risk-neutral hazard rate.

Step 2 — Q vs P: Rating agency hazard rates use real-world default frequencies (P-measure). Risk-neutral \(\lambda\) from bond spreads includes a risk premium, so it is typically higher than rating agency estimates. For pricing derivatives: use Q-measure, discount at risk-free rate.

(c) Merton structural model — option features and unobservable parameters

Step 1 — Option features embedded in claims
In Merton's model, firm assets \(V\) follow GBM. At debt maturity \(T\):
Equity (limited liability) = call on assets with strike \(N\): \(E_T = \max(V_T - N, 0)\)
Debt = assets minus equity: \(B_T = V_T - E_T = \min(V_T, N) = N - \max(N-V_T,0)\)
\(\Rightarrow\) Debt = risk-free bond minus put on assets with strike \(N\).

Step 2 — Unobservable parameters
BSM requires asset value \(V_0\) and asset volatility \(\sigma_V\). Neither is directly observable (only equity \(E_0\) and equity volatility \(\sigma_E\) are). Must be backed out from two equations: \[E_0 = V_0\Phi(d_1) - Ne^{-rT}\Phi(d_2),\quad \sigma_E E_0 = \sigma_V V_0\Phi(d_1)\]

Step 3 — Comment on the model
Useful structural insight: default probability linked to leverage and asset volatility. Main limitations: assumes assets follow GBM (no jumps), requires unobservable inputs, single class of debt, ignores strategic default behaviour. \[\boxed{B_0 = V_0\Phi(-d_1) + Ne^{-rT}\Phi(d_2),\quad d_1 = \frac{\ln(V_0/N)+(r+\tfrac{1}{2}\sigma_V^2)T}{\sigma_V\sqrt{T}}}\]

Question 3

(a) Give a definition of a payer and a receiver interest rate swaption (also called a swap option).
(b) Give an argument that a receiver swaption can be seen as a call option on a properly defined fixed rate bond with no credit risk. What can you say about the coupon and principal of such a bond?
(c) Let \(PS\) denote the value of a swaption to pay a fixed rate \(s_K\) and receive LIBOR between times \(T_1\) and \(T_2\), let \(RS\) denote the value of a swaption to receive a fixed rate of \(s_K\) and pay LIBOR between times \(T_1\) and \(T_2\), and let \(RFS\) denote the value of a forward starting swap that receives a fixed rate of \(s_K\) and pays LIBOR between times \(T_1\) and \(T_2\). Assume that there are no arbitrage opportunities and i) show that \(PS + RFS = RS\) and ii) deduce that \(PS = RS\) when \(s_K\) equals the current forward swap rate.

► Show official solution
OFFICIAL MARKING GUIDE

(a) Cf. Hull p.659f (8th ed) and p.684f (9th ed). Definition (Swaption). A payer (receiver) swaption or swap option gives the holder the right to enter into a payer (receiver) Interest Rate Swap in the future.
(b) Cf. Hull p. 660 (8th ed), p. 685 (9th ed). If the fixed rate coupon on the bond is equal to the swap rate the principal is the par value of the bond (barring credit risks, liquidity considerations etc.). The receiver swaption can be seen as an option to receive this bond by delivery of the principal (which we, somewhat idealized, assume will be equal to the continuing value of the variable leg of the swap). We can note that both swaption and bond option will increase in value with lower longer rates.
(c) Consider the payments from \(T_1\) to \(T_2\) in the following two cases that depend on the realized level of the swap rate \(s_{T_1}\) at \(T_1\) with maturity at \(T_2\):
Case \(s_K < s_{T_1}\): PS pays \(s_K\) and receives LIBOR; RFS receives \(s_K\) and pays LIBOR; RS has payment 0.
Case \(s_K \geq s_{T_1}\): PS has payment 0; RFS receives \(s_K\) and pays LIBOR; RS receives \(s_K\) and pays LIBOR.
From the table it is seen that the payments for the payer swaption and the forward starting swap equals the payments on the receiver swaption. Thus barring arbitrage the equation must hold. If \(s_K\) is the current forward swap rate the value \(RFS\) is by definition zero. (Hull 9th ed., practice question 29.18).

► Show step-by-step solution
STEP-BY-STEP
📖 Study Guide: Interest Rate Derivatives   |   Flashcards: review these topics in the Flashcards tab

(a) Payer and receiver swaption definitions

Payer swaption: right to pay fixed \(s_K\), receive LIBOR from \(T_1\) to \(T_2\). Valuable when market swap rate \(s_{T_1} > s_K\).
Receiver swaption: right to receive fixed \(s_K\), pay LIBOR. Valuable when \(s_{T_1} < s_K\).

(b) Receiver swaption as call on a bond

Consider a fixed-rate coupon bond with coupon = swap rate \(s_K\), principal = notional, maturity = \(T_2\). At \(T_1\): receiving fixed \(s_K\) and paying LIBOR = owning this bond and financing with floating rate debt (par). Exercise the receiver swaption iff bond value at \(T_1 > \text{par}\) ⟺ \(s_{T_1} < s_K\). Therefore: receiver swaption = call on bond with strike = par. Coupon = \(s_K\), principal = notional.

(c) Swaption put-call parity

(i) Show \(PS + RFS = RS\)

Step 1 — Long receiver (\(RS\)) + short payer (\(-PS\)): at \(T_1\) exactly one swaption is exercised. Net position: receive fixed \(s_K\), pay LIBOR from \(T_1\) to \(T_2\) with certainty.

Step 2 — This is identical to the forward-starting receive-fixed swap \(RFS\). By no-arbitrage: \[RS - PS = RFS\] \[\boxed{PS + RFS = RS}\] where \(RFS = \displaystyle\sum_k P(t,t_{k+1})\tau_k(s_K - f_k)\), with \(f_k\) the current forward rates.

(ii) Deduce \(PS = RS\) when \(s_K\) = forward swap rate

Step 1 — The forward swap rate \(s^*\) is the value of \(s_K\) that makes the forward-starting swap have zero value: \[RFS_{s^*} = \sum_k P(t,t_{k+1})\tau_k(s^* - f_k) = 0\]

Step 2 — Substitute \(s_K = s^*\) into the parity: \[PS + \underbrace{RFS_{s^*}}_{=\,0} = RS\] \[\boxed{PS = RS}\] When the strike equals the forward swap rate, payer and receiver swaptions are equally valued — the interest rate analogue of put-call parity at-the-forward.

August 2021 — Re-Exam

Pricing of Financial Assets · University of Copenhagen · 3 questions · 3 hours · No aids permitted

Question 1

(a) Define a forward contract on a stock.
(b) Consider a stock that does not pay dividends with price \(S_t\) at time \(t\). Assume a constant continuously compounded interest rate of \(r\). Using an arbitrage argument find the forward price \(F_t\) at time \(t\) on a forward contract on the stock that matures at time \(T > t\).
(c) Consider the value of a forward contract with forward price \(K\) at time \(t < T\) and assume that the stock price follows the geometric Brownian motion \[dS_t = \mu S_t\,dt + \sigma S_t\,dz\] Using Itô's lemma find the process followed by the value of the forward contract. What will the drift be under the risk neutral measure?
(d) Assume now that the stock pays a constant continuous dividend rate of \(\delta\). What will the forward price be?

► Show official solution
OFFICIAL MARKING GUIDE

(a) Definition (Forward Contract). A Forward Contract is an agreement to buy or sell a quantity of an asset of a specified quality and type (called the underlying asset) delivered at a specified future time \(T\) (the expiration date) and at a specified place for an agreed delivery price \(K\) as measured in some defined numeraire/currency. The price is initially (at \(t\)) set at a level \(K = F(t,T)\), such that the value \(V_K(t,T)\) of the contract is 0, i.e. by definition \(V_{F(t,T)}(t,T) = 0\). The price \(F(t,T)\) is called the forward price at \(t\) for delivery at \(T\).
(b) The following arbitrage argument can be made: Buy the stock spot financed by a loan of \(S_t\) repaid at \(T\). The only cashflow on this will be the repayment \(S_t e^{r(T-t)}\) at \(T\), leaving you with the stock. An alternative is to enter into a forward contract with delivery at \(T\). To eliminate arbitrage it must be that the forward price at \(t\) for delivery at \(T\) is \(F_t = S_t e^{r(T-t)}\).
(c) For a forward contract with forward price \(K\) the value is \(V_t = S_t - Ke^{-r(T-t)}\). Using Itô's lemma you get that the differential form for the value process for the forward: \[dV_t = \bigl[\mu S_t - rKe^{-r(T-t)}\bigr]dt + \sigma S_t\,dz\] For a currently priced forward (i.e. where \(K\) gives the forward contract a zero net present value \(K = F_t = e^{r(T-t)}S_t\)) we see that the drift is 0 under the risk neutral measure (by setting \(\mu = r\)), while a non-negative market value will grow at a rate \(r\). It will be considered ok just to derive this for the currently priced forward.
(d) In the arbitrage argument above you get too much if you buy the stock paying dividend, but you can repay and reduce the interest payments continuously leaving only \(S_t e^{(r-\delta)(T-t)}\) to repay at \(T\). Alternatively consider the price of a synthetic stock paying no dividends and compare that to \(S_t\) and go on from there. In either case \(F_t = S_t e^{(r-\delta)(T-t)}\).

► Show step-by-step solution
STEP-BY-STEP
📖 Study Guide: Forward Pricing   |   Flashcards: review these topics in the Flashcards tab

(a) Definition of a forward contract

A forward contract is an agreement to buy (long) or sell (short) a specified asset at a specified future date \(T\) for a delivery price \(K\) agreed at inception. The forward price \(F(t,T)\) is set so the contract has zero value at inception: \(V_{F(t,T)}(t,T) = 0\).

(b) Forward price by arbitrage — no dividends

Step 1 — Two strategies to own 1 stock at \(T\):
A) Enter forward at \(F_t\). Net cash at \(T\): \(-F_t\) (pay forward price, receive stock).
B) Buy stock now for \(S_t\), borrow \(S_t\) at rate \(r\). At \(T\): own stock, repay \(S_t e^{r(T-t)}\).

Step 2 — No-arbitrage: \[\boxed{F_t = S_t\,e^{r(T-t)}}\]

(c) Process for forward contract value \(V_K(t,T)\) via Itô's lemma

Step 1 — Value formula:
\[V_K(t,T) = e^{-r(T-t)}E^Q[S_T - K] = e^{-r(T-t)}(F_t - K) = S_t - Ke^{-r(T-t)}\]

Step 2 — Apply Itô's lemma to \(V = S_t - Ke^{-r(T-t)}\):
Partial derivatives: \(\partial V/\partial S = 1\), \(\partial^2 V/\partial S^2 = 0\), \(\partial V/\partial t = rKe^{-r(T-t)}\). \[dV = \frac{\partial V}{\partial t}dt + \frac{\partial V}{\partial S}dS + \tfrac{1}{2}\frac{\partial^2 V}{\partial S^2}(dS)^2\] \[= rKe^{-r(T-t)}\,dt + dS_t\] \[= rKe^{-r(T-t)}\,dt + \mu S_t\,dt + \sigma S_t\,dz\]

Step 3 — Under the risk-neutral measure Q (replace \(\mu \to r\)): \[dV = rKe^{-r(T-t)}\,dt + rS_t\,dt + \sigma S_t\,dz = r\underbrace{(S_t - Ke^{-r(T-t)})}_{=V}\,dt + \sigma S_t\,dz\] \[\boxed{dV_K = r V_K\,dt + \sigma S_t\,dz}\] Drift under Q = \(r V_K\) (forward value earns the risk-free rate). Diffusion = \(\sigma S_t\,dz\) (same absolute risk as the stock).

(d) Forward price — continuous dividend \(\delta\)

Step 1 — Buy \(e^{-\delta(T-t)}\) shares now, continuously reinvest dividends. At \(T\): 1 share. Cost now: \(S_t e^{-\delta(T-t)}\). Finance by borrowing, repay \(S_t e^{-\delta(T-t)}e^{r(T-t)}\) at \(T\).

Step 2 — No-arbitrage: \[\boxed{F_t = S_t\,e^{(r-\delta)(T-t)}}\]

Question 2

Assume that a stock with price \(S_t\) at time \(t\) pays no dividends before time \(T > t\), and that there is a constant continuously compounded risk free interest rate of \(r\). Let \(c(S_0,K,T,r)\) and \(p(S_0,K,T,r)\) be the price at time \(t=0\) of a European call and a European put, respectively, on the stock with the same strike \(K\) and expiry \(T\).
(a) Use the put-call-parity to find a relationship between the Thetas (\(\Theta\)) of the call and put. Comment on the result.
(b) Consider two call options with the same strike \(K\) but different expiry dates \(T_1\) and \(T_2\). Assume \(r \geq 0\) and use an arbitrage argument to show that the price of the longer call, \(c_2\), is (weakly) greater than the price of the shorter call, \(c_1\).

► Show official solution
OFFICIAL MARKING GUIDE

(a) The put-call parity for options with same maturity and same strike price on the same stock paying no dividend states that the price of long call less the short put must be equal to the forward price on the stock with the same delivery prices as the strike and the same maturity, i.e. \[c(S_0,K,T,r) - p(S_0,K,T,r) = S_0 - e^{-rT}K\] The Theta is the partial derivative with respect to calendar time, i.e. the negative partial derivative with respect to maturity \(T\). By taking the partial derivative we find: \[\Theta_c - \Theta_p = -re^{-rT}K\] This depends on the sign of \(r\). E.g. with a positive rate there is from discounting an implicit advantage for the call option of the later payment of the strike compared to the current date (and a similar disadvantage for the put of the strike received later). The negative sign on the right-hand side reflects that this relative advantage of the call between the two otherwise similar options becomes smaller as the maturity date gets closer. Hull (9ed, p.409) reports this for the special case of the BSM-model.
(b) One may first use the same argument as when showing that an early exercise of an American call under these assumptions is non-optimal.
